A corrosive is nothing but a corruption in a member that spreads, and it gnaws away the member itself: just as fire consumes dry wood.
If you see a corrosive in a place that can tolerate a cautery with fire, then prepare many cauteries, small and large, according to what is suitable for the place where the corrosive is.
Then cauterize it on every side until you eradicate the entire corruption, and none of it remains in any way.
Then diminish it for three days, and apply over the cauterized location burnt sulphur with oil, until all eschars are eradicated, and all corruption.
Then treat it with ointments that generate flesh.
If therefore you see that after three days healthy flesh is born without corruption, it is good.
And if not: then repeat the cauterization over that which remains from the corrupted places.
The corroding is treated whenever with sharp medicine. It itself arises in place of the cautery.
But indeed the cautery with fire is of swifter cure: and I have already spoken of its cauterization with sharp medicine in division: therefore take it there when you need it.
On the method of cauterizing in pimples and warts.
Chapter 55.
It often happens in the lower parts of the feet this disease: and it is a coarse, foul thing, which harms the foot.
And the cautery indeed for them is in two ways: the cautery with fire, and the cautery with hot water.
As for that with fire, it is like a hollow cautery similar to a feather of a vulture: which is made from iron, according to the quantity that the nail contains on all sides: and let it be subtle in its margins.
Then apply it ignited over the nail. Then turn the hand with the cautery around the nail, until the cautery penetrates to the depth of the nail: and diminish it for three days, until it begins to emit pus. Then plaster it with woodland marshes, pounded with salt: and diminish the plaster over it at night. For it will be eradicated from its own roots.
Then treat the place with a generating ointment of flesh, until it is healed.
What if they are not turned and often occur on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet: then you will administer to them a cautery with hot water: and it is, that you take a small tube made of bronze, or iron, or of a bird's quill: and put it on the nail, or the outer skin. Then throw into the tube a small quantity of hot water: hold your hand, and you turn it with compression of your hand for a short time, so that the margin of the tube is imprinted on the root of the nail, and the hot water finds its way to the depth, to the root of the nail: and let the weak one wait for a while on the biting of the hot water for one hour.
Then dispatch it, for the nail is eradicated with its roots.
Do this with them one after another, until you come to all of them that are on the body.
Then treat them after they have been eradicated, with that which generates flesh from ointments.
On the method of cauterizing for Tremors.
When Tremors occur to someone from cold in a nerve, or from quartan fever, or something else: then it is necessary that you cauterize him with four cauteries, or five on the vertebrae of the back, and between all the vertebrae a cautery, and on his chest a cautery, and above his stomach a cautery.
For Tremors are quieted with an oily cautery, and hasten the health of cold ague.
On the method of cauterizing for the Albahtir (possibly a disease or condition).
Chapter 57.
When foul Albahtir are expelled from the body, that is, pimples, which are made from corrupted thick materials.
It is necessary therefore in the beginning when they appear, that the pustules on the entire head be cauterized with a subtle cauterization of myrtle wood, whose extremities have been ignited with fire. Or with the root of long aristolochia. Or with a lentil-shaped cauterizer.
And when they are cauterized with a small point, at the beginning of its expulsion according to this method: and it does not increase: and the surplus is disrupted making them, and it heals from them.
Nevertheless it is necessary that it be done after evacuation with bloodletting.
On the method of cauterizing in a flow of blood from an artery in a wound.
It often happens that there is a flow of blood from an artery which has been cut near a wound, which happens externally, or at the time of an incision of an aposteme, or of a cauterization of a limb, and similar things to these: and it becomes difficult to stop it.
When therefore it happens to anyone, then quickly with your hand to the mouth of the artery, and put on it your index finger: and press it strongly, until the blood is blocked under your finger, and does not go out from it at all.
Then put on the fire small olive cauterizers, and a large number of them: and blow upon them, until they are strongly ignited.
Then take from them one, or a small one, or a large one, according to the wound and the place, in which the artery is disrupted, and put the cauterizer on the vein itself, after which you remove your finger quickly: and hold the cauterizer, until the blood is stopped.
Which if it is expelled when the finger is lifted from the mouth of the artery, and the cauterizer is extinguished, then take another cauterizer quickly, from the cauterizers which are prepared in the fire.
You must not stop doing that one thing after another until the blood is cut off.
And beware, lest you confuse the nerve that is there, and the injury falls upon a weaker part.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, then it is not possible to cut it off: especially when the artery is large, unless with one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its ends are contracted, and the blood is cut off. Or let it be tied with a strong thread of ligation. Or let medicines of the proper nature be applied to it, which are to cut off blood, and let it be tied with a dressing of suitable constriction.
He who administers the cutting off of it with a ligature, and a dressing with a bandage, or an application of burning things, and similar, does not benefit him thoroughly, except rarely.
If it happens to anyone, and there is not a doctor present to him, nor medicine: then let him begin with the application of the index finger on the opening of the vein itself, as we have narrated, and a strong constriction of it, until the blood is stopped from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery. And the finger is not removed from upon it, but rather with cold water of vehement coldness always, until the blood congeals, and coagulates, and is cut off.
And meanwhile let him consider what it needs from a cautery, or from medicine.
Of Incision, Perforation, and Phlebotomy in wounds, and similar things.
We have stated in the first chapter, all inflammation in which it is suitable to use with fire a cauterization and burning medicine, and its occasions, its causes, and its instruments, and forms of cauterizations.
And I have placed chapters from the head to the foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter with the same method: so that the searched-for thing is alleviated regarding the thing being searched for.
And before I begin that, it is necessary that you learn, O sons, that in this chapter is from the fear of what is in the first chapter concerning cauterization.
And because of that, it is necessary that caution be more vehement, since in the operation which is in this chapter, evacuation often occurs with blood, by which life stands at the aperture of a vein, or incision over an aposteme, or aperture of an exit, or cure of an ulcer, or extraction of an arrow, or incision over a stone, and similar things: from all of which are associated trembling, and fear, and death often occurs in many of them.
And I warn you, that there not befall you that in which there is hesitation, for sometimes men of a certain kind fall to men of a certain kind with modes of infirmities. For there is one who becomes angry with his own illness, and death is light to him: on account of the vehemence of what he finds from his infirmity and the length of his suffering. And the illness is from fear which signifies death.
And there is one who gives his physician, and says to you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore, it is not necessary that you agree to him who comes to you of this kind. And let your caution be more vehement than your greed and rapaciousness. And do not assume anything from it, unless after certain knowledge, which proves to you that which leads to praiseworthy success.
And that you use judgment in the cure of all your weaknesses, and the prediction and indication of that to which health is most conveniently suited.
For in it is your aid for acquiring fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you, O sons, with His intellect, and may He not withhold from you guidance and inspiration: for that is in His hand.
And I have indeed arranged this Chapter by distinguishing it into chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on Cautery, from the neck down to the feet: so that may be alleviated for you what you want from it, and what you need.
On the cure of water that gathers in the heads of children.
Chapter One.
Know that this weakness often happens to children at birth: or when the midwife pulls the head of the child with excessive force.
And it also happens sometimes from a hidden cause which is not known.
And I have indeed not seen this sickness in others than in children: and for every one of them whom I saw, death came quickly: therefore it seemed to me that it was wise to desist from the operation on it.
And I have indeed seen one of them, a child, whose head was filled with water, and the head was increasing in size every day, until he could not sit up by himself, because of the size of his head: and the moisture kept increasing, until he died.
And this moisture indeed either gathers between the skin and the bone, or gathers under the bone above the periosteum.
And the operation on it, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then you should make a single incision in the middle of the head, in width: and let the length of the incision be about two knots, until the moisture flows out.
And this is the form of a spatula.
If then the humidity is greatly increased and the aposteme is greater, then put two fissures in accordance with this form.
And if the humidity is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the skull open on all sides, and water is pressed out when you compress it with your hand to the interior, and it is not hidden from you: then it is necessary that you make three fissures in the middle of the skull, according to this method.
And after the incision, extract all the humidity. Then tighten the fissures with bandages and poultices. Then anoint it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then undo the ligature, and cure the wound with dressings and ointments.
And do not cease to bind the head with evenness. And feed the weak person all dry food of little humidity, until the member is comforted and healed.
And another method of incision is that you consider where the greatness of the aposteme appears, and the collection of water.
For sometimes it is more in the back of the head, or on one side, or to the right, or to the left.
Go up therefore with the incision where it appears to you that the aposteme and collection of water are, and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And beware lest you cut the artery, and there occurs a flow of blood, and the weak person dies from that flow with an evacuation of humidity.
Concerning the incision of arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter Two.
When there occur to someone catarrhs to the eyes, or to the chest, and that is aged, and the cure of medicines does not help: then the greatest cure is in that, the incision of these two arteries.
It is necessary therefore when one wishes to make an incision on them, that the weak head should be shaved. Then rub the place with a coarse cloth, so that the arteries appear. Then let the weak one constrict his neck, with any cloth of his. Then consider where the vein beats. And the places of both are places which are behind the ears depressed: and is rarely hidden, except in certain men.
Then mark with a branding iron above both: and let an incision be made on both sides with a sharp instrument, the incision extending to the mouth: and let it be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, introduce the instrument under the artery, and make an incision upwards with the incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers joined.
For when the vein is cut, blood comes out with a pulsating flow, which jumps to the inside with a frequent leap.
If however the artery does not appear to the sense, then it is necessary that you measure from the ear the quantity of the space of three fingers. Then mark with a branding iron, and extend to the mouth.
And what is necessary to emit from the blood, is six ounces and perhaps you emit more or less from it. All that according to the quantity which appears to you from the virtue of the weak one and the fullness of his artery.
Then consider the ulcer. Then if something remains at the mouth from the flow, then cut it, so that hot aposteme does not occur: then constrict the ulcer with ligatures made from linen cloth.
Then treat it with an ointment, until it is healed.
On the extraction of arteries that are in the temples.
Chapter Three.
When an old headache occurs to a man, or sharp catarrh because of acidic moistures and heat in the temples, or an old abscess, and similar things: and it is treated with modes of curative medicine, and it does not work: then we are experienced in these diseases in the extraction of arteries from the temples, or cauterization of them, as we have narrated.
And the method of operation in extracting them, is that the weak [operator] should shave the hairs which are on the temples, and direct it to the apparent artery in the temple. It itself shows you from its own pulse, and is rarely hidden except in few men, or by the forgetfulness of cold.
If it is hidden from you, then the weak one should bind his head with any bandage of his: then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or fumigate the place with warm water, until it appears to you with a clear appearance. Then take the spatula, which I formed a little before. Then scratch the skin with it with ease, until you reach the artery. Then throw into it a hook, and pull it upwards, until you pull it out of the skin, and make it protrude from the tunics that are under it on every side.
If the artery is slender, then envelop it with the extremity of the hook. Then cut from it a part according to the quantity by which its extremities stretch and contract, and there does not occur a flow of blood. For if it is not cut, and if it is not severed, the blood does not ascend inwardly.
Then let out from the blood a sixth of an ounce, up to three.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with a double thread, strong, of silk: or of Alhohod cord, so that putrefaction does not come to it before consolidation of the wound, and there occurs a flow of blood.
Then cut away the redundancy which is between the two ligaments. Do that at that hour or after another hour.
And if you use it, cauterize it with a cauterizing iron to the mouth, with a razor-shaped cauterizer, until its extremities are cut and it stands in the place of the same operation.
And it is a better work, unless the patient has a fever or is of a hot complexion. For the cauterizer is one of those things which help to end moistures. Therefore it is a better remedy.
And after the extraction of the arteries, the place should be filled with old cotton, and powders properly made should be placed on it.
And after the healing, it should be treated with dry medicines which make flesh grow, and with bandages, until it is healed.
What if meanwhile while you are operating there occurs a flow of blood from the artery, then hasten to cut it with a cauterizer, or to fill the place with cotton, and constrict your hand until the blood is stopped. What if none of those things are at hand, then place your finger over the spot until the blood coagulates. And moisten the spot with water of vehement cold, until the acute pain subsides, and is constricted according to what is necessary.
And from that which is lighter and easier than the extraction of an artery, it is to cauterize a vein with this cauterizer having two razor-like edges, and let the sharpness be similar to the file of a double-edged instrument. However they are much less sharp than two razor-like edges. For if they are sharp like two razor-like edges, the flesh hastens to become cold, and they do not cut with swiftness. When there is some thickness in them, heat of fire is retained in them, and the flesh is cut quickly: when this work is done it is better than any work, and lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of the cauterization.
And let the space that is between two cauteries be the quantity of the thickness of a finger, after you have marked two places with cauterization: and apply both heated irons until they reach the mouth, and cut the vein in two places: so that what is between the extremities will be pulled out. For they are not consolidated with this cauterization thoroughly.
Chapter 4.
On the treatment of a flow of tears that flows constantly from the eyes.
When there are constant tears and there is a flow of them from the veins that are on the outside above the skull of the head: and no treatment with medicine or medicines helps them, and you see the face of the weak already reddened, and he feels in his forehead a crawling, as is the crawling of ants: and his eyes are strained, moist, so that already the lashes have been eaten away by corrosion: and his eyelids have been heated up from the sharpness of tears: then the ancients discovered this operation for their treatment, and they called it treatment with Ense.
And it is that you should instruct the weak person to shave the hairs that are on his forehead. Then cut three equally distant fissures in the forehead according to its length: and let the length of the fissure be about two fingers, and one fissure be equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second following the length a little from the movement of the arms, and the third on the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries that are in both sides: and stretch out your hand from the continuation of the two jaws, and let the space of all incision be the quantity of three joined fingers: and have a piece of prepared sponge, or many dried cloths, with which you will dry the blood.
Then insert a sharp spatula between the two extremities, whose next mole is in the form. From the incision which is on the temporal side, up to the second incision: and excise with it all the skin, which is between the two incisions, with the sifac, which is above the mouth. Then do that also from the middle incision up to the second incision. Then extract this spatula, and insert into the first incision again another instrument, which is called a sharp cultellum, sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side. And this is its form.
And put its sharp side towards the flesh joined with the skin, and its blunt side towards the mouth: and drive it, until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut with it all the vessels, which descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision continues up to that which appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And after a quantity of blood has flowed out equal, press out the places from clots of coagulated blood.
Then put in all the fissures a dressing from old cloth, and put over them poultices soaked in wine, and oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot aposteme does not occur.
And on the third day loosen the ligament, and administer a large infusion with warm water. Then treat it with royal ointment, and other things which are used to treat wounds: until it heals.
Chapter 5.
On the treatment of sharp tears and discharges from the eyes from the interiors of the head.
When sharp and bitter catarrhs occur to someone, and you see their eyes weakened, pale, and already their sight is debilitated: and the eyelids are ulcerated, and hairs fall out from them, and in the depth of the head there is sharp pain, aching, and consequent sneezing: then take from these accidents, that those matters and catarrhs do not come, except from many veins.
And better treatment in them is this. And it is that you should order the weak to scratch their own forehead. Then cut in it a single fissure in the middle of the forehead, or another one across the width, and start with the fissure from the left temple to the right temple: and let the incision be up to the bone: and stretch out your hand from the muscles of the temples, which move during mastication: until when the bone is laid bare: and you will dry up all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the fissure with a cloth of old fabric, or with a linen thread. Then bind from above with small balls: and soak the small balls with wine and oil, so that no hot aposteme occurs.
And when you loosen it, and you see that the hot aposteme has already diminished: then it is necessary to rub the bone, until flesh begins to grow in it.
Then treat it with an excitative regimen that makes flesh grow: as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony: and prepare from them a plaster, and administer to the growth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter Six.
On the treatment of that which falls into the Ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or similar to a stone, such as iron and vitriol: or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans: or it is a fluxible thing, such as water and vinegar, and things similar to them: or some animal.
When therefore a stone or a kind of stone falls into the ear from those that do not adhere nor grow in the ear, expose the ear to the sun: if therefore you see the stone, then drip into the ear something of violet oil, or of sisamum. Then administer the extraction of it, and make sneeze with clysters, and stop the nostrils at the approach of sneezing, after you put in the circle of the ears pieces of cloth, or wool, and stretch the ear upwards.
For it often comes out with this treatment.
If it does not come out, then administer the extraction of it with Gestum, that is, finely ground Pizigarolis: whose form is this.
If therefore it comes out with gestum, it is good.
And if not, then administer the extraction of it with a finely ground hook of a small double quantity.
If therefore it does not come out through that, then make a tube from bronze, and insert the end of the tube into the opening of the ear deeply, and block what is around the tube with wax softened with oil, so that there is no passage except for the tube: then extract with your forced blowing attraction.
For it often comes out with what we have described. If it does not come out in this manner: then take a small amount of helkalimbat, or of uisco, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught, and put it at the end of the style after you have wrapped around it a cloth decently positioned: then insert it into the opening of the ear with ease, after you have cleaned the ear from humidity.
If therefore it does not come out with all that we have described, then proceed to cut, before there occurs a warm aposteme, or spasm.
And the manner of cutting is, like a phlebotomy of the weak in the beginning in the head: and let out from it blood according to the measure of its virtue. Then make the weak one sit between your hands, and turn its ear upwards, and find a small cut in the root of the ear at its fat in the depressed place itself: and let the cut be of a lunar figure, until you reach the stone: then remove it with what is possible for you from instruments.
Then heal the fissure from your hour with speed and cure it until it is healed.
What if a thing falls into the ear is one of the grains which grow and become inflamed then administer its extraction.
If it does not come out for you at the exit, then take a thin, subtle spatula, according to this form.
And administer with it the cutting off of that species of grains which fall into the ear and do not do that when you think that grain has already been moistened by the vapor of the ear until you put many a poultice: then extract them with forceps or with a subtle instrument or with suction as we have said for its extraction will be alleviated.
In the water which enters into the ear, it is necessary that he use a weak snorting with condiments at first: and now his ear has been filled with some old cotyle: and it lies on that ear in which there is water. If then it comes out through that, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones, according to the length of a finger, thin, gentle, and warm them with fire for a little while, and let him put one of them into the opening of his ear: and let him step on one of them with his foot from that side, and strike with another stone above the stone which is in the ear. He should not therefore cease to do that with stone and stone until all the water comes out.
And now the water comes out thus, that you take from a pomegranate or from a juniper one, and put one end of it into the ear: and burn the other end with fire until most of it is consumed. Then take another juniper and do that many times until all the water comes out: and extract it with a tube according to what was previously said regarding the stone.
In the extraction of an animal entering into it, if it is of small body, such as a flea, and its like, treat it as I have said in the division.
But if it is of large body, which appears to the senses: then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks: and its affair is easier than all those that are held in the ear.
In the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division, and in the treatment of distillations, and your treatment does not work: then it is necessary that you look at the ear in the sun. If, therefore, anything appears there of worms, then extract it with forceps, or with subtle hooks.
If, however, nothing appears there, then take a cannula, whose shape is this, narrow below, wide above: and introduce the subtle extremity into the ear according to the quantity that the weak part tolerates: then suck with a strong suction. Do it many times, until all the worms come out.
If, however, they do not obey you to come out, then stop up the ends of the cannula with wax, as I have said concerning the stone.
What if they do not come out with what we have said: then administer the distillations which the ancients have already experienced in the affliction of worms: and you will find it in the treatment of distillations.
And let your pouring be what you pour in oils and medicines into the ear with this instrument, whose shape is depicted here. As you see, make it of silver, or of copper, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, wide above.
Let it be so that the thing which is in the concavity of the cannula, is made from copper, turned on a lathe. And if you use it, take a rod, and anoint the extremity of it with cotton of a suitable anointing: then pour oil, or juice, or what you wish of these medicines into the cannula: and let it be in the ear: then introduce the rod with the cotton on top, and express it by your hand with tempered compression, until the oil is poured into the concavity of the hearing, and the weak part feels it inside: and what is poured into the ear, let it be previously warmed with fire for a little while: and beware lest there be something that is distilled in the cold valve: for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
On the treatment of an injury to the ear.
Sometimes infants come out of their mothers' wombs unperforated.
And it has already happened to some men that an obstruction occurred in the passage of the ear from a wound or flesh born in it.
And this obstruction is sometimes made in the deep hole of the ear, which sight does not reach: and sometimes it happens at the outer part of the hole, and sight reaches it.
The one, therefore, that happens in the deep ear, and which sight does not comprehend, is in many cases difficult to cure.
And in the one that sight reaches, it is necessary to put the ear of the infirm person in the sun, and look at it: then if you see the apparent obstruction, open it with a spatulum subtle, whose form is this which follows, let its extremity be where there is some little width, whose part is acute: and let the rest of the spatulum be smooth on both sides: so that it does not harm the ear.
If the obstruction is flesh born in the ear's passage, then hold it with a subtle hook, and cut it with a vehement facility, until all the flesh is removed.
What if the obstruction is in the deep ear: then take a subtle and smooth rod, and heat it with fire for a little while: then put it into the ear's passage.
If you feel the obstruction with this, that the infirm person meets with heaviness of hearing, try to open it with facility: and beware, lest you wound the nerves of the ear.
Then put in the ear a plug according to the quantity of the width of the hole, which you wrapped in Egyptian ointment: do this for some days, until you are secure about the consolidation of the wound.
Take a piece of linen cloth, and soak it in rose water, and sprinkle powdered sugar over it.
If you see that the abscess has already formed a postule, then it is necessary to extract the linen cloth, and let it out with another cloth, which you will have wrapped in cotton soaked in rose oil, until the hot aposteme subsides: then cure it until it heals.
And if a flow of blood happens: then submerge a sponge or cloths in cold water and put them on the ear: and use the remaining treatment that stops the flow of blood as described in the chapter on distillations.
Of the treatment for ulcers of the eyelids.
Chapter 8.
These are the ulcers that occur on the eyelids of the eye, when they are moist and when they are dry.
It is necessary therefore to pull them with forceps, or with a hook, and to cut them with scissors from their roots. Then put powdered sugar on the places.
If blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine lancet.
And it is better to cauterize them after the incision. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Of the treatment for pimples of the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter 9.
It often happens on the eyelids something that resembles a nut in its hardness and density, and because of this it is called a pimple: and it is a collection of thick humidity on the upper eyelid and lower eyelid.
The operation for them is therefore to consider then if it is a pimple that proceeds from what appears on the eyelid and moves to every part, and its treatment is easy.
Find then an incision above it in width. Then scratch it on every side with ease, until it is hollowed out. Then hang it with a hook, and cut it off.
If however the cutting away of it is not prepared for you, unless after you penetrate the eyelid with a small incision, then it will harm the weak one in something.
What if the incision is large: then bring it together with a suture: and treat it, until it heals.
And if it is small, no evil will come to you from it. For an ointment restores it and fleshes it over.
And if a gland is inclined towards the interior of the ear turned to the inner surface, then turn the eyelid, and hang the gland with a hook: besides which you will need an incision, and press it from every side.
What if the eyelid is lengthened by an incision, it does not harm it in anything.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the gland with salt water: and treat the place with that which fleshes it over, until the weak one is healed.
On the treatment of eyelids.
Chapter 10.
Axirnath is a tumor that forms in the upper eyelids: and it most often happens to children: and it weighs on their eyes, and they get cataracts in the mornings, and they cannot look at the light of the sun, because tears rush to them, and on account of this you see them rub their faces constantly, and they sleep with them, and their eyelids are moist under the eyebrows, which an inflammation has already covered: and a visible bulge of Axirnath appears to the eye, and when you press the place with your fingers, you feel the Axirnath between them.
And the method of its operation is that the weak person puts his head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare on it ligatures, and make from that ligature a circle according to the quantity that the Achilles contains on all sides. Then put it above it, and press with your fingers from all sides, so that the Achilles gathers in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that humidity with a spatula making an incision through the width, and let the incision not be greater than what is made in phlebotomy in depth.
Or it is necessary that you make a cut through the entire skin, until you reach the Achilles, for in many dispositions the Achilles proceeds from an incision according to the form of a fragment of white fat.
Extract it therefore with the cloth that you had placed between your fingers on the outside, and you move your hand to the right and left until it is dispersed, and beware lest you add to the incision, so that you do not penetrate with the spatula to the eye and harm it.
If however the Achilles does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, you add a little to the incision with ease, until the Achilles proceeds. Then extract it, as we have said.
Then soak the cloths in vinegar and water, and put them on the place, and bind it with bandages.
And there are men who apply salt, and put it in the cavity of the incision, so that it dissolves what remains of that humidity.
Then treat it, until it is healed.
If however a hot abscess occurs in the place, then treat it with cooling plasters, until it is healed.
On the ways of relaxing the eye, that is, when the eyelid becomes softened, whence it happens that it protrudes as it should not.
Chapter 11.
When hairs grow in the eyelids of the eye besides the normal course of hairs, and become long: then they harm the eye, and cause various ailments, such as constant tears, and softening of the eyelids, and a white spot, until it becomes a cause of destruction of the eye.
And the cataract of the eye is treated in four ways. Either by cauterization with fire. Or with sharp medicine, according to what was previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization. Or cataract is treated by incision and suture. Or with a needle, as I will say.
It is necessary that you put the head of the afflicted person on your lap. Then turn back the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If it is thus turned back, it is good. And if not: introduce the needle into it, where the thread is from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the thread from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the margin of the eyelid, where the hairs grow: and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it back with the needle.
Then find on the inner part of the eyelid under the additional hairs with the spatula inserting from the greater lacrimal to the lesser lacrimal.
Then extract the thread, and put under the eyelid a small cotton pad from a cloth or a pouch: then mark above the eyelid with cauterization, which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Truly it is necessary that the figure be made according to the quantity and elevation of the eyelid: because it is already diversified in men.
For there are some who require that a large quantity be cut from the eyelid, according to the quantity which is softened by the eyelid.
Then with the spatula find above the two lines that you marked, and start from the larger tear to the smaller tear, and let there be one incision near the natural hairs, as is the thickness of the radius.
Then insert the hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then scrape out the whole thing. Then bring together with a suture the two lips, with a needle and thread of fine wool: and wipe away the blood, and make adhere what overflows from the threads above the two eyebrows, with certain things that cause scar formation, if you want to do that: and if not, do not trouble.
Then leave the suture and threads until three days, or four: afterwards attend to it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without a suture, and treat it with that which dries and tightens. For the eyelid is elevated at the time of the wound's inflammation and its aggregation: but the future will be better.
This method of treating, the ancient ones taught Ataxmir.
But truly in it is solicitude for the weak, and it is from a good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method exists also in Ataxmir: and it is that you mark on the eyelid a figure, which is like the shape of the myrtle leaf, as we have described.
Then elevate the eyelid with three separate hooks, or joined ones, according to this shape which follows, then cut away what is excess with small forceps, according to this shape, with equality of incision.
If however it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it does not equal that: then take a needle in which there is a thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure: and insert another thread near the larger tear, and a second thread near the smaller tear: and bring together between your fingers the threads with equality.
Then elevate your hand to an equal elevation and elevate the cut above which the scar is marked, as we have narrated.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture: and cure it, until it heals.
If a tumor or an aposteme occurs at your incision or your fissure: but apply that aposteme with a plaster, and similar things, from sitting ointments.
And when it happens to the eyelids also, so that their hairs are converted.
It is necessary therefore that you use what we have said in the incision: but the suture of both is more artificial. On the treatment of Ataxmir with dogs.
And it is, that you convert the eyelid, and split the fissure which is within, according to what I have narrated. Then skillfully make two rods, or two thin sticks: whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and the width of them is less than the width of a spatula: and you have already made an incision at the extremities of both from both parts where you hold the threads.
Then bring together both with subtlety above that which overflows from the eyelid of the eye, and bind two rods from both parts with a strong bandage, and leave it there for some days. For the skin will be constricted and will become black and will rot, until it falls off by itself.
If then it is delayed, then cut it with forceps. Then cure it, until it heals.
When therefore it is consolidated, the eyelid is elevated: and the hairs do not protrude.
And this is the shape of the two rods, which you will make according to this figure, and quantity in length and width.
Chapter Twelve.
On the elevation of protruding hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation on it is that you take a sharp needle and insert into it a thread of fine silk, thin. Then you gather its ends and tie each knot with a subtle knot, and the length of the threads is one palm.
Then you put on the Alinxotati another subtle thread under that briefly, and tie its ends if you wish.
Then the weak person puts his head in your lap, and it should be near the sun as the operation is declared: since it is a subtle operation, or a minute one.
Then you insert the needle with the thread into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly, until you extract from above a natural hair.
Then you pull the hair up to the thread of the Alinxotati: and insert into the Alinxotati that hair: if it is one, or two, or three, no more.
Then you pull your hand with it, until the hair goes out with the Alinxotati from the eyelid with the natural hair.
If the Alinxotati is pulled and the hair does not go out with it: you pull the Alinxotati downwards, with the thread that you put in it, until the Alinxotati goes out from the upper hole, and you see it with the sight of the eye: then you pull the Alinxotati, and the thread: and extract both, and close the eye: and keep it closed for two days, or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh is born over it.
What if the hair is short, then either associate to it a long hair from the natural hairs, and lift it with that: or leave it until it grows after a space, and then lift it.
Chapter 13.
When this condition occurs in the eye, it is called Leporina or Alxatrati disease of the eye.
And this Alxatrati disease is either natural or accidental.
The accidental one occurs from the consolidation of a wound, or from a cut, or from a cautery, and similar things.
And the method of treatment for it is that you find the consolidation and separate its lips and put between them a linen pad and tie it until it heals.
And it is not suitable to use in the treatment of it things that dry and constrict, for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was; instead use in it things that soften, such as fenugreek: and apply with water, in which cooked althea, flaxseed, and an ointment dyaquilon, which is melted with some oil: and anoint with it a linen pad, and treat with it.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you strive with all ingenuity, so that it does not consolidate, according to the shape it had initially.
And this operation that we mentioned is not other than a kind of correction of the Alxatrati, so that the shape of the place does not return according to what it was entirely.
On the treatment of Alxatrati that occurs on the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter 14.
This Alxatrati that occurs below is the one that is truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental one occurs from a wound, or a tear, or a cautery, and similar things.
And the method of operation for it is that you take a needle, in which there is double thread, and you insert it into the flesh, and make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread passes through both extremities of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with a needle, and cut it with a spatula on the side. If then the figure of the eyelid returns according to what is proper, it is good.
And if not, take a needle, and put it in the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it: and find two fissures on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the extremities of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and an angle is formed: until when they are gathered this figure is formed, and it is the letter lauda in Greek.
Then remove that flesh to the amount, which is the sharp lower side of it, from that which follows the eyelid. Then gather the separated parts: sew them with a thread of wool.
Then treat them with what we have spoken of, concerning softening medicines and plasters, until they heal.
And if there is a falling away of the eyelid from the fissure, and the suture, or the cauterization: then it is necessary that you find a flat fissure under the hair of the eyelids, also according to what has preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a plaster, according to what we have said.
And the summary of the speech concerning the treatment of the eyelid, when it is above or below, is that it runs on this operation, according to what is prepared from the form of the eyelid.
For it itself is of many diversities in form: and the skilled artisan prepares his device with whatever mode is possible for him, so that he makes it return to the natural figure, or near to it, and the infirmity itself, according to what is convenient for him from the operation and instruments in many dispositions.
Chapter 15.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the eye with the conjunctiva, or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many men, so that the upper eyelid becomes consolidated with the white of the eye and prohibits it from its natural operations.
Therefore you should insert the extremity of the needle under the eyelid and elevate it upwards; or extend it with a hook: then cut the adhesion with a subtle spatula, which is not sharp in its incision, but rather dull for a short time: so that the weak eye is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is moved and hurts the eye with its sharpness: and your incision should be as if you excise the adhesion with facility, until when the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Pour then into the eye salty water, with water wash it: or distill into it from a sieve made of dissolved zinc with water.
Then separate between the eyelid and the eye with a thread of flax: and put over the eye a woolen cloth soaked in the white of the egg: and after the third day use a sieve, and with poultices, until it is healed.
On the incision of the nail and elevation of the flesh of the tear ducts.
Chapter 16.
Know that the nail is according to two modes. Or it is nerve-like: and is similar to a thin, subtle splinter. Or it is not nerve-like, similar to the humidity of frozen white matter: which when it touches iron, or a hook is used to seize it with a hook, it is cut off, and the hook does not stick into it.
And both types do not begin except from the greater tear duct, whence it proceeds gradually, until it covers the use of it, and prohibits the light and motion of the eye.
And the method of operating on it is: that the weak person should place their head in your lap. Then open their eyes, and elevate the eyelid with your hand. Then collect the nail with the pincers of a pair of forceps, and extend it to the upper parts. Then take a needle, and insert into it a thread from the hairs of a horse, and cow, or goat: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle for a short while, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread, and extend it to the upper parts: and scratch the side of the nail which follows the pupil, as if you were to sew it with the thread up to its posterior.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a subtle spatula, or with small forceps: and remove the natural flesh of the tear duct, so that there does not occur from the cutting a constant flow of tears.
And sometimes the nail is extended with pincers alone: or with a thread, as we have said. Then scratch with a blunt spatula, whose shape is this.
And beware, lest the spatula touch the corneal layer, and there occur a rupture in it, and the pupil be perforated.
Then after the cutting, throw into the eye some ground salt, or a compound made from zinc, and let it remain there until another day. Then treat it with what is necessary, until it is healed.
For if the nail is not nerveless, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor can a pincer be fixed in it, then this type of nail is often treated with medicines, and healed as I have described in the section.
For if you intend to cut it, then open the eye of the weak person, and take a subtle, thin, delicate spatula, with a sharp edge, according to this shape. And scratch the nail from above with subtle scraping. What if you see that it itself becomes rarefied and crushed, and the scraping is imprinted on it, then distill from your hour, into the eye, either a decoction made from lily or from red lead, or from triticated salt: and cover the eye until another day.
Then repeat the operation over it, until all of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in your eye to the contrary: then cease that, and cure the abscess, until it is healed.
Then repeat the operation over it with scraping, until it is healed.
The flesh of the tear duct, if it harms the eye with great annoyance, should be suspended with a hook, and a portion of it cut away: and do not make the last cut, so that the flow of tears does not happen.
Then pour into the tear duct either red lead or made from verdigris, until it is healed.
Of the incision of Alguardenic, and flesh born in the eye.
Chapter 17.
There is born in certain eyes of men a red flesh, composed or overlaid, until it covers the pupil, or approaches, or presses on the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward: and it is similar to the rose called balas ruby.
And the operation for this also is, that the weak one places his head on your lap. Then open his eyes, and collect all that flesh with hooks suitable for that: and hold it with one forceps or tongs.
Then cut the flesh first, until the whole of it is ended with cutting. And beware of the eye, so that you do not harm it during operation: and let your cutting be with which you cut it, or with a spatula, which we have described in the cutting of the nail: or with small forceps, with which the asphodel is collected, according to what will appear after this chapter.
When the incision is complete and the entire white cataract is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt or pour into it red powder, and similar things from corrosive medicines, and put on top of the eye a plaster made from the whites of eggs to secure it from the hot aposteme.
What if there remains something of the cataract and hot blood dominates you, and a hot aposteme is coming on: then leave the eye, and cure it with what settles the aposteme.
Then repeat over it the treatment until it is healed.
And do similarly with the flesh that grows in the eye from this condition.
Truly it is necessary that you should omit the cure of every weak eye from natural or accidental damage, by operating with iron: and do not put your hand into any of these operations until you see that the member can tolerate that operation.
Of the collection of pus in the eye.
Chapter 18.
Pus are red veins that gather over the eye and prevent the sight from performing its function, and weaken the eye with the passage of time.
It is necessary therefore for you first to consider if the eye in which there is pus is strong and does not have any illness in it except for the pus: then collect its pus.
And it is that you should order the weak person to put their head on your lap.
Then suspend those veins with one hook or two according to your judgment and let the hook be subtle and double-edged, according to this shape, or let there be two hooks graduated on one body, according to this shape.
Then collect those veins with subtle forceps with subtlety and wipe away the blood hour after hour until you see those veins removed from the eye and resolved and beware lest you harm the eye with the extremities of the spatula.
And let your operation be at midday before the sun, and you should be strong in your operation indeed: so that you do not cut except those veins.
And when you have cleansed, then distill into the eye either a green or red substance: so that its sharpness wears away what remains of the cataract.
If however it is not possible for you to collect all of it in that hour, then apply a plaster to the eye with what soothes the pain of the eye: and leave it for some days, until its pain has subsided: and it is secure from a hot abscess: then repeat over it again according to the same method, until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps.
On the reduction of the nasal fracture.
Chapter 19.
Physicians call it Arissat's fistula, which you will treat with a cautery, or with a sharp burning medicine, according to what its description has previously stated.
And if it does not heal, then there is no ingenuity in it: except that you find a collection of pus at the birth of it, and extract all the moisture that is in it and pus, until the bone is exposed.
When therefore the bone is exposed, and you see in it a rottenness or blackness, then scrape it with an instrument, whose form is this.
And it is called the head of the scraper.
Let it be made from Indian iron, and let its head be round like a grindstone, which should be punctuated with the punctuation of a file, or the punctuation of a fine awl: and put it on the corrupted place of the bone.
Then rub it between your fingers, and you press your hand for a little while, until you know that the corruption has already been scraped: and do that several times.
Then restore the place with medicines that are astringent and caustic.
If then the place is consolidated, and flesh is born in it, and the flow of blood is stopped: and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and is not burst, and no accident happens to it: then you should know that it is already healed.
And if not, then there is no ingenuity in it, except to reduce the fistula to the opening of the nose, according to this method.
And it is, that you scrape the bone with iron, or with sharp medicine: and when the bone is scraped, then take an instrument according to this method, let it be a metal extremity, triangular: and its wood be a pyramid extended to the extremity, as you see.
Then put that on the bone itself: and let it be near the tear duct: and stretch out your hand a little from the eye. Then turn your hand with the instrument, until it penetrates the bone, and the weak one feels it: by which he finds the breath coming out of it, when he holds his hand over his nose.
Then restore the place with medicines that dry and tighten, as I have taught you.
When then flesh is born, and the place becomes hardened: then the matter that was flowing to the outside, returns to the nose: therefore it is easier on the weak one.
On the reduction of the protrusion of the Eye.
Chapter 20.
When the Eye protrudes with its entirety, and there is no damage to the sight, nor diminishment: then it is necessary that you release the weak one: then you phlebotomize him: afterwards you put cups on the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then put on the Eye an ointment that is made from gum acacia, and aloe, and galbanum, and myrrh. Then tie a bandage over the eye with many poultices on top.
And the method of poultices is that you take many soft cloths and double each cloth four times: make many of them: then put them one on top of another, a second according to the quantity which the eye needs: then tie over them with a hard cloth tightly.
And repeat the applications with suction without scarification.
Then remove the applications, and bind the eye tightly by day and night.
If however the bandage is again softened, then it is necessary that it be tied tightly: then remove the bandage after a day and night, as we have said.
If therefore you see the eye already returned to its own coat, it is good: and if not, then repeat the plaster, and poultices, and bandage, and applications, until it is healed.
On the incision of the conjunctiva.
Chapter 21.
When a disruption occurs in the conjunctiva, then it protrudes and comes out from between the eyelids like a grain of the eye, and the shape of the man is disfigured because of that, and there is need to cut it: then the operation on it is, according to what I relate: and it is, that you insert a needle in the root of the conjunctiva from the lower to the upper part.
Then insert another needle where there is a double thread on the tear side, and make it penetrate: and leave the first needle according to its own position.
Then cut the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the conjunctiva around the needle: and tie it tightly.
Then pull out the needle, and put over the eye a woolen cloth on the white of the eye: and leave it, until the thread falls away with the conjunctiva.
Then treat the eye with what comforts it, until the wound is healed.
And when the wound is constricted on every side with a circle of cloth, until the eye adds great prominence.
Then it should be perforated with a subtle spatula, which penetrates to the depth of the eye, and the white moisture flows out, and the eye sinks down, and immediately enters: then constrict it, until it is healed.
On the treatment of cataract.
Chapter 22.
This disease which is called Alcomnati, is nothing but a humor, that accumulates in the eye, similar to descending water, and it is not itself.
And the method of operating on it is, that you make the weak patient sit on a prepared seat, or a directed one.
Then take the head of it with your hands on both sides, and move it, until you see the cataract pass to the lower parts of the eye. Then it is fixed, and does not come down, and the light is freed: and the weak person sees things as he used to see: and if it does not come down below, know that it is water.
If however the descent of the cataract is not prepared with what we have said, then make the weak person sit between your hands. Then take a subtle spatula, and press with it above the corneal membrane a little at the continuation of the conjunctiva with the corneal tunic in the corona, until the cataract comes out.
When it comes out, then distill into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey: or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey: then let it be treated with the remainder of the treatment, until it is healed.
On the treatment of water that descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter 23.
We have spoken of the types of waters in their division, and those that are suitable for perforation, as the discourse declares and sets forth, then take that same thing according to health.
Then therefore it is necessary that you make the weak one sit between your hands square, opposite to the light, near the sun: and bind his healthy eye, and hold it strongly: then touch the eyelid of his eye with your right hand, if the eye in which there is water is the left eye: or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
Then take with your right hand the cataract needle, if the eye is the left one: or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
Then place the point of the needle near the corona through the thickness of the radius on the white of the eye apparent at the lesser lacrimal corner: then push the needle with strength: and you withdraw your hand with it, until it penetrates into the white of the eye: and you feel that the needle has now reached the empty space.
And it is necessary that the length of the journey of the needle to the depth be the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness: and this is the corona of the eye. For you will see it in the same pupil of the vision of the eye, because of the clarity of the corneal tunic.
Then place the needle to the place where the water is. Then press it below little by little one time after another.
If therefore the water descends at once, then the weak one sees that which is above what has opened his vision at once, and the needle in his eye.
Then rest a little. If therefore the water descends, then put it back a second time, except that the needle should come out.
When I elevate the arm, and it does not rise: then extract the arrowhead with ease: and you will free your hand little by little.
Then dissolve something from clear salt of gem in water: and wash the inflamed eye with it. Then place outside the eye a whole pledget or a cloth soaked in rose oil and white of egg: and tie it to the sound eye.
We however, and those who are of our time, put on top a mixture of ground cumin with white of egg.
What if the arrowhead does not pass into the eye for you on account of its hardness, since it is from a man whose eye is very hard: then you should take a spatula, which is called Alberid, of this form.
Perform with it the conjunction only so much, and do not put your hand in the opening: and it is not except to make a way to the arrowhead subtle place.
Then insert the arrowhead, according to what we have said.
And at the completion of your work, prepare for the weak person a bed decently made, in which they sleep on their back in a dark house: and prohibit them from all movements, and from coughing: and put their food that which soothes their nature: and do not move their head to the right or left at all.
And let the dressing be according to its condition until the third day.
Then undo it in that dark house, and test his vision: and make him see things. Then return to the dressing until the seventh day.
And it is not necessary to do that at the time of treatment, or immediately after the incision: but rather it is necessary to avoid it: because water rises quickly through violent looking.
If however a hot aposteme occurs: then it is necessary that you loosen the eye before the seventh: and straighten it with what sits on the aposteme, until it sits: then without its use, and put over its face an oral bandage which regulates its use under it for some days, it being in that dark house.
Then he should go out of the house gradually, and stay in his own houses.
And know that the disciple is not to be blamed for looking at the zältea several times: for then he will dare above the operation.
And now indeed there has come to us one of those who are from Alayrach, who said that a perforated magdan was made in Alayrach, through which water is sucked.
For I have not seen anyone in your land who has done that: nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients: and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of species from the magda, so that he who is ignorant of them will stand upon them.
Let them be made from their own copper: and let their extremities be with the same subtlety triangular, and the extremities pointed.
Chapter 23.
Of the treatment of flesh born in the nose.
Sometimes there are born in the nose diverse fleshes that are added, among which is something that resembles a scorpion of many feet: and from it is a cancerous flesh, of stone, of dark color: and from it is what is a smooth flesh, not of dark color.
In that therefore which is from these fleshes and is smooth, not deceitful, nor cancerous, it is necessary that you make the weak one sit between your hands opposite the sun, and open its nostrils, and throw a hook into those fleshes: then draw them to the outside, and cut what you have comprehended of them with a subtle sharp spatula on one side, until you know that the whole flesh has already been removed.
What if there remains anything of it whose removal is not possible then rub that with one of the subtle instruments with ease until there does not remain anything of it. If however the blood dominates you, or there occurs a hot abscess, then apply to it what is suitable, or it is one of the malignant abscesses, then cauterize him quickly, until the blood is cut off, and all the flesh is taken away.
Then let vinegar and water or wine be thrown into the nose after the incision. If however the nose appears and moisture proceeds from it up to the palate, then know that he is already healthy.
If however the moisture does not penetrate according to what is suitable, then know that there is flesh born inside it in the upper bones of the nostril, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Then therefore it is necessary that you insert a thread of linen which has some thickness and knots on it many knots and put between each knot the quantity of a finger or less and let the weak one ingeniously so that he puts one end of the thread into the nose with a rod, or with whatever is possible for him after he made something similar to azar and pull his mucus until it reaches the lowest part and comes out above his throat.
And many boys indeed do something similar to this in schools of Atto, and it is an easy thing for one who wants to.
Then put two ends of the thread, one end which comes out above the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then take a minister to saw the flesh with the knots that are on the thread. Do that until you know that the flesh is already caught with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread and put into the nose after wiping away the blood a dressing that you have dipped in Egyptian ointment, do that for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then put at the end of the thing a lead tube in the nose for some days until it is healed.
If however it needs a treatment which dries, use that one.
To make a head purging device, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it out of silver or copper: which should be similar to a small lamp, open like a shell: and its passage similarly.
And if you want to make a closed tube, like a reed.
And the shell of the instrument for making a head purging device should be full, detected: which has a handle at its end, with which you hold it, when you heat in it oil, or whatever you want from juices and flowing things.
Chapter 25.
Often a polyp grows at the end of the nose and increases and grows with time until its appearance becomes foul.
And because of that it is necessary to cauterize it at the beginning of its appearance and remove all of it. Then put on the place either a cautery or a medicine that burns which takes its place.
If the cutting of it is delayed until it becomes large, then consider if it is stony, hard, of a dark color, of little sensation: then do not touch it with iron: because it is a cancerous apostema.
For I have often seen that he who cuts a cancerous apostema and because of it a great evil happens to the one who has it.
And if the apostema is of a soft touch, not dark in color, and you see that cutting all of it is possible, then evacuate the weak part, and cut it without fear, without anxiety: and treat the place with what dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
On the suture of the nose, and lip, and ear.
We should know when there occurs a solution of continuity in one of these cartilages, the operation is not very reliable, except in certain men.
It is necessary therefore when anything of that sort happens to someone, that you consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood, and then bring the lips of the wound together with a suture, and afterwards treat it until it heals.
And if the lips of the separation are already separated, and each part has become healthy, then it is necessary that you excavate every part from its own skin until it becomes bloody. Then bring the two lips together with a suture, and tie them, and powder over the blood with dragon's blood and ground frankincense, and put above the powders a piece of plaster of diafion, and one of the other consolidating plasters: and leave it tied for two days, or three. Then untie it, and change the medicine, and leave it until the threads come apart by themselves. Then treat it with ointment, until it heals.
The method of the suture is that you bring together the separation of continuity, either with needles, as we have described in the suture of the belly, or bring together the separation with a thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter 27.
On the extraction of knots of the lips.
Many men have occurrences of hard scabs on the insides of their lips similar to grains of a herb, and some are smaller and others larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you turn back the lip, and find above every knot of it, and suspend it with a hook, and cut into it from every side.
Then fill the place after the incision with zegi trot, until the blood is cut off.
Then let it be glued with vinegar, and the place be cured with it, in which there is a contraction, until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 28.
Of the incision of the flesh born above the gums.
Flesh often grows above the gum, which the ancients call abuluss.
It is necessary therefore that you suspend it with a hook: or that you hold it with forceps, and cut it at its root, and let the blood or serum flow.
Then put over the place zegi trot, or other powders that are astringent and excitant.
For if that flesh returns after the cure, for it often returns, then cut it a second time, and cauterize it: for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter 29.
Of the scraping of the teeth with iron.
Sometimes there gather on the surfaces of the teeth from the inner and outer part, and between the gums rough, foul, and sometimes blackened cortices, and they become yellow, and green, until corruption comes from that to the gum, and the teeth are made foul by it.
It is necessary therefore that you make the weak person sit between your hands, and his head in your lap, and scrape the molar and tooth, in which you see cortices, and things similar to sand: until nothing remains of them, and do similarly with the blackness, and greenness, and yellowness, and other things until they are clean.
If therefore that which is in them from the beginning of the scraping is removed, it is good: and if not, then repeat the scraping over them a second time, and a third, until the utmost effort reaches what you are using.
And you should know that the tooth requires razors of diverse forms, many figures, according to which your operation is prepared: namely, because the razor with which the tooth is scraped from the lower part is different from the razor with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms.
And these forms of razors should be prepared by you, which you will use.
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of toothache.
It is necessary that you treat the tooth for its pain with all ingenuity, and delay in extracting it. For it is not that its place is supplied when it is extracted: because it is a sublime substance: until when there is no excuse for extracting it, then it is necessary when the tooth is weakened to extract it, so that it may be strengthened and certified by you as a painful tooth.
For often the pain deceives the weak tooth, and it seems that it is in the adjacent tooth, and extracts it: then the pain does not cease, until the weak tooth is extracted.
We have already seen that from the operation of bloodletting physicians.
When therefore the painful tooth itself is certified by you, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is something of virtue, until the gum is loosened on all sides: then move it with your fingers or with subtle forceps at first little by little, until you shake it.
Then place large forceps in it with a good grip, and hold the head of the weak one between your knees: which you have already held well, so that it is not moved: then pull the tooth according to its straightness, so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not come out, then take one of those instruments, and introduce it under the tooth on all sides with ease, and constrain its movement, as you did at first.
If the dense is perforated or corroded, then it is necessary that you fill that hole with a piece of cloth and tie it with a slender extremity of a radius, so that it does not break in the hour of your constriction upon it with forceps.
And it is necessary that you act skillfully with scarification around the gum on all sides abundantly.
And preserve your strength, so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and it returns upon the weak part from that evil which is greater than its pain at first.
And beware, lest you do what ignorant bloodletters do in their haste and boldness over the eradication of it, beyond what we have administered.
For they often cause great weaknesses to come upon men, which are difficult to break, and all the roots of it remain, or some of them: or so that they extract it with certain bones of the jaw: as we have often testified to that.
Then let it be cleansed after its eradication with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
What if a flow of blood happens from the places (for it often happens), then take something of gum and fill the place with it: and if not, cauterize it if the gum does not adhere to you.
The shape of the slender forceps which moves the dense thing at first, should be of long extremities, with a short handle, thick, so that they do not double when pressed upon the tooth: they should be as you see of thick handles, until when you press with them, they do not bite themselves, nor double: of short extremities, and let them be made from iron or made decently from refined steel, with straight extremities: and in their extremities let there be teeth, which some enter into others, and press with a decent constriction, strongly.
And when there are extremities such as the form of the mouth of a bird which is called a cicatrice, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter 31.
Of the eradication of teeth, and the extraction of fractured jaw bones.
When there remains upon the eradication of a tooth which breaks something, then it is necessary that you put on the spot a plaster with butter for one day or two days, until the spot softens: then introduce to it cotton threads or forceps: whose extremities are similar to the mouth of a bird which is called ciconia. The form of this is that of forceps.
Let their extremities be made like the file of teeth: or like a toothpick.
If therefore it does not yield to you for exit with these forceps, then it is necessary that you cut above the root, and you remove all the flesh, and you introduce an instrument which is called a small lever, whose form is this.
Let it be of short extremity, somewhat thick: and let it not be imbued, so that it does not break.
If therefore the root goes out through that, it is good: and if not, it is necessary to help with these other instruments whose forms are these.
The form of the first is triangular extremity, in which there is a certain thickness.
The form of triangular extremity subtle.
And with other instruments and iron tools which were mentioned previously in the scraping of teeth.
And also let there be help with this instrument which is similar to a large hook, of this form, triangular extremity twisted, in which there is a certain thickness, so that it does not break: and let it not be imbued.
And you should know that there are many instruments for teeth: and similarly the rest of the instruments are not counted, and the skilled artisan, subtle in his art, finds by himself the instrument, according to which he signifies the operation and the diseases themselves.
For there are diseases for which the ancients did not mention an instrument, because of the diversity of their species.
If the bone breaks from the jaw or from other bones of the mouth, or becomes putrid: then probe above it in its place with that which is suitable for it from the instruments and forceps which I mentioned in the extraction of roots, and help with a gesture, whose form is this.
Let there be in it something of thickness gradually, so that it may be tightened, or the bone may be held with it, and it does not flee and is stable, until the bone emerges: and restore the place with medicines suitable for it.
If however there is little putridness in the bone, then scrape the bone itself from its putridness, and cure it, until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
On the sawing of prominent teeth above the others.
When the teeth do not protrude according to their natural course, a foul form is made by it: and especially when this happens in women, and girls, namely those who are vain.
It is necessary therefore that you should consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth, and does not fit in its place, or it is possible to file or smooth it: then extract it: and if it is connected to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, whose form is this.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made from Indian iron, of sharp extremity, strongly.
And let your cutting be that which you cut with for many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, so that another of the teeth is not disturbed.
If it is prominent and exposed to its own lime, then the lime itself with lime from indigo, whose form is this.
Let it be all from indigo, and its handle with few points of valda, just as the lime with which needles are made: with which it is smoothed gradually over many days with ease, so that the dens is not disturbed and falls away: then smooth the lime itself in the end, and rub it with certain rasps.
What if it is a dens, from which a part breaks and injures the tongue: then it is necessary to smooth it also, until the roughness of that fracture is removed, and it is made even: and flattened, and does not injure the tongue, nor corrupt the speech.
Chapter 33.
On the gold wire retention of teeth.
When there happens to the anterior teeth a concussion and commotion from a blow or fall: and the weak one cannot grasp onto what he eats so that it does not fall: and you have treated them with astringent medicines, and the treatment was of no use: then the ingenious plan for them is to bind with a gold wire: because silver becomes greenish, and putrefies after days: but gold never changes.
And let the wire be of moderate thickness and coarseness according to the quantity which it can hold between the teeth.
And the form of the retention is, that you take the wire and insert double of it between two healthy teeth: then weave with both ends of the wire between the movable teeth, whether one or several, until you come with the web to a healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving until the part from which you began: and tighten your hand with subtlety and wisdom, until it is not moved further: and let your constriction by which you tighten the wire be at the roots of the teeth, so that it does not protrude.
Then cut the superfluous filaments of the thread with the forceps, and take up both, and turn them with the tongs, and hide them between the sound tooth and the moved tooth, so that they do not hurt the tongue: then let it be tightened thus while it remains.
And if it becomes loosened, or is cut, tie them with another thread, and it will stay thus for the whole time.
And this is the form of the teeth, and the figure of the retention of two sound teeth, and two moved teeth, as you see.
And when it happens that one tooth or two return to their places after a fall, and are retained as we have described, and remain: and this is not done except by an artisan who is prudent and experienced.
And when something is adapted from the bones of the cheeks: and it is made into the form of a tooth, and put in the place, from which the tooth is strengthened and tied, as we have said: and it remains, and serves for a long time.
Chapter XXXIII.
Sometimes the ligament that occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which man is born, or accidental, from a wound which has already consolidated.
And the operation on it is, that you open the mouth of the infirm person in your lap, and elevate their tongue: then cut that nervous ligament in width, until the tongue is freed from its retention.
What if in it there is something hard and knotted, and it is from the consolidation of a wound: then introduce an instrument into that, and probe it in width, until you cut the ligament, and the knot is loosened.
And beware, lest there be a fissure in the depth of the flesh, and you cut the artery there, and there occurs a flow of blood.
Then let it be dressed in the wound with rose water, or with vinegar, or with cold water.
Then put under the tongue a plaster of flax, which will hold the weakness for all night, so that it is not consolidated by the second.
