If there should be a flow of blood, then put on the place powdered zeolite. If the blood truly conquers you, then cauterize the place with a lentiginous cautery, which is suitable for that: then cure it with the remainder of the cure, until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
It sometimes happens under the tongue an apostema which resembles a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural operation: and perhaps it grows so much, until it fills the mouth.
And the operation on it is, that you open the mouth of the weak person before the sun: and examine the apostema.
If you see it to be of a dark color, or black, and hard, which the weak person does not feel: then do not touch it, because it is a cancer.
And if it leans to whiteness, in which there is humidity, then throw on it a poultice, and cut it with a subtle spatula, and free it from every part. What if the blood conquers you during your operation, then put on it powdered zeolite, until the blood is stanched: then return to your operation, until you extract it with its complement.
Then let it be dressed with vinegar and salt: and cure it with the remainder of the cure suitable for that, until it is healed.
On the cure of the apostema of the two tonsils.
Chapter 36.
It sometimes happens in the throat a swelling, that is, glands, which are similar to almond-like swellings, which occur in the jaw: and they are named the two Amygdalae: which when you cure with what we have said in the division, and do not heal, then consider.
If it is a hard aposteme, of a dark colour, and of little sensation: then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of a red colour, and its root is thick: then do not touch it with iron either, fearing a flow of blood, but rather leave it until it matures. And then you will either bore through it: or it will rupture by itself.
And if it is of a white colour, round, and its root is subtle: then it should be cut.
And the operation on it is, that you consider before its operation, if the aposteme has completed its heat quietly, or is diminished by some diminution: then make the weak sit in front of the sun, and its head be in your bosom: and open its mouth, and let the servant receive between his hands, and press its tongue below with an instrument, of this form.
Let it be made from silver, or copper, which is subtle like a knife.
When you press its tongue with it, and the aposteme is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it: then take a hook, and fix it in the gum, and pull it outwards as much as you can, except that you draw with it something from the tunics.
Then cut it with an instrument, of this form which follows. It is assimilated to the forceps: nevertheless its extremities are curved, so that the orifice of each one is directly opposite the acute end: hence take care that it be of iron unbroken, or steel steeped.
If however this instrument is not at hand, then cut it with a spatulum, of this form. Let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you cut one gum, cut the other according to the same species of cutting.
Then afterwards the weak person should gargle with cold water or with vinegar and water.
And if a flow of blood happens to him, he should gargle with water in which the barks of pomegranates have been boiled: or myrtle leaves, and similar things from astringents, until the flow of blood is cut off: then treat it, until it is healed.
And when other abscesses form in the throat besides the two tonsils. Cut them therefore according to what I said in the incision of the two tonsils equally.
And I indeed have already treated a woman for an abscess that had formed in her throat, which was pulling towards the throat, with little sensation, which had almost closed the throat: and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and it had already prohibited her from eating, and drinking water: and she had already come close to death, if she had remained one day, or two days.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had sent out two branches, until they went out above the two nostrils.
I approached quickly and fixed a hook in one of them. Then I pulled it and a large portion was pulled out from it.
Then I cut it off, where it was grasping from the nostril.
Then I did to it what had come out of the other nostril.
Then I opened her mouth, and pressed her tongue: and afterwards I fixed a hook into the abscess: then I cut off from it a part of it, and there did not come out from it except a little blood: therefore the throat of the woman was freed, and she immediately began to drink water, and then took nourishment.
I did not therefore cease to cut into that abscess many times for a long time: and the abscess would fill in the place of what I was cutting, until it was prolonged too much and with it.
I cauterized the ingrown and the aposteme between the throat.
It stood firm after the application: then I walked away from that place, and I did not know what God did with it after me.
Of the incision of the aposteme of the eyelid.
Chapter 37.
When the cold descends to the eyelid and it becomes inflamed and is long: then it itself is called perpendicular: and if it is in the lower part thick, round, then it itself is called a pustule.
When it is treated with what we said in the division, and the treatment does not work: and you see that the hot aposteme has quieted from it, and is subtle: then it is necessary to incise it.
And that which from it is gathered round, and is not length to it, and is bloody, or of a dark color, or black, and there is no feeling to it: then it is necessary to avoid the incision of it. For in it is fear concerning the weak.
It is necessary therefore when you see it according to the method which I said from its whiteness and length, that you make the weak one sit before the sun: and press his tongue with an instrument, whose description was previously mentioned: then fix a hook in the eyelid, and pull it downwards: and incise it with one of the two instruments which I mentioned in the incision of the two glands.
And it is necessary that you do not incise from it, except that which is added above the natural limit without addition.
For if you incise from it more, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after the incision use that which we described in the incision of the two glands: and treat it, until it is healed.
If however the weak one trembles at the incision: then it is necessary that you use cunning in the cauterization of it without fear and dread.
And the manner of cauterization in it is not, unless with sharp water; and it is, as you put the head of the weak one in your bosom. Then press his tongue with the instrument that we mentioned. Then take from the water that I mentioned to you in the chapter on cauterization, and prepare with it a lime not extinguished, and put it not thick, nor subtle, and fill with it the concavity of this instrument, whose form is this.
Let the extremities of it in which the medicine is placed be concave, like the concavity of a spoon.
And put the instrument with medicine on the wound itself: and let the weak one be lying on his side, so that saliva runs out of his mouth from the inner medicine, so that nothing descends from it to his throat, and harms him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you apply it to the wound for the first time in the quantity of a half hour, until you see it already blackened, and the biting of the medicine is settled.
And if you want to take a piece of cloth, and wrap it around the extremity of the radius, and pour the cloth with medicine, and insert the radius with the cloth into the canal above, until the cloth adheres to the wound.
Do that many times, until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the wound. Then leave it alone: for it will fall off by itself and drop off after three days, or four.
What if you judge that you should repeat the medicine, repeat it: and after cauterization cleanse the area around the wound with a cloth soaked in butter, and clean it with what is in the area of the wound from the medicine: and wash with cold water.
And let it be treated externally with poultices, and internally with gargles, until it is healed.
This therefore is a more wholesome species of cure by incision and longer from fear.
And when the ulcers are cured also with that which is more subtle than cauterization and incision: for they are cured with fumigation, according to this method. Take calamentum, and hyssop, and origan, and rue, and ssach, and chamomile, and abrotan, and similar things from herbs: and gather all those things, or some of them in a pot, and submerge them with vinegar, and boil: and the pot should be luted with clay decently: and there should be an opening in the middle of the pot, over which is composed a concave instrument according to this method.
Let it be made from silver or brass: and the extremity, in which is a grain, should be inserted into the mouth of the infirm, until the vapour ascends to the ulcer above the tube, until the ulcer is much obscured.
Then iterate over it many times, until it is scraped away.
And beware, that you do not make this cure at the beginning of the approaching abscess. For a poultice adds to the abscess: and it is not proper to make that unless at the decline of the abscess when it becomes cold.
If however this instrument is not present to you, then take a cane, and compose on the extremity of it a cortex of egg: so that the mouth of the infirm does not burn: for the cortex of egg prohibits vapour from burning the mouth: and this is from good cure with health.
Of the extraction of thorns and bones from the jaw.
Chapter XXXVIII.
Many times it happens that a bone, or a fish spine, or something else sticks in the throat.
It is necessary therefore to extract that which of them appears obvious on which the sight falls, after you press the tongue with an instrument at the seat, and it is declared to you what is in the throat.
And what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary that you make the weak vomit before it is digested by food in the stomach. Perhaps the attached thing will come out through vomiting.
Or the weak person should swallow a piece of nap, or a root of lettuce, or swallow a small dry crumb of bread: or take a piece of soft dry sea sponge: and tie it on a thread, then swallow it. When it reaches the place where there are vertebrae, or bones, and is pulled out, then it comes out.
If it does not come out with what we have said, then use an instrument made of lead in this form.
Let it be thicker than a rod for a little while, and at its end let there be a curvature, which enters into the throat with ease: and let it have its head elevated upwards.
And let it beware of touching its own epiglottis, so that coughing does not happen to it, and let it push the mouth, or bone, or the doctor himself insert it with his hand.
But what the weak person inserts, this is better: on account of his knowledge of the place of the attached thing: and he pushes downwards, or pulls his hand with the instrument upwards, all that according to the quantity which is prepared for him, until it comes out.
Of the extraction of bloodsuckers.
Chapter 39.
When the bloodsucker is treated with what I have said in the division on curation with medicines, and it does not come out, then examine the throat of the weak person before the sun, or at the sun after you press his tongue with the instrument that I have described to you.
If your sight then falls upon the bloodsucker, extract it with a small hook, or with dexterous fingers.
What if it is not possible with them: then take a curved tube, and insert it into the throat of the weak person up to the throat; afterwards insert into the curvature of the tube ignited iron with fire. Do that many times: and let the weak person abstain from water for the whole day.
Then let him take a full pitcher of cold water, and open his mouth in it: and let him rinse his mouth with it, and not swallow anything of it: and let him move the water one hour after another with his hand. For a leech sticks to it when it feels the water.
If however it does not come out with what we have described, then fumigate the weak person with bugs, or with rue with an instrument that I described in the fumigation of the ulcer.
Do that many times, because it itself will fall out.
And the method of operation in fumigation is, that you take a pot, in which there are hot coals with fire: and let the pot be covered with a cover, in whose middle there is a hole: and let the extremity of the instrument be placed in that hole.
Then throw in the fumigation, and let the weak person put his mouth on the extremity of the tube, and let him close his mouth, so that the fumigation does not come out, until you know that the fumigation has already reached the leech. For the leech will immediately fall out.
If it does not fall out, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the weak person endure thirst, and eat salty things, and garlic, and not drink water.
It is necessary therefore that it comes out with this treatment.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a leech is extracted from the throat, when it falls on the same uvula.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that it has this curvature which is introduced into the throat. And its extremities are similar to the mouth of a bird, which is called a cicoria, in which there is roughness of a file: when it grasps something, it does not release it at all.
On the opening of abscesses, and their cutting, and treatment in general.
Chapter 40.
There are many species of abscesses, of many modes, according to which the treatment of them comes, namely one and one in this book: and they are diverse in the perforation of them, and the cutting of them from two modes.
Of which one is of the species of abscess by itself, and that which it comprehends from humours.
And the second mode is on account of the places, in which it occurs in the body.
For an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the anus: and an abscess occurring in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in the joints: and each of them has its own judgment from the operation.
And of the abscesses there are some which ought not to be perforated, unless after the maturation of the pus in them and its completion: and of them there are, which ought to be perforated, while they are raw, and do not mature according to the completion.
As abscesses that are near the joints.
For when an abscess occurs near a joint, and its existence is prolonged, until it putrefies what is in the area around it, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint become corrupted: which is a cause more proximate to the contraction of that limb.
Or it is an abscess near a principal member: for if you delay the perforation of it, until it matures, it will harm either it or through that principal member.
It is next to the anus, perforate it if it is immature: and it is not suitable to perforate a crude aposteme, not fully digested, unless it is next to the anus, so that it does not putrefy the depth, and reaches into the interior of the anus, and becomes a fistula, or becomes in its termination what will not heal.
It is necessary that you know the time of perforation of the aposteme, which has matured according to its completion: and it is at the sedation of pain, recession of fever, and diminution of redness, and pulsation, and acuteness of the head of the aposteme: and other signs.
And it is necessary that you make the perforation in the lowest place of the aposteme, and of the most prominent elevation: and let the perforation be going in the length of the body, if the apostemes are in the parts of the hands and feet, and places of the shoulders, and tendons, and nerves, and arteries, and at last in all equal places, to which there is not a reflection.
In those however that reflect, let it go with the perforation according to that membrane.
When indeed the aposteme is in fleshy places: then it is better that you omit its perforation, until its digestion is confirmed, as we have said according to its completion.
For if you perforate it before that, the course of the pus is prolonged from it, and there is much filth and foulness, and perhaps the lips of it will become hardened, and its depth.
And some apostemes are perforated according to the width of the body by necessity: or according to that which the membrane needs.
And it is necessary that you administer to small apostemes a small perforation, and to large apostemes a large perforation: or many sections by the similarity of the size of the aposteme.
And there are of the apostemes, in which it is necessary that the skin be concave: and incised when already made like a cloth, and made at the termination, which already is dead: as that which happens to many, as to the dubre, and buboes.
And of them are those which are cut with a section having three angles. And of them is one, from which an incision is made like the figure of a myrtle leaf.
Like a groin abscess.
And of them are those in which is administered a round incision, and a lunar incision: and similar to them from the incisions.
That one indeed which has no head, as are lenial superficial abscesses, ought to be pierced by a superficial perforation, or merely a plane.
And it is necessary, when the abscess is thick, and has already collected much pus, and you pierce it, that you not press and extract all the pus at that hour: but rather extract a part of it from it. Then dress the abscess until another day. Then extract something of the pus again.
Do that many times according to the degree, until it comes out: especially if he is weak of strength, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or a decrepit old man: for the animal spirit is often dissolved with the exit of pus: and perhaps the weak person dies, and you do not perceive.
Beware therefore this chapter with great caution.
And after your perforation, by which you pierce these abscesses, it is necessary that you clean the wound: and take care. If therefore the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision, then administer dressings made from linen, or old cloth. And if the abscess is large, and there are many openings of the incision, it is necessary then that you insert a dressing into every opening, until they reach the bottom.
And if you have cut away a part of the skin in the abscess, or have hollowed it out around the edges: then it is necessary that you fill it with old cloth, or as when cloth is woven, or wool is shorn from flax without moisture: and bind it until the third day.
Then apply it, and treat it with what is suitable from the ointment, until it is healed.
And if a flow of blood occurs during your operation, then administer cold water and vinegar, after which the cloth is soaked in it: and you put it on the place of the flow of blood many times.
And if the flow of blood persists, then it is necessary that you administer a treatment, and cure, and powders, which I have described in many places in this our book, and from division.
And if it is in the time of winter, and the place of the abscess is of many nerves: then it is necessary that you pour in powders with wine, and hot oil: and you put them on the places.
And if it is in summer, and the places are fleshy: then it is necessary that you put powders soaked in water, and oil, or wine and oil, and all of it cold. Until when it will be the third day, as we have said, then it is necessary that you loosen the abscess, and wipe it clean, and administer in its cure what is suitable for it, until it is healed.
This is therefore what is necessary from the knowledge of the cure of abscesses in general.
Following the path of division, I have already spoken of every abscess, in what way it is a route to its cure, chapter by chapter, individually.
Chapter 41.
Of the incision of abscesses that occur on the skin of the head.
There occur on the skin of the head small abscesses, and they are of the species of Knots, which are contained in sacs, which are attached to them: as if they themselves were the eggs of a hen.
And there are many species of them.
Some of them are fatty: and some of them contain a humidity similar to slime: and some of them contain a humidity similar to decayed flesh and pus: and similar things, and some of them are hard stony.
And in the cutting of all of them, and their extraction, there is no fear, provided that at the time of their cutting it does not cause a contradictory condition of the arteries.
And the operation in the cutting of them is, that you should try it with an instrument, whose form will be described later, which is called Intromissum, until you know what it contains.
For if it is that which contains moisture, then dry it by a cut in the length of the superficial cut: according to this form.
And let the cutting begin from line b to line g, when the moisture is evacuated, then scratch out the cyst which contains that moisture, and do not leave any of it behind.
For it returns many times, if anything of it remains.
Then immerse the stump in Egyptian ointment, if it is present: and if not, in water and salt: and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day. For it eats away what remains of the cyst.
Then repeat over the stump with the Egyptian ointment a second and third time, and if you apply it to it, until you are confident that nothing of the cyst remains, then treat the wound with ointment, until it is healed.
What if the apostema contains a fatty knot: then cut it according to this form, having the shape of a cross: and apply poultices between the lips of the incision, and excise it from every side: and show your skill in the extraction of the tunic which contains it.
If however the artery makes a contrary condition for you, then do that which I will tell you afterwards.
And do similarly in the case of a stone, from cutting and cure of the same, according to what we have said.
And the cutting over a stone apostema is easier: since there is little blood and moisture. And sometimes there are certain apostemata which are on the head in certain men, with which there is no humidity inside.
Which is: that I cut into an apostema on the head of an old woman, and I found the apostema like a solid and hard stone, rough, white, which could not be broken: and if someone were to strike it by throwing something, it would wound it.
In other accidental apostemata on the head, besides these, namely the apostemata that occur on the heads of children, and at the roots of the ears, the section of all of them should be a superficial section.
Or make a perforation of them always from the lower part of them, so that the flow of blood may be alleviated to the lower parts.
Then treat it with what is suitable for it from the treatment.
Of the cutting of strphulons on the neck.
Chapter 42.
These apostemata often occur on the neck and under the armpits and in the groin: and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And above those which occur on the neck, there are one or many: and they generate from among themselves: and every Strophula of them is within its own proper tunic, as it is with knots and apostemata of the head, as we have narrated.
And there are many kinds of these Strophulons. And some of them are stony: and some of them contain humidity: and some of them are malignant, which do not obey treatment.
In that one then which of them you see of good disposition to the touch, whose manifest proximity is to the color of the skin, and it moves in every part, and it is not attached with the nerves of the neck, nor with the gullet, nor with the artery, nor is it deep: it should be that you cut it with a superficial section from the upper part to the lower part of the body along the line, and along the line, and excavate it from every part: and extend the two lips of the skin with a hook, or with many hooks, if you need it, as we have said in the apostemata of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is above the caution, that the vein or nerve is not cut.
And let the spatula be not sharp, lest you add your hand with the incision, or disturb the weak part, and cut what is not necessary.
If you cut the vein, or artery, it prohibits you from operation, then put on the wound zegi powder, or some powders that staunch blood, and bind the wound, and leave it, until the sharpness of the aposteme quiets, and the wound softens: or it begins to putrefy.
For blood is staunched by a tunic of bandage, return therefore to your operation, until you complete it.
Then inquire with your index finger, if anything remains of the small scales: therefore cut them, and clean them.
What if it is in the root of the Strophula a large vein: then it is necessary that you do not cut that Strophula from its root: moreover it is necessary that you tie it with a double strong thread, and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old cotton, which you have already steeped in Egyptian ointment. Then take care of it.
If the whole Strophula is cut, then it is necessary that you bring together the two lips of the wound: and sew them themselves immediately, after you see that no excess remains.
If you see that excess remains because of the size of the Strophula, then it is necessary that you proceed to cut its root, and extract it: and administer the suture that we mentioned.
And which of the Strophulas contains humidity, make a superficial hole in it: so that the place of its maturation appears to you: and put the hole from that which follows below the body, as we said.
Then administer a dressing with ointment after the perforation, and similar things to it, so that it corrodes what remains from the corruption, until when you see the wound is already clean: then treat it with a generating ointment of flesh, until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
Of the cutting of the epiglottis from an abscess that happens inside the throat.
The ancients spoke of this cutting on the epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has done it: and this is the writing of their speech.
However, in those who have a strangled or constricted condition, it is necessary that the cutting of the epiglottis be avoided: since no benefit comes from it: because all the veins and lungs are weak.
In those indeed who have a hot abscess in the mouth or throat or two tonsils, when there is no illness in the canal, it is necessary to use the cutting of the epiglottis, to flee from the destruction which comes from the suffocation.
It is necessary therefore when we want that, to cut the epiglottis under three circles of the epiglottis, or four, a small cutting in width, in what is between two circles in quantity, so that it is a cutting in the sifac, not in the cartilage: and this place is suitable for cutting, since it is deprived of flesh, and the blood vessels are far from it.
For if the doctor is fearful, then it is necessary that he stretch the skin of the throat with a hook. Then let him pierce the skin, until when he reaches the canal: let him avoid the blood vessels, if he sees any of them.
Then let him cut the sifac which we have described: and it is indicated above the cutting of the canal from the phlegm which comes out of it, with that which is disrupted, and from the separation of the voice.
And leave the open wound for some time.
When the hour arrives in which the prostration is feared, bring together the two lips of the wound of the skin, and suture it by itself without cartilage.
Then administer medicines that make flesh grow, until it is healed.
Said Albucasis. The summary of this discourse which we have narrated is not an exposition, except when they see that one of these apostems has already strangled the throat of the infirm, and the infirm has been lifted up from death, and his breath has been intercepted: they proceed to cut the Epiglottis, so that the infirm may breathe over the place of the wound by some breathing, and be saved from death: and for this reason they have ordered to leave the wound open, until the malignity of the disease is consumed, and its malignity is three days, or nearly; then therefore they have ordered to suture the wound, and to cure it, until it is healed.
And what I have seen with my own eyes is, that a servant girl took a knife, and thrust it into her throat, and cut with it a part of the canal of the lung.
And I was called to the cure of it, and I found her dying, as is the dying of one who is about to die.
I probed the wound, and I found blood that had come out from the wound a little. I was confident therefore that she had not cut the vein, nor the windpipe: and air came out from the wound.
I treated the wound therefore, and I cured it, until it was healed: and nothing happened to the servant girl, except hoarseness of voice: and she returned after days to her better disposition.
Therefore I say: that in the cutting of the Epiglottis there is no fear.
On the surgical incision of the throat hernia.
Chapter 44.
This condition called hernia of the throat is a large abscess, second to the color of the body, and it occurs frequently in women.
And it is in two modes: for it is either natural or it is accidental.
In the natural one certainly there is no swelling.
The accidental one, however, is in two modes: of which one is similar to fatty knots. And the other mode is similar to an abscess that forms from a knotting of the artery. And in it there is fear, and it is not fitting that an incision be made into it with iron, unless it is small: and you will test it, and investigate it with a probe. If therefore you find it similar to a fatty knot, and it is not suspended with any vein: then cut it, as knots are cut: and extract it with what it contains from the cyst, if it is in a cyst: and if not, then thoroughly search all of it. Then treat the place with what is proper for the cure, until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
On the surgical incision of the kinds of knots.
There are many kinds of knots, and I have already spoken about all their kinds in the division: and it is necessary that I describe here the difference between a Knot and an Exitura: since it causes doubt.
I say therefore, that when there is an Exitura, there is heat, and fever, and violent pains, until it is calmed by the boiling of the excess, and completed by putrefaction: then indeed the fever and heat subside.
With a Knot, however, there is no heat, nor fever, nor pains, and it contains them in cysts of the spleen, which is their proper sac, and it is second to the color of the body: and its shape is like a chickpea, and it forms like a bag, and is greater and lesser.
And they are divided into two types: either fatty or containing humidity. And the colors of humidity are many, according to what I said in the division.
It is necessary therefore, when you approach the cure of a Knot, that you examine it, and investigate it first with an instrument which is called Intromissum, according to which its form will appear in the chapter which is after this.
And the method of inquiring about abscesses and all Knots is that you take this instrument and insert it into the most humid place which you find in the abscess: and you move your fingers with it gradually, until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the skin. Then you put your hand last according to the measure of the magnitude of the abscess.
Then extract the Intromissum, and consider what goes out in its track.
If therefore a flowing humidity comes out, whatever the color may be: then dry it with a superficial incision, according to what I said about the remaining abscesses.
And if humidity does not come out in the track of the Intromissum, then know that it is fatty: dry therefore above it with an incision having the form of a cross, according to this form, as I have taught you: and suspend it with hooks, and rub away the skin from every side with ease: and keep a plaster if you can over it, so that you extract it healthy with the Knot.
If however the plaster breaks apart during the operation, and it is not possible to extract it healthy, for this often happens, then extract it by force, until nothing of it remains.
For if a little or a lot of it remains, the Knot returns according to the majority.
If it overcomes you and a little of it remains: then fill the wound at the completion of your task with certain corrosive powders: and bind the wound, and put on top of it what sits on the abscess, a warm application, and cure it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
If then it is a large Knot, then sew its two lips, and cure it with that which brings flesh.
If then you have a contrary vein, pulsating or not pulsating: then press, and fill the place of the zegi with a plaster, and keep it tight for two days, or three, until the wound putrefies, and the ebullition of blood subsides: then return to cut that which remains of the Knot.
Chapter 46.
Of the forms of instruments for cutting and boring.
These are the forms of the Introductors, and they are of three kinds.
For there is one large, and one medium, and one small among them.
Let them be made from iron of a quadrangular shape with well-finished extremities, so that they hold the entrance in the scabet.
And these are the forms of the Experimenta, which is to say, the perforators: and one of them is called a Beris, that is, a perforator: and they are of three kinds.
For there is one large, and one medium, and one small among them.
They are adapted to investigate through them the abscesses, and the exit wounds, and the fistulas, and the buboes from that which is between them of bones.
And others are made round, smooth, and blunt, like large needles with which they sew the sutures, namely of citrus, or of copper, or of iron, or of silver.
And the best of them are those that are made from citrus.
And the Experimenta are also made from black lead, which are adapted to probe through them the fistulas, of which the concavities are reflections, so that they reflect with their bluntness along with those reflections.
And they are again of three kinds. For there are some of them which are long, some which are medium, and some which are short, according to the quantity of the width of the fistula and its stricture.
The forms of ulcers, and there are many species of them.
For some of these are simple, namely those which have one treatment, and they are of three species, as you see, Great, Middle, and Small.
And of them there are hidden ulcers, and they are of three species.
And of them there are ulcers having two treatments, and they are of three species: and of them there are ulcers having three treatments, and they are of three species: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the forms of surgical scrapers, with which they cut and excise knots and apostemes: and they are of three species: for some of them are great, middle, and small.
Their extremities with which a section is made are acute, the other extremities are not acute: and they are not placed thus, except so that with them there is made a juncture in the excision of knots in fear of cutting the vein or nerve: and so that the weak one may be healed by it, and find tranquility for a time from the burning which it found at the excision of an aposteme.
They are made of brass, and are similar to a razor, with which is made a scrape: and on the flat extremity there is a hidden point of the spatula: and it scrapes towards the interior, and towards the exterior when you wish, as you see.
The form of the spatulas which are hidden between the fingers for the perforation of apostemes: and the weak ones do not perceive them: and they are of three species, great, middle, and small.
The forms of the cauteries with which the flow of blood is stopped: and they are of three species, great, middle, and small.
Let them be made of brass or bronze, round, of a moderate length, as you see: and let them be subtle.
And it is necessary that these species of Ventsarum are with you, both large and small, so that you can cut blood quickly when necessary, and then when medicine is not present to you.
However, you should not use them for cutting blood in every part of the body, and you should not use them except in fleshy places: such as the arms, thighs, and hips, and arms, breasts, belly, groin, and similar fleshy and moist ones.
And sometimes small instruments are made from them similar to the blades of lancets, according to their shape: with which blood is cut when it flows from the place of phlebotomy, or at the cutting of a vein, or artery: and sometimes they are made round according to this shape.
Chapter 47.
On the treatment of breasts in men that resemble women's breasts.
Sometimes the breasts of certain men become inflamed with an accumulation of fluid when they begin to sleep, until they resemble women's breasts, and remain as foul abscesses.
He who abhors this, it is necessary that he cut above the breasts in a lunar section, according to this shape from line b to line g, then scratch out all the fat. Afterwards fill the wound with incarnative medicine. Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture, and treat it, until it heals.
If the breast however declines to the lower parts and becomes soft because of its size, as it happens with women: then it is necessary that two sections similar to the lunar figure be found on its upper sides, each of which is connected to the other at their ends, until there is a larger line containing a smaller one, according to this shape from line b to line g. Afterwards scratch out the skin which is between the two lips, and remove the fat, and apply what we said about sutures and powders, which is necessary for the cure, until it heals.
If you do not close the incision of what you need to cut, because of the discomfort of the weak or because of the flow of blood: then you should fill the wound with a submersed corrosive ointment, and leave it, until it corrodes what remains of the fat. Then cure it, until it is healed.
On the perforation of a postilence, which happens under the nipple.
Chapter 48.
These postilences that happen under the nipple, are of the kinds of hard Strophulatum, which contain cysts, and of them are those which contain humors.
Which therefore are of those which contain humors, it is necessary that they be cut with a lunar section, according to this form, from line b. to line g., when therefore the whole thing that is in the postilence is evacuated of blood, then fill it with old oil, and leave it until another day.
Then let the plaster be removed, and cured with an ointment suitable to it, until it is healed.
What if the postilence was of the kind of nodes of Strophulatum, then cut above it, as we have said in the section on Strophuluses equally.
If however the wound extends to this that it emits pus, then administer to it a cautery, according to what we have said in what has been previously mentioned.
Chapter 49.
On the cutting of a postilence which happens because of an artery and a vein.
When an artery is disrupted and the skin which is above it is consolidated, then very often a postilence happens from it: and similarly it happens to veins also, so that a postilence and inflammation happen.
And the signs by which it is known that a postilence and inflammation are because of an artery and a vein, are: since when a postilence is because of an artery, it is long, gathered in the depth of the body: and when you press on the postilence with your fingers, you feel as if there were a hissing.
And that which is on account of the Vein, is round in the visible body.
And the cutting above this abscess is an error, and especially that which is under the armpits, and the groin, and the neck, and in many places of the body: and it is a great fault. It is necessary therefore that the care of it should be withdrawn from iron, and what of them is also in the extremities, or in the head, it is necessary that it should be withdrawn.
On that therefore which of them is from the inflation of the opening of the artery, make an incision in the skin in length. Then open the incision with forceps, afterwards the artery: and free it from its coats, until it is exposed.
Then insert under it a needle, and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie the artery with double thread in two places, according to that which I have taught you in the extraction of the two arteries which are in the temples.
Then prick the place, which is between the two ligaments with a spatula, until all the blood that is in it comes out, and the abscess is resolved.
Then administer a cure that generates pus, until the ligaments fall off: afterwards treat it with ointments suitable to it, until it is healed.
What if the abscess is on account of a section of the vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand what is possible for you from the abscess with the skin. Then insert a needle under the place which you held with your hand, and make it penetrate: and let there be in it double thread, until you draw it out from the other side. Then tie the abscess with a good ligature: according to that which I said to you in the ligature of the vein, from two parts with two extremities of thread.
If however you wish to extract the thread, then insert another needle with a different thread under the entire suture at the site of the incision of the wound: press, and tie your threads in four places.
Then cut the suture in the middle of itself, until when what is in it comes out: then cut away the excess skin, and release what has been tied from it.
Then put on top of it a poultice which you have steeped in wine and oil. Then administer the treatment, which is done with wraps and ointments, until it heals.
Chapter L (or 50)
Of the suture that happens from the twisting of nerves.
Just as a suture happens in an artery and a vein, similarly a suture happens in nerves: which happens to them from a blow, or happens from excessive labor, and similar things: and it happens most often in the places of the joints, or heel, and in every place where the joints move: and it is a serious suture.
Its color resembles the rest of the body, and it is in many dispositions without pain: and when it is tied with a bandage, it feels in it a weakness similar to numbness.
And it is not a suture gathered in the depth of the body, but rather it is under the skin, and it moves to every part, and it does not proceed to the front, nor forward, nor backward to the rear.
What therefore is in the joints, does not need to be treated with iron. Perhaps indeed a contraction happens.
In that which is on the head, or on the forehead, cut the skin with a spatula. If therefore it is a small suture, then hold it with forceps, and cut it off from the root.
And if it is large, then hang it with a hook, and scratch it: then remove it, and gather the wound with a suture: and treat it, until it heals.
Chapter 51.
On the cutting of an abscess that happens in the belly.
It happens to certain men many times in their bellies and in the rest of their bodies, these things that are called Fungal infections, on account of their similarity with mushrooms.
Of which the root is subtle, and their head is thick, and their lips are inverted. And there is one of them that is small: and there is one of them that is greatly enlarged.
And I have indeed already seen a man, and in his belly there were two fungal growths, which were more similar to mushrooms than the others: there was no difference between them, they were white, with small roots, and their lips were inverted, and they oozed, and moisture flowed out of them always.
I cut them out, and I found that one of them weighed about 9 ounces and the other about 6 ounces. And the operation for cutting them out is, that you should consider then, if the patient is weak and the color of the fungal growth is white, moist, with a subtle root: then cut it out with a broad spatula, and let cauterizing irons be at hand in the fire.
For often much blood is expelled during the cutting of it: therefore if the blood overcomes you, then cauterize it.
If you see that the patient is weak, fearful and timid of the cutting with iron, then take a thread made from lead, and tie it around the tumor: whose method is what we have said, and leave it on for two days.
Then apply to the constriction a lead plaster. Do not then cease to tie on the lead, as many times as you press on the fungal growth, until it is cut off and falls away by itself without effort.
If the fungal growth is thick-rooted, then there is something to fear and be anxious about in its cutting: especially if it is in the belly.
It is possible to cut through the middle of it. Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then treat it, until the wound is healed.
And beware that you do not cut into the blackened part which is of a dark color, of little sensation, and foul appearance: for it is a cancerous aposteme: and I call it with recollection of Cancer after this.
Chapter 52.
On the treatment of the navel's swelling.
The swelling of the navel occurs from many causes. Or from a tear of the diaphragm, which is above the belly: and there comes out from it either gut or intestine, according to what happens in the remainder of the rupture. Or from blood that flows from a vein, or from an artery, according to what has been previously mentioned. Or from flatulence constricted in it.
If however it is on account of a tear of the diaphragm, and the exit of the gut: then it is a color of an apostema similar to the color of the body: and it is soft, without pain: and it appears in different situations. And if it is on account of the protrusion of the intestine, then its situation is with that which we have described, of more vehement diversity. When therefore you press it with your fingers, it is hidden: then it returns, and perhaps there is a noise with it: and it is greatly increased at the entrance of the bath, and vehement labor.
If indeed it is on account of moisture, it is soft, then it is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor does it increase.
For if it is on account of blood, then with these signs an apostema appears like a blackness from the tumor.
If however it is on account of flesh that has grown: then it is a grave, thick, hard apostema, and it is shaped according to one quantity.
If the swelling is due to obesity, then the touch of it is gentle.
And the operation on it is, that you should consider then, if the prominence of the navel is due to blood of the Artery, or Vein, or obesity: then it is necessary that you prohibit the cure of it. For there is fear and terror in it, as I have taught you in the chapter, in which I said that apostema is due to the artery and vein.
Which if the prominence of the navel is due to the intestine or the hernia: then it is necessary that you order the weak person, so that he may hold his breath, and stand standing upright. Then mark with a cauterizing agent around the entire navel. Afterwards order him, that he should be raised up between your hands on his back. Then go around with a wide spatula in the area of the navel above the place that you marked with cauterizing agent. Afterwards extend the middle of the apostema upwards, with a large hook. Then tie it around the strong part with a thread, or with a string of firm silk binding: and let the knot of the binding be secure.
Then open the middle of the apostema that is extended above the binding. If therefore you have found it already caught by the binding: then extract the secured one, and insert into it your index finger, and push the intestine to the interior of the belly: and if you have found a hernia, then take it with a hook, and cut off its excess.
If on the other hand the artery or vein is opposite to you: then go around it much. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and insert into them two strong threads: and insert the two needles around the area that you made around the apostema in the shape of a cross, which you make penetrate.
Then tie the thread around the abscess in four places above the needle, and if it is necessary, remove the needle, and leave the place until the rotten flesh falls off by itself, or cut it when it becomes subtle and rots.
Then treat it with what is suitable from ointments, until it is healed.
What if there is a protrusion of the navel from the flesh, either from it or from moisture: then it is necessary to concave the abscess, as we have said. And extract the blood, or moisture, which you find in it.
Then treat it with what solidifies the place.
Chapter 53.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have already spoken about the division of the species of cancer, and how it is the way to cure it with medicines: and what is to be avoided in its cure with iron, so that it does not become an ulcer: and we have spoken about cancer generated in the matrix, and the caution in its cure.
And the ancients said that when cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, such as cancer which is in the breasts, or in the hip, and similar members located for extraction of all of it, especially when it is beginning and small.
When, however, it becomes old and is large, it is not necessary to approach it.
For I myself was not able to heal anyone from it, nor have I seen another before me who has approached that.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is to precede and solve the weak part with black bile several times. Then perform bloodletting on it if you find it with a clear fullness.
Then prepare the weak part for preparation, in which it will be treated with an operation.
Then throw onto the cancer ointments that are suitable for it. After that, press it from all sides with the skin, according to the inspection, until nothing of its roots remains: and let the blood flow, and do not cut it quickly, but rather express the place, and wipe away all the gross blood with your hand, or with any instrument that is possible there.
If however the flow of gross blood causes a contrary condition for you from the incision of an artery or a vein, then cauterize the vein until the blood is cut off. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it heals.
On the treatment of Dropsy.
Chapter 44. We will now describe in the division of the species of Dropsy, and how the collection of water occurs: and the signs of each species, and its cure with medicines.
And that which is treated with iron, is not except the single species of ascites: and it does not approach with iron the species of tympanites: nor the species of hypozarca inwardly: for that is mortal.
When you have already treated this species of Dropsy, tympanites, with medicines, and your treatment has not been effective: then consider. For if weakness has already befallen the patient, or if there is another infirmity with it besides Dropsy: such as if there is with it a cough, or a loosening of the stomach, and similar things: then beware lest you treat him with iron: for that is to be feared.
If however you see the weak person of overflowing strength, and there is no sickness with it besides Dropsy alone: and he is neither a child nor an old man: then the method of operation on him is, that you should lift up the weak person standing between your hands, and the servant behind him presses his stomach with his hand, pushing the water towards the lower part of the chest.
Then take a spiney spongy  bone, according to this shape. It has two sharp parts, is long, and the extremity is pointed like a spine. But let there be in it something of shortness little by little, so that you do not pass through with it during the operation to the intestine, and harm it.
Then consider. For if there is a generation of dropsy from the intestines, then it is necessary that you should lengthen the incision by the quantity of three fingers from the navel downwards directly above the breast.
If indeed the generation of dropsy is because of disease of the liver, then let your incision be to the left from the navel by three fingers.
And if the generation of it is because of the spleen, then let the incision be on the right side: and it is not necessary that the incision be on the side above which the weak person wishes to lie, so that the superfluities do not flow to that weak place.
Then pierce the skin entirely with an instrument. Then insert into that incision, and raise your hand with the spiney instrument between the skin, and scrape, as if you were exfoliating it: and let the quantity which is scraped be the quantity of a nail, or around that.
Then scrape and pierce until the spiney instrument reaches the empty place: and it is the place of the water. Then extract it, and insert into the opening an instrument, which has this form. And it is similar to a cannula: made from silver, or copper, or gold, polished: which has in its lower part a small opening, and on its sides are three openings, two on one side and one on the other side: and its edge is cut, according to the manner of cutting a reed, as you see: on its upper end is a handle.
When the instrument penetrates the water, then it itself descends immediately upon the instrument, so it evacuates from the water half of its quantity.
For if you evacuate from it more than is necessary, then the weak person will perhaps die immediately by resolution of its animal spirit, or a syncope will befall him which approaches death.
Evacuate from it according to the quantity of its virtue, and according to what the dispositions of the weak person signify to you from the virtue of his pulse, and from its goodness.
Then withdraw the instrument, and retain the water.
Which is, that it is retained immediately because of the skin which retains the orifice that is above the fistula: about which I have told you, so that you should perforate it according to that method.
Then repeat the instrument on the second day, if you see the weak person tolerate it: and also evacuate a small quantity from the water.
Do that for some days without fear, and guard yourself against error: until there does not remain from the water except a little.
If however you fear for the weak person, and you leave a large amount from the water: then treat it with burial in hot sand, and much sweat in a bath and sun, and wait upon thirst: and treat it with evacuating medicines, until it is healed: and cauterize above the stomach, and liver, and spleen, after the evacuation of the water: according to what I have told you in that which precedes.
Concerning the treatment of infants who are born with their urinary places not perforated.
Chapter 45.
When any infant goes out from the womb of its mother, and the head of its bladder is not perforated, or the orifice is constricted, or is not in its proper place.
Therefore it is necessary to help with the perforation of it immediately when it is born with a very fine spatula, according to this method.
Then put in the hole a subtle key made of lead, and tie it: and hold it for three days, or four.
When the bladder is raised to emit urine, let it be removed, and micturated. Then reduce it.
And if you do not put lead, then it is possible that the urine which falls on the place will not permit it to close.
He, however, whose hole is narrow, is treated with lead, as we have said, for many days, until it is widened.
Those, however, in whom the hole is not in its proper place, are wounded. That is, because among those who are born with it, the hole is at the end of the head of the penis: wherefore he cannot urinate to the front, unless he lifts the penis with his hand to the upper parts. And there is not for him generation, because the sperm cannot pass to the womb according to nature: and it is a foul disease.
And the method of treatment for that is, that the weak one should be placed on his back. Then extend the head of his penis with your left hand by forceful extension, and cut the head of the head of the penis from the place of the priap, with a needle or a sharp spatula: just as is the cutting of a reed, or just as you scrape something, so that the middle of it comes to be like the head of the penis: and so that the hole falls in the middle according to what is proper.
And beware for yourself during the operation of a flow of blood. For it has happened many times. Apply to him therefore that which stops the blood, and cure the wound, until it is healed.
Of Albucasis, concerning what happens to the foreskin and head of the penis, and corruption, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter 6
These Albather often occur on a branch, and it is an eminence of foul flesh.
And some of them are malignant, and some are not malignant.
You should therefore suspend the non-malignant one with a fine hook, and cut it off, until you cleanse it entirely. Then put on it a plaster soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it afterwards with dyafinicon ointment, until it is healed.
Which if the Albather is malignant, of foul color, then you should administer to it a cautery after the incision of it and its rasping.
If the Albather is on the foreskin of an uncircumcised man, and part of the Albather is under the foreskin, and part of it is outside: then you should remove the Albather that is inside first, until when it has consolidated, then treat the outer one: for when you treat them both at the same time, the foreskin does not heal unless it becomes perforated.
And they also occur on the testicles, and a blackening and corruption of the foreskin. You should therefore burn away all that has already become blackened, and what you want to be corrupted, or is already corrupted.
Then apply on it afterwards a mixture made with honey that has been mixed with the flour of toasted coriander seeds and crushed herbs.
Then treat it with the remainder of the treatment, until it is healed.
If a flow of blood occurs, then administer a cautery with lunar cautery, in this manner.
For cautery is useful for two conditions at the same time, namely for a flow of blood and for an ulcer when it becomes corrupted.
Which if the head of the branch is corroded and destroyed entirely in one hour: then you should insert into the wound of the penis a pipe made of lead, so that urine flows out through it.
In the adherence of the skin of the foreskin to the head of the virga, and this adherence does not occur except in one whose foreskin is healthy and does not require circumcision; and when adherence occurs due to a wound or an abscess, it is necessary to cauterize it with a blunt spatula until the ligament is dissolved and the head of the virga is freed from every part.
What is difficult to discern by feeling, then it is necessary to cauterize something from the head of the virga and from the foreskin. What is, that the foreskin, when it is subtle, is perhaps perforated on account of its subtlety quickly.
Then separate the foreskin and the head of the virga with a thin linen cloth, which has already been soaked in cold water, so that they do not adhere again.
Then it should be treated with astringent wine until it is consolidated.
Chapter 57.
On the purification of infants, and the cure of errors that happen to them by chance.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity, like other wounds: nevertheless since it is from our operation, our will, and its use is properly in children, it is necessary that we indicate in this operation the better method, and the easier way leading to health.
I say therefore, that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, since they do not make use of it in their laws, and it is not except what we have acquired through experimentation.
What is, that I have found the greater part of artisans and phlebotomists to use purification by circumcision with a razor, and with forceps; and they use cauterization, and a ligature with a thread, and an incision with a nail.
And I have already tried all these methods, and I did not find a better purification than with forceps and a ligature with a thread.
Because in cleansing with a razor, it often slips from the skin. Since the skin of the prepuce has two layers: therefore perhaps the upper skin is cut and the lower layer remains: it is forced to a cutting to cut the other, and future pain. And with the cleansing that is done by rubbing, that is, digitally, which barbers use, the cutting of the extremity of the member of the viril does not secure: since perhaps it enters into its opening.
In cleansing with the nail, perhaps the iron slips and corrupts your operation: and the skin of a child is brief by nature, and often they are born such that they do not need cleansing: and I have already seen it.
Cleansing with forceps and ligature with thread has shown me a good experiment: since the forceps are proportional to the incision: therefore, because the sharpness that is above is as the sharpness that is below. When therefore you press your hand, the two sharpnesses are proportioned, and they cut according to one operation at one time: and there is made a compression of similar figure, containing the skin of the member from every part, with which there does not occur error altogether.
And the method of the operation itself is, that you cause to extract, especially a child, if it is one of those who understands for a little while, what you do not do, except that you tie the thread on its member as much: and you leave it until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be glad in every way that is possible for you, from the fact that he receives his own understanding. Then make him stand between your hands prepared for the stature, and let him not be sitting: and hide the forceps in your sleeve, or under your foot, so that the child does not see them at all, nor any of the instruments.
Then insert your hand into its branch, and blow on the skin, and pull it upwards, until the head of the branch emerges. Then cleanse it from the filth that has accumulated in the squalor.
Then tie the marked place with a double thread. Afterwards tie below it for a short space a second ligature. Then hold with your index finger, and thumb, the place of the lower ligature with good retention, and cut between the two ligatures. Then lift the skin upwards quickly, and extract the head of the branch. Then allow the blood to flow gradually (for it is better) and put powder on top: and over the powder a cloth in which the yolk of an egg has been spread, mixed with fresh rose oil: and let it stay on until the next day: and treat it with the remainder of the treatment, until it is healed.
The shape of the lancet for cleansing is this.
Let the two extremities be dull, and let them be cutting: so that a branch of the nail does not protrude from them: and let the length of the two edges be equal to the length of two finger joints.
The error that occurs in cleansing is this: because perhaps the whole inner skin slips away, or a part of it at the incision.
Therefore it is necessary that you stretch it out with your finger before the cicatrization of the place: and cut it according to equality.
What if you cannot hold it with your finger, then extract it with a hook, and cut it.
If however three days have passed and the swelling that is under the branch remains, then let it be until the hot aposteme has settled, and remove it with ease, and cut it according to what has been prepared for you: and beware of the head of the branch. For it will not harm him.
Treatment is therefore with that which consolidates the wound from powders, which we have described in the treatise on powders: and if it cuts into the skin above the measure, and goes to the upper parts, it will not harm him either.
Treatment is therefore with that which we have said, until it is healed.
Chapter 58.
On the extraction of retained urine from the bladder.
The retention of urine in the bladder is caused by blockage made from a stone, or coagulated blood, or pus, or ingrown flesh, and similar things to them which we have spoken of in the methods of treatment in the division.
And if the urine does not flow, and you see that its retention is on account of a stone, which is already in the neck of the bladder: then it is necessary that he should be made to lean forward over his knees. Then let his feet be placed on his back, and let him stretch out his belly from the upper part of his back, and let him tightly hold his breath as much as is possible for him. It is pressed therefore with the neck of the bladder for the expulsion of the stone to the back: wherefore the urine is released.
What if it is not solved with that which we have said, and the thing is severe on the patient: then it is necessary that you administer its extraction with an instrument, which is called a syringe, whose form is this: Let it be of silver, and let it be subtle, thin, hollow like the quill of a feather in subtlety of a radius, long in likeness to one and a half palms: to which let there be a thin tube at its head.
And the method of exit of urine through it is, that you take a doubled thread, and tie at its end a thread or a cotton, with a good ligature: and insert the end of the thread into the lower part of the Syringe: and cut with forceps if something overflows from the thread, truly insert into the tube with vehemence, like an almond.
Then anoint the siringa with oil, or with butter, and the white of the egg: and make the weak sit on a seat, and embrocate the vesicle, and its own virga with moist oils, and olive oil, and warm water.
Then insert the siringa into the virga with ease, until it reaches to the root of the virga.
Then double it over itself at the part above the navel.
Then push the siringa into it, until it reaches to the bladder, and the weak person feels that it has already reached to the empty space: and this is not done according to this order, unless the passage, through which it goes, is where there is a curvature.
Then extend the thread with a woolen string little by little. For the catheter will follow the wool.
Then pull it out, and make the catheter run through the urine passage: and repeat the siringa, and do not cease to do that, until the bladder is emptied, and the weak person finds relief.
On the quality of cleansing the bladder with a Clyster.
Chapter 59.
When there happens to be an ulcer in the bladder, or blood congeals in it, or pus is confined in it, and one wants to distill waters and medicines into it: then let it be done with the instrument that is called a Clyster, and this is its form.
For it is closed, that is, stopped up. And in it there are three openings, two on one side and one on the other side, as you see.
And the concave place, in which is that which pushes, should be according to the quantity that fills it without addition, until when something is pulled with it, it attracts from the humidity: and when it is pushed, it expels afar: according to what makes the clyster, with which naphtha is thrown, that is, Greek fire on the hearth of Mars.
When I insert the instrument to project humidity into the bladder, I introduce the extremity of the catheter into the humidity, and pull with it that which pushes upwards, for humidity is pulled into the concavity of the catheter.
Then I introduce its extremity into the vein, according to that which we have narrated in the syringe. Then I push humidity with that which pushes it, for humidity immediately penetrates to the bladder, so that the weak person feels it.
The shape of a thin enema instrument by which the bladder is enemad.
Let it be made from silver or gold: whose head is higher like a small spoon, and below it a notch in which the thread falls.
Then take the bladder of a goat, and put in it humidity, with which you will enema the bladder. Then tie it between two notches with a strong ligature with double thread: and warm that humidity a little over the fire.
Then introduce the extremity of the enema instrument into the vein, afterwards press your hand over the bladder with humidity with a suitable pressure, until the weak person feels that the humidity has already penetrated to the bladder.
If however the bladder is not present to you, then take a piece of parchment, and make from it a circle, and pierce it throughout all its roundness. Then introduce a strong thread into those holes, as they are turned. Then gather the circle, as the rose is gathered: after which you put in it that which you use from humectants, and oils, and waters.
Then tie it on the instrument: and do with it as you have done with the bladder from expression with your hand, until the humidity penetrates to the bladder.
Chapter 60.
On the extraction of a Stone.
We have already spoken about the division of the species of Stone, and its treatment with medications, and I have said the difference between the Stone generated in the kidneys and bladder, and the signs of all that: and I have said the Stone in which the operation falls by hand and cutting: and it is a Stone generated in the bladder properly: and that one which is fixed on the staff: and I am narrating that with clear exposition and abbreviation.
I say therefore, that the Stone generated in the bladder, happens most to children.
And of its signs is that urine goes out from the bladder similar to water in its thinness: and appears in it white sand, and the weak man rubs his staff, and labors with it, and many times it hangs. Then it rises, and goes out with it the anus in many of them.
And the healing is easy for children, as long as they are up to fourteen years old. And it is difficult in the elderly. In youths, however, it is moderate, in that which is between the two: and of whom the Stone is larger, the treatment is easier: and if small, the care is the opposite.
When therefore we come to the treatment, it is necessary first to cleanse the weak one with a clyster, which will extract all the dung that is in his intestines: for it sometimes prevents the finding of the Stone during the examination.
Then let the weak one be taken by his feet and shaken, and moved downwards: so that the Stone descends to the depth of the bladder, or jumps from a high place several times.
Then make him sit between your hands prepared, and let his hands be under his hips, so that the whole bladder inclines downwards.
Then examine him, and feel him from the outside.
If therefore you feel a stone in the space, then immediately with the proper section over it.
What if it does not fall under your touch at all, then it is necessary that you wipe the index finger of your left hand with oil, if he is a weak boy: or the middle finger if he is a grown youth: and insert it into his anus, and feel for the stone until it stands under your finger: and turn it little by little to the neck of the bladder.
Then press on it with your finger, and push it to the outside against the place where you want the section: and instruct one assistant to press the bladder with his hand, and instruct another assistant to stretch the testicles with his right hand upwards: and with his other hand to remove the skin which is under the testicles on the part away from the place in which is the section.
Then insert a spatula blade, whose shape is this. And find in what is between the anus and testicles, and not in the middle, to the side of the left buttock: and let the section be over the stone, and your finger be in the anus: and let the section be transverse, so that it be exteriorly ample, and interiorly strict, according to the quantity that it is possible for the egress of the stone from it not being larger.
Perhaps indeed the finger which is in the anus compresses the stone at the section, and it goes out without difficulty.
And know that because of the stone there are angles and edges, for which its exit is made difficult on account of them.
And it is smooth, similar to a acorn, and round: and its exit is made easy.
Therefore for those which have angles and edges, add an incision.
What if it does not go out thus, then it is necessary that you grope over it: or bind over it with a device decently, whose extremity is like the crescent moon, which binds over the stone and does not depart from it: or that you insert under it an instrument subtle, of curved extremity.
If you cannot see on top of it, then enlarge the opening a little. What if anything of blood comes out from it, cut it off with a ligature.
If there is more than one stone in the bladder, then first push the largest to the mouth of the bladder. Then cut on top of it. Afterwards push a small one after that, and do likewise if there are more than two.
What if the stone is very large, then it is ignorance to try to cut out the large one itself. For it happens to the weak one of two things, either that he dies, or that there occurs to him a continuous dripping of urine: therefore, because the place is not consolidated at all.
Administer its expulsion, until it comes out: or use instruments in the fracture with forceps, until you extract it bit by bit.
When you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with resin, and aloe, and dragon's blood: and bind it, and put over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water: so that the hot aposteme may be assuaged.
Then let the weak person rest with his neck elevated, and do not loosen the bandage, until the third day. When you then loosen it, bathe the place with water, and a lot of oil.
Then treat it with the ointment called diaphenicum, and the ointment basilicum, until it is healed.
If however a hot aposteme should occur in the wound, with added corrosion, and similar things: such as if blood congeals in the bladder, and urine is prohibited from it: and it is recognized by the discharge of blood with urine. Therefore insert your finger into the wound, and extract that blood. For if it remains, it provokes the corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, and water, and salt: and apply all the species with that which is suitable from the treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary during the hours of treatment of the whole, that you bind two hips, and gather, the medicinal causes which are placed on the place.
What if the stone is small, and it is in the flesh of the rod, and it is fixed in it, and the exit of urine is prohibited: then treat it with what I will narrate before you proceed to the cutting. For many times indeed you will be exempt from the cutting through this treatment, for I have already proven that.
And there is, that you take a perforator made of iron, whose form this is, triangular pointedness, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take a thread, and tie it with that rod under the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then insert the iron perforator into the rod with ease, until it reaches the perforator to the stone itself: and turn the perforator with your hand on the stone little by little: and complete the perforation of it, until you make it penetrate from the other side.
For urine will be freed at once.
Then press your hand on that which remains of the stone from the external part of the rod. For it itself breaks, and comes out with urine, and the weak man is healed.
If however this treatment does not prepare itself for you, on account of something that prohibits it from that: then tie a thread below the stone, and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone in the same rod between the two ties. Then extract it. Afterwards loosen the ligament, and clean the congealed blood that forms in the wound.
And it is not necessary to tie the thread under the stone, except that it does not return to the vein; and another tie above, except that when the thread is loosened after the removal of the stone, the skin returns to its place, and covers the wound: and for that reason it is necessary for you, when you tie the upper thread, to elevate the skin upwards, so that it returns to your complement, and covers the wound, as we have said.
Chapter 61.
On the extraction of a stone from women.
A stone is rarely generated in women.
If however it happens to any of them that a stone forms, then the treatment is difficult, and it is forbidden because of many methods.
One of them is: that the woman is perhaps a virgin.
And the second is, that you do not find a woman who reveals herself to the physician, when she is chaste, or among those having husbands.
The third is, that you do not find a woman who knows well this art, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is the remoteness of the place of the stone: wherefore it requires a deep incision, and in that there is fear.
What if necessity calls for it, then it is necessary that you take a woman physician, knowing well: and she is rarely found.
If you truly keep them private, then seek a modest physician, subtle, and present a female midwife well taught in the matter of women, or a woman who has acquired a part of the art: make her present, and especially instruct her, that she do all that you prescribe to her from the examination concerning the stone.
At first what is, to examine if the woman is a virgin: then it is necessary that she insert a finger into her anus, and seek the stone.
If then he finds it, he constricts it under his finger, and then orders the midwife to insert her finger into the weak opening, and feels above the stone, after which she places her left hand on the bladder, and squeezes it with a good compression.
If then he finds the stone, then it is necessary that he gradually moves it from the orifice of the bladder to the lower parts with the greatest strength of his, until he reaches it at the root of the hip.
Then he cuts above it at the opposite of the middle of the opening at the root of the hip, from whichever side is prepared for him, and he feels the stone on that side: and his finger does not move away from the stone, constricting under it: and let the incision be small at first. Then he inserts the branch above that incision.
Then he inserts the branch above that incision. If then he feels the stone, then he adds to the incision a second cut proportional in quantity to that which the stone protrudes from it.
And know that there are many kinds of stones. For among them is the small and the large, the smooth and the rough, the long and the round with branches.
Know therefore its species, that it may be indicated to you by this above what you are going to do.
If then the blood overcomes you, then add to the place of the injury a pound of flour, and hold it for one hour, until the blood is stanched.
Then return to your operation, until the stone comes out: and do, that you prepare with yourself the instruments which I have spoken of in the extraction of the stone from the bladder, so that you may assist by them in your operation.
For if the flow of blood overcomes you, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from an artery which has been cut: then put powder on the place, and constrict it with a strict bandage: and do not touch it: and leave the stone, and do not extract it. For a weak woman will probably die. Then treat the wound.
When therefore a gathering of blood sits after some days, and the place putrefies, then return to your operation, until the stone goes out.
Chapter 62.
Of the cutting of the watery hernia.
A watery collection is not a hernia, unless it is an aggregation of humidity in the white membrane, which is under the skin of the testicle containing the egg: and it is named a membrane.
And sometimes it is in its own proper membrane, which nature has prepared for it in the part of the egg, until it purifies: which is another egg, and is the skin of the testicle between the white membrane, which we have spoken of: and that is not made except rarely.
