Then cut the ends of the threads, so that they do not injure the weak part by their rubbing: and put little pads made from cloth on all sides, which hold the ends of the threads: and leave them, until you know that the wound is already consolidated.
And this type of suture with needles is thus more suitable for small wounds. For one or two needles, or about that number, are sufficient for the suture of them.
The second common suture is to gather with the suture four ends: two ends of the skin, and two ends of the flesh in place of one, with a needle in which there is a thread wound equally in fineness and thickness.
Then when you penetrate with the needle these four ends, bring the needle back from the part where you began, so that the tied thread falls from the upper part of the wound: so that it is a suture, just like the suture of cloth which clothes are sewn with, and put between every suture the thickness of a small finger.
These men know this suture.
And with this suture I sewed up the wound that had occurred to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and there was a disruption of the wound larger than one palm, and about two palms of intestine had come out of the middle intestine, and there was a disruption in the middle of the belly.
I reduced it back after his intestine had stood outside the wound for 24 hours.
The wound was then consolidated in the space that it had been for around 15 days: and I treated it, and it healed: and he lived many years after that, living out his natural term.
And the physicians judged that it would not heal.
And it was a miracle that I did not treat it with an ointment. For I was in a place where no ointment could be found, nor any of the medicines. I placed therefore an old doublet over the wound, and I applied a frequent washing of it with honey water, until it was healed.
But I will describe two proper kinds of suture according to the narrative speech.
And it is that you begin with a sharp suture: and you insert the needle from the outer part to the inner part.
When the needle thus penetrates into the flesh, and into the torn apart flesh according to the length and width of the whole body: release the thread from the side where you inserted the needle, and make it penetrate from the inner part to the outer in the other side of the other flesh.
When you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate according to this same thing on the same flesh from the outer part to the inner: and release the thread which is in that side, and make the needle penetrate into the other side from the inner part to the outer: and make it penetrate with your penetration, which you make it penetrate in the thread of the flesh, which is in its part, until it penetrates into the whole thing.
Then begin also from this same side, and sew up the wound with the extremity that is from the suture on the other side, and extract the needle from the skin which it has penetrated.
Then bring it back to that skin, and sew up the extremity of the suture which is on the other side, with this extremity of the thread: and extract it from the skin which is on its own side, and make one knot, and another: and make that knot after the knot, until you have completed the whole thing according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is that you sew up the pelles like those who sew up skins: so that you put one knot on one side with one extremity of the skin: and so that you pass it to the other side, until you finish.
The second type of proper suture, which Galenus called.
This speech of his has been narrated as they sew them up according to a likeness, which some physicians do, so that they bring all the part to its corresponding part by nature: and apply the extremity of the thread to its other extremity, and the extremity of the needle to its other extremity. And that is done according to what I tell you.
It is necessary that you fix the needle in the extremity of the thread near to you from the outer part, and make it penetrate to the interior in that alone: and leave two extremities of the thread. Then turn the needle around, and make it penetrate from the outer part to the interior in the two extremities of the thread, namely on both sides.
Then turn both around, and make them penetrate from the interior to the exterior, into the two other extremities of the needle thread, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is a better suture than the common easy one, and it is a suture which recall has preceded.
This is therefore the treatise of Galen.
And they said certain things. The suture of the wound is reinforced with two needles. Which is, that you insert into them both one thread: and begin with the insertion of the needle, from that which is with you, and make it penetrate up to the other side, following the suture of those who sew the skins equally.
And you should know, that when a disruption is in the middle of the wound, then its suture is more difficult than that of the other places of the wound.
The judgment of the treatment of a wound, is the judgment of other wounds.
Which is, that when you treat a fresh wound with its own blood, before the air alters it, and you reduce the intestine, and sew it, and adapt it: then put on top an incarnative powder.
Which if the air has already altered the wound: then put on it certain ointments, which are present to you, until they make a pus: and the threads fall, and consolidate the flesh, and form a scar. Then treat it as is the treatment of other wounds, until it heals.
If however the medicines are not present to you, then put on it immediately, from which it begins to emit pus, old grease: and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it heals.
For you do not apply other treatments in many conditions, when the wounds are simple.
If however you fear that the wound communicates with the principal members in a lesion, then it is necessary that you soak a woolen thread in tempered oil of moderate warmth, or in rose oil: and put it around the places which are between the groin, and under the armpits.
Which if he feels pain, or stitches, or putrefaction in his intestines (for it often happens) then clysterize him with a warm black wine: especially if the putrefaction has already penetrated to the intestine, and an ulcer has been made which penetrates up to the perineum.
And you should know that what is from the thick intestine is of easier cure, and what is from them is from the subtle, of more difficult cure.
The intestine which is called the Jejunum does not receive a cure from any wound that occurs in it at all: and this is because of its multitude, which is from the veins, and their greatness, and because of the subtlety of its body, and proximity of its nature to the nerves.
If however what proceeds from the wound is feces, and you treat it freshly: reduce it according to your reduction, by which you reduce the intestines equally.
If indeed the space has passed beyond it, and already it has become green or black: then it is necessary that you constrict it with a thread above the place which has been discolored by it, so that there does not occur a flow of blood. For in feces there are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is beyond that ligature, and put two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside of it: so that the attraction of it may be alleviated by you, and the extraction of it in case of feces, and the emission of pus of the wound.
A reminder of the wound which occurs in the intestine.
When there occurs a disruption in the intestine and it is small: then it is possible that it be restored in certain men: because I have seen one who was wounded in his belly with a thrust of a lance, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound had become chronic, and a fistula was formed, and there came out from it an evacuation and flatulence.
I have put therefore a cure, until which I was not confident in the healing of it: nor did I cease to treat it subtly, until it was healed and the place was consolidated.
After I saw the place already consolidated, I feared for the weak, that which would happen to the weak happening a disease in its stomach: and there did not happen to it from that happening a disease. And it remained in its better dispositions, eating and drinking, and blowing, and it entered a bath, and was exercised in its diets.
And now certain experimenters have said, that when a wound happens in the intestine and is small: then it is necessary that it be bound in this manner. And it is, that you receive poultices of large heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and put a poultice one of them which has an open mouth over the two lips of the wound. When it thus takes hold on them, and closes its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres and is not loosened.
Then put another poultice near the first, and do not cease to do that with a poultice after a poultice according to the quantity of the whole wound.
Then reduce it, and heal its wound. For those heads remain attached in the intestine, until the intestine is healed and cured, and no harm happens to the weak one inwardly.
And now it is possible that the intestine be bound with a subtle thread: which is taken from the intestine of an animal, and attached to it after it has been thrust into a needle.
And it is, that you take the end of this thread from the intestine, and much flesh comes out. Then bind at its end a flax thread subtle and unwound. Then thrust that thread into the needle, and sew with it the intestine. Then reduce it to a closure.
And this method of sewing with poultices and intestine is not but according to the way of trust and hope.
One knows a wound that is great, ample, especially if it is in one of the slender intestines: then there is no ingenuity unless subtlety, nor is its healing at all.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids and fistulas.
Know that every wound or aposteme, when it becomes old and aged, and becomes an ulcer, and is not consolidated, and always discharges pus, and does not close: then it is universally called a fistula, in whichever member it may be, and we call it Zuchemen.
And a fistula is indeed, according to truth, a hardened, white, calloused nodulation: with which there is no pain, to which the concavity of a bird's wing is similar: and because of that some people call it Risati, that is, a feather.
And it is in certain hours humid, which discharges pus. And perhaps the humidity is discharged at certain hours. And sometimes this humidity is much, and is little: and it is coarse, and it is subtle.
And I have already stated in the division, then when I spoke of wounds, that every ulcer which is not healed, nor flesh grows in it, is not that except on account of one of nine causes.
Of which one is, or on account of the smallness of blood in the body: or on account of its bad quality: or because between it and above its edges there is hard flesh, preventing the growth of flesh: or because there is much filth and foulness: or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the material which is applied to it is of bad quality: or because the medicine is unsuitable for its cure: or because corruption falling in the region is a kind of pestilence, or by the property of the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta: in which there is difficult digestion of diseases, and delayed healing of apostemes by nature: or because in it there is one bone, or a number of bones.
And I have already shown all these causes and their signs, and their cures with medicines in the division, and it is necessary here that I make you know the cure of Fistula and Zuke, which is done by way of operation with the hand.
Know therefore, that Fistula occurs in all members of the body. For some of them are Fistulas that extend to the large veins and arteries, or nerves, or diaphragm, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to one of the ribs: or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to one of the composite joints of a composition: such as the hand, and foot: and some of them are which approach the proximity of the principal member, and similar things.
Which therefore of these Fistulas is according to this method, is from diseases of difficult healing, and does not receive cure, and the occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
What if it is from the Fistulas any of which the depth does not extend to these places that I spoke of: and you hope, and trust in its cure, and you use to extend to the knowledge of it, then take a probe made of bronze, or iron, if the Fistula tends according to straightness, and seek it with it.
If however there is tortuousness in the Fistula, then investigate it with a probe of refined lead. For lead, with the gentleness of its body, is lifted and bent around the tortuousness.
What if the Fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to investigate it with a probe. Probe therefore one of its openings from its own openings.
For the moisture that you probe it with, goes back to other openings, and flows out from them.
Then carefully inquire according to which method it is possible for you: to know what that is a mouth, or a nerve, and is the concavity of the Fistula far or near: or if it is one Fistula with many openings, and stand on all that, with the greatest strength of yours: with your questioning, which you question the infirm, and the invention of pain upon compression with your hand over the place, and similar signs and causes occurring to the apostema.
When you stand over all those stations of that place, then proceed to the cure according to your confidence: and it is, as you consider then, if there is a fistula appearing nearby or in a place far from the junction, or from a nerve, or an artery, or a vein, or one of the places which I mentioned to you: then cut the Fistula according to what was previously stated from my narration, and remove what is in it from filtration, and corrupted flesh, which grows in its opening from the flesh, or the callus, and similar things: and cure it, until it is healed.
If the fistula is of deep and distant profundity, and it is according to rectitude: then it is necessary that you cut it as deeply as is possible for you. Then clean it from all its corrupted flesh.
Then administer unbroken bandages in sharp medicines: and insert them up to the concavity of the fistula which you comprehended with the iron: do that with it several times, until the sharp medicine corrodes all that remained in the concavity of the fistula from corruption.
Then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh, until it is healed.
If it does not heal by that, then cauterize it according to what we have narrated.
When the cause of the fistula is an opening, and it is confirmed by you: then investigate it, and cut it according to what I have narrated to you: if nothing prohibits you from the vein, or nerve, or principal member, as we have said.
When an opening is detected by you, and there is something of corruption and blackness in it: then shave it, until all its corruption is removed. Then cure it with that which makes flesh on it, until it is healed.
If it does not heal, and it emits pus as it was doing: then know that you did not penetrate to the total eradication of the corruption.
Remove that which is above the wound second, and make a precise incision of it, and cleansing of the same with your utmost strength.
Then restore it. If then it heals, it is good: and if not, then you should know that the corruption is in the depth of the body, which you have not reached. There is therefore no ingenuity for you in it, except to entrust it to nature.
What if the small mouth is corrupted, and it is possible for you, draw it back with subtle forceps that convene to it.
If however there are many bones, then make a precise attraction of all of them, and do not leave any of them behind with your strength.
If indeed anything among the humors makes a contrary to you, and there is no fear of incision or error: then incise those humors and flesh, if they are there, and free the bones.
If indeed there is not for you preparation to remove the bones from your hour, then put on the wound what will rot it: and leave it, until it rots what is in the vicinity of those bones from flesh and humors.
And guard the wound so that it does not consolidate. And let the section which you cut in your operation be constrained. Below put on it, if you wish, a plaster submerged in sulfur beaten with oil: or a plaster submerged in Egyptian ointment, or some of the green ointments.
For when those humors rot, and the bones are separated from them, the removal of them will be alleviated, and their attraction.
If however there is one large bone, such as the thigh bone or the hip bone, and similar to them, and what of it is corrupted is a large part: then scrape it with a final scraping, until that blackness and corruption are removed. Then restore the wound.
If the bone which is in it is corrupted, and it is a large part, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it, and to cut it entirely to the place where the corruption penetrates: then treat it, until it consolidates.
And I will tell you a fracture that happens to a man in his thigh: so that you may put it as an example and aid upon your operation.
There was a man of youthful age around thirty years, to whom pain happened in his thigh, from a cause which moved upon it within the body, that is, from the interior, until the matters reached the thigh and it became an aposteme with a large aposteme: and there was not an external cause for him.
It was extended therefore with it the time when the error of the physicians, until the aposteme was opened, and many matters converged with it. And the treatment of it was postponed, until the thigh became fistulous, and many orifices were made in it: which all emitted pus, and the humidity of the body.
A multitude of physicians therefore treated him for about two years, and there was not in it a wise hand in the art, until I was called, and I saw his thigh, and the matters which flowed from those orifices with a great flow: also the whole body of the man had already wasted away, and its color was citrine.
I therefore inserted a probe into one of the places of the orifices, and the probe reached to the bone.
Then I examined all the orifices: and I found them to reach to a sinew from all parts of the thigh.
I therefore cut and divided above one of those orifices, until I uncovered a part of the bone: and I found it corrupted: which already was corroded, and blackened, and putrid, and perforated, until it had penetrated to the marrow.
I will scrape away what has been detected by me, and prepared from the corrupted bone.
And I have scraped, what was not in the bone except that corruption, which I cut away and scraped. And I will now eradicate it.
Then I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidating medicines for a long time. And it is not consolidated.
Then I felt and probed over the bone secondly above the first detection, and I found a continuous corruption with the bone, and I scraped what appeared to me also from that corruption.
Then I attempted the restoration of it. And it is not restored.
I probed therefore over it again, and did not cease to cut the bone piece by piece, and to attempt its restoration: and it is not restored, until I cut around one palm from the bone, and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it was consolidated quickly, and healed: and this iteration in the operation of it, and its section, was not necessary, except because of the disposition of debility of the infirm, and the paucity of his tolerance, and my fear over it of death: since there happened to him in all hours from an excess of evacuation of bad syncopy.
He was healed therefore by an integral healing: and hard flesh was born in the place of the bone, and a good disposition was made in his body, and his strength returned: and he was converted in his dispositions: and no harm was done to him in walking, not at all.
If however the bone is prominent in the place of the body and already fractured: then it is necessary that you scrape it, according to this method: and it is: that you take a ligament, and tie it on the extremity of the prominent bone: and order someone to extend it upwards: and put another ligament made of coarse wool on top of the first ligament. Then tie it above the flesh that is under the bone and stretch out the limb so you pull the flesh to the lower parts and you will find the flesh above the place you want to cut, so the saw does not hurt the flesh.
Put a stick or a board under the lower bone decently, for when you do that, it is not prevented from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be above the corrupted place for a little while, so that the corruption is not in the concavity of the bone, and does not appear in its appearance to the sense, and compels to the sawing of itself rather than another.
For if the bone is corrupted and is not prominent, namely the part of it continues with the part, and the corruption is in the middle of it or in its own part, then strip the flesh from all parts entirely. Then put a stick below.
Afterwards saw it from the first side, where is the corruption, until the sawing continues from the other side: and let the sawing be according to the length of the corruption for a little while, according to what we have said.
For if the corruption is in the joint, cut through the corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it continues from the other side.
If indeed the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no ingenuity in it except scraping.
For if the corruption is in the chest of the hand or in the chest of the foot, then the matter in it is very difficult. It is necessary that you investigate how the corruption appears to you. And scrape it and clean it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever ingenuity you can make straight, when the vein or nerve does not make a contradiction for you.
And know that the incisions and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and fineness, and size, and smallness, and hardness, and rarity. Wherefore it is necessary that you prepare all kinds of instruments suitable for that operation.
And know that these operations signify to you above the form of an instrument which you need, when with you is a long study. And the knowledge of the modes of this art is a testimony of the modes of diseases for then you will already find by yourself that which is suitable to it from instruments for every weakness.
And I will establish for you at the end of this chapter a number of instruments which you will cite as examples upon which you rely and a test by which you try other things.
The shape of another saw.
The shape of a razor for joints. Let the head of this razor be according to the shape of the head of a star-shaped key: and let its points be according to the shape of the points of an awl.
And it is not suitable unless the heads of joints rub against it when they are corrupted or the mouth is wide and large.
The shape of a larger razor.
The shape of a broad razor.
The shape of a razor in which there is a concavity.
The shape of another subtle razor.
The shape of another razor with a curved extremity.
The shape of a small razor, subtle and held.
This is similar to a file, and the extremities are like a saw: it is shown in many places from the rasping of bones.
The form of a rasp which suits the rasping of that which is perforated by bones.
Its extremities are triangular, and it is sharp at the extremities.
It is made from Indian steel. And similarly it is necessary that all things which we have spoken be made from rasps, and incisors, and saws.
On the cutting of extremities, and the sawing of bones.
The extremities rot, either from an external cause, or an internal one.
When therefore you have treated that corruption with medical cures, and the treatment is not effective: and you see the corruption going into the member, which nothing has struck: then it is necessary that you cut that member until where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may be saved from death, or from the evil which is a greater defect of the member.
And the sign to which this happens is that the member turns black, until it rots: which fire has burned it, or it rots after turning black: until the rot is cut out to that which follows that member, and creeps into the whole body.
It is necessary therefore with the cutting of it, and similarly if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of certain poisonous things: such as the marine scorpion, and viper, and weasel, and similar things.
If therefore the corruption, or the sting is in the extremity of a finger, then cut the finger, and do not give space to the corruption, so that it may not go into the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then cut it out from the root of the forearm, and do not give space to the corruption, until it takes hold in the forearm.
And if it invades into it, then cut the arm at the elbow in its joint.
If however corruption passes through and you see it around the elbow, then in that there is death of the weak. And to administer beyond that from cure, is a quantity of strength.
And do similarly for the foot, when corruption invades into a toe, then cut it at one of the joints: and if it takes hold in the breast of the foot, then cut off the whole foot. If however it ascends to the knee, then cut the leg at the joint of the knee.
What if corruption has already passed beyond the knee, then there is not in it a remedy, except abandonment, and to leave the weak to death.
And the method of cutting a limb, or sawing it, is that you tie a ligature below, which you want to cut: and tie another ligature above the place. And let the assistant stretch one ligature downwards to the lower parts: and let the other assistant stretch the upper ligature upwards. And you separate the flesh that is between the two ligatures, with a broad flat instrument, until the whole flesh is divided. Then let it be cut, or sawn.
And it is proper, that you put a linen cloth on all the parts, and the saw does not touch the healthy place: and an additional injury happens to the weak, and a hot abscess.
If however a flow of blood happens internally while you are operating, then cauterize the place quickly: or put on it some powders that staunch blood. Then return to your medication, until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded limb with a bandage suitable for it, and take care of it, until it is healed.
And I tell you what happened to a man with his foot, this same thing that I am telling you.
What is it, that which happens to its foot blackness, with a burning that is likened to the combustion of fire: and that corruption first happens to its toe, until it begins the whole foot.
It has brought out a man from itself, after it saw the corruption befalling the limb with its vehemence, which it found out from the pain, and the burning of it: and it fell upon the joint, and it was healed.
And after a long time had passed for him, the same corruption happened to him on the index finger of his hand.
He came therefore to me, and I attempted to recover that excess with what I placed on the hand from medicines after the purification of his body: and the excess did not rebound, and it began to enter into another finger: until the corruption began in the hand.
He called therefore for me to make an incision on his hand: and I was unwilling to do it, hoping from me for a rebound of that excess: and I also feared death itself in the incision of his hand. For the man's strength was upon the case.
After I had given up hope for him, he returned to his land: and he came to me concerning it, that he had hastened, and the whole hand was affected: and he was healed.
And I have not added this narration, except that it may be of help concerning that which falls from the kind of this disease: and that it may be a signification which is signified, and according to which it is acted.
Of the treatment of abscesses, and the quality of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess happens in certain fleshy limbs, and the space of the abscess is prolonged, until it makes a collection of pus: then it bursts, or is perforated, and what is in it of pus comes out, and the empty place remains as if it were a vessel: and the skin which is over it is like cloth that has already been worn thin: and it does not penetrate to the utmost in its corruption, nor does it impress on the bone, nor on the nerve, nor on the ligament: it deserves to be named an abscess: and it is not called a Fistula: nevertheless if its space is prolonged, until the corruption impresses on any of these members, then it is called a Fistula, or a zuken.
And the treatment of it is that you make an incision into the entire wound as it is turned out, and especially when it has been rendered subtle and made like a cloth, and you are confident because it will not adhere to the place on account of its own corruption.
And if you suspect that the skin will adhere, and there does not follow from it the last corruption of the consequence, and if there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with a clyster, and it is that you should consider regarding the anus.
Then if it is large, and the pus that is emitted from it is of foul odor: then clysterize it with an Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, and oil, and zingar, taking equal parts, mixed together in a vessel: and decocted over fire, until the medicine reddens: and applied to the thickness of honey.
Then take from it what you need, and dissolve it with water and honey, and clysterize the anus with it: and constrict the orifice of it, and let the medicine stay in it for the quantity of two hours.
Then extract it with expression: do this for as many days as until the anus is cleaned and the foul smell is removed.
And when you clysterize with water of ashes, when this medicine is not present to you: and it is that you take the ash of grapevines or the ash of holm oak trees: and throw water upon it. Then strain it, and clysterize with it, until you are confident that the anus is now cleaned.
Which if the weak person is not able to tolerate the Egyptian medicine, nor water of ashes, then clysterize it with water and honey, into which something has already been mixed from crushed ginger. Or clysterize it with honey and wine mixed together, since it is a property of honey to clean and wash, and the wine makes the anus adhere, especially when there is an excess of dryness and astringency in the wine.
When you do that several times, and are confident that the corruption of the sinus has already been removed: then insert a suppository with what makes flesh grow in it.
And it is, that you take from the plaster dyafinicon, and dissolve it with rose oil and styptic wine, or insert a suppository with certain medicines and other ointments, whose properties I have written about in the treatment of ointments.
What if the orifice of the sinus is narrow, in which the cannula of the clyster does not fit: then widen it with iron for a little while: or put in it a pledget wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or in royal ointment, until it is widened.
And similarly if the orifice is also wide, then bring its lips together with a suture: and reduce from it the quantity in which the clyster can enter without addition.
And similarly if the orifice from which pus runs is elevated to the upper parts: then make an incision in the lower place that is in it, so that the pus may run out from it downwards: for when pus is confined in the depth of the sinus, it prevents flesh from being generated in it.
What if it is not possible for you to make an incision in the sinus around its lower part according to what you wish: then try to prepare a membrane with a preparation so that pus may run out from it with ease according to what is prepared for you. Try, that nothing of the pus is retained in it at all: and take consolidating medicines that are put on the wound, suitable to what you wish. And spread them on a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such quantity that it makes contact with the entire sinus. Then cut with forceps directly at the orifice of the sinus a hole, slightly larger than the orifice of the sinus. Then extend from the unguents a gentle dressing over another cloth, according to the quantity of the wound: and put it on the same position lengthwise from the orifice of the Sinus, so that the pus may be drained from it: and you will not remove the large dressing, except after some days once.
That one, however, which is over the orifice of the Sinus, is that which you remove every hour, so that what accumulates in it from pus may go out: and so that you know, whether what flows from it is much, or little: or digested, or undigested.
And with this again consider the location of the Sinus itself, whether the master of it feels pain in it, or there is no pain in it, and whether there is an abscess in it, or there is not an abscess in it.
When therefore your treatment is thus extended, and you see the matter which goes out over the orifice of the Sinus, then know that the Sinus already adheres, or approximates to adhesion: then therefore loosen it in the morning, or on the third day: and when you loosen the wound, then change the cloth, and renew the unguent.
If however there goes out from the Sinus after a long space subtle pus, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, since there are men who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and there are some who act contrary to that.
If however there goes out from the Sinus after many days undigested pus, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If therefore the adhesion of the Sinus is delayed, and its matter is prolonged: then add to the excitation of medicines, which truly make medics, so that they are medicines in their substance humid, and in their virtue dry: such as the unguent dyafinicon, when there is added to it an excess of addition from zegi.
And from the greatest last of which it is cured, it is that you take the ointment called dyafinicon, and dissolve it with rose oil, and apply it on top of the old wine equal in its substance.
Then mix it with that, and use it.
And now it does a similar action to this, when the ointment dyafinicon is not present to you, honey, when you have decocted it, until it becomes thick, and you use it. Or take myrrh, and aloe, and frankincense: grind all of that, and pulverize it over the honey while it is over the fire. Then smear it over a cloth, and bind it over the wound: and smear with honey that you have decocted, until it has thickened: and pulverize the spices over it, and put over it a cloth, and bind it.
And when it is administered, there are given to it Aristolochia longa, and the roots of lily of heaven, and it is lily: and flour of the herb, and centaury. Use them simple, or mixed, grind them, and sift them: and pulverize over the honey that I have described, and use it.
What if the matter of the wound is prolonged, and it is not healed by this cure: then know that there is a corruption in its depth: or now it makes an impression on the bones, or on the remaining hard members, or on the ligaments: then treat it with the cure of Fistulas, according to what has been stated before.
On the treatment of a wounded or rubbed-away nail, and an incised toe, and a cut.
A panaritium is a tumor that grows under the nail of the thumb of the hand, or foot: and perhaps it grows on the other toes.
When it grows, the matter of it is prolonged, and its cure is neglected, and it suppurates with a hot tumor: or it becomes rotten, and emits pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail: and perhaps it rots entirely, and perhaps the corruption passes to the bone, until there is a foul smell: and the extremity of the toe becomes broad, and its color becomes dark.
When you raise your treatment with what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not work: then it is necessary to cut with iron all the surplus that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For cauterization is a strong help.
If indeed the bone is healthy, and the nail is also healthy, and the additions of vacant nail now press on the flesh inside, and begin to pierce it, and wound it: then it is necessary that you put a subtle pumice under the angle of the nail which pierces the flesh, and push it upwards: and cut that flesh with ease, and put on top of what remains of the flesh from medicines that burn and corrode, until all of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments, until it is healed.
If however the corruption has already made an impression on the bone, then it is necessary to cut out that bone, and extract it: for it will not be healed as long as the corrupted bone remains in it.
What if you see corrosion and corruption entering into the finger, then cut it at one of the joints, according to which its movement has been previously established. Then treat it, until it is healed.
If however there occurs nail attrition or a blow, and there is severe pain in it, then it is necessary to perform bloodletting on the weak part first. Then cut the nail with a sharp spatula, a cross-section from above downwards.
And beware, lest the cut penetrate to the flesh that is under the nail, for you will cause through it severe pain on the weak part, and it will be a cause for the birth of additional flesh in the location.
Then treat the place with what calms pains.
The digit which is added to the birth of some hands of men, is perhaps entirely fleshy: and perhaps there are bones in some of them, and perhaps there is a nail in it: and some of them are born at the root of the joint, or their birth is in some nodes of the digit. That which is born at some node of the digit, does not move: and that which is born near the joint of the digit, perhaps moves.
The section of that which of them is fleshy, is easy. That you should cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
The treatment of that whose birth is at the root of the joint, is difficult. Therefore refrain from cutting it.
In that which is born on the digit near the last node, then it is necessary to cut the flesh first with a round incision up to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for that purpose. Then treat it, until it is healed.
The joining together which happens to the digits, often happens either from the birth of the man, that is, it is born with it, or from the consolidation of a wound, or from the burning of fire, and similar things.
It is necessary therefore to cut away that consolidation, until the digits return to their natural form. Then put between them a dressing or cloths soaked in rose oil, so that they do not consolidate quickly, and separate between them: or put between them a subtle sheet of lead, until the place is consolidated according to what is necessary.
And similarly if there happens a joining together of some digits with the palm, then cut away that consolidation, according to what prepares and suits the shape of the member.
On the incision of the vein.
There are varicose veins, thick and full of melancholic superfluities which occur in many parts of the body: and they most often happen in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers and laborers.
It is necessary therefore that you administer a strong purging of the body from black bile many times with strong purging.
Then perform bloodletting on the patient in the vein of the arm.
The treatment of them with iron is according to two methods. One of them is that they are cauterized and black blood is extracted. And the other method is that the vein is lifted up and entirely removed.
The cutting of it is according to this method. First apply warmth with hot water to the leg until the thick bloody feculence is dissolved. Then bind the weak leg from the upper part of the thigh to the lower part of the knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one place, or two, or three incisions as needed.
Then lift, that is, extract the blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and it is necessary that you perform bloodletting on the weak one first. Then cut the vein until below, until a quantity of blood comes out that you see is sufficient and which the strength of the weak person can tolerate.
Afterwards bind it, and instruct him to abstain from foods that produce black bile, and also evacuation and bloodletting when the veins are full, for this harms the weak person.
The evacuation of it is according to this method. Shave the weak leg if there are many hairs on it. Then insert it into the bath, or immerse it in its hock with hot water, until it becomes red and the veins become swollen, or let it be exercised with strong exercise, if the bath is not at hand, until the member becomes warm.
Then cut the skin in the direction of the vein's section along its length: or at its end near the knee, or in its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and excavate the vein from every part, until it appears to sensation: and at its appearance, that which you see is that it turns from redness to blackness downward. When therefore it emerges from the skin, you see it as a white thread.
Then insert under it the razor, until when it is elevated and comes out of the skin: suspend it with a blunt hook, gently.
Then cut another section near that section with three fingers. Then excavate the skin from the upper part of the vein, until it appears. Then elevate it with the razor as you have done, and suspend it with another hook, as you have done at first.
Then cut another section, or several sections, if you need them. Then elevate and cut it at the last section near the heel.
Then pull it out to the section which is above it.
And do that until you pull it out from the section which is superior, or higher than all the sections, until when it comes out whole: then cut it.
If it does not obey you to pull out and elevate: then insert a needle with a strong double thread, and tie it, and pull it out: or insert the razor under it: and turn your hand to every part, until it comes out. And beware, lest it be cut. For if it is cut, it will be difficult for you to heal the wound, and an injury will increase from it on account of its weakness.
When you have extracted it entirely, then put on the place of the wound a wool cloth soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil, and treat it until it is healed.
What if the vein is contracted, having a twisting where there is an involution to the parts, and it is not as we have said according to rectitude: then it is necessary that you find on it at every part of its twisting, and the place of its involution.
Then suspend it with hooks, until you elevate it entirely: and beware at your dissection above it lest you strike a vein, or wound: for it will be difficult for you to elevate, or extract it. Preserve therefore your prudence.
The shape of the incision by which the vein is healed.
The shape of a blind hook.
It does not have curvature like the rest of the hooks, nor is it of sharp extremity, so that it does not wound the vein: and it is of gentle flexibility, thick: for if it were subtle, it would strike the vein with its subtlety: indeed let it be a superfluous thickness, as we have said.
On the extraction of the saphena vein.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot climates, such as Alizegi, and the land of the Arabs, and in dry, hot lands of little tenderness, that is, in which no herb or tree is born.
And perhaps it is generated in other places of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is indeed from the putrefaction that occurs under the skin, just like it happens inside the bodies of snakes and worms, and worms generated between the skin and the flesh.
The sign of the inception of this vein is an effusion which occurs in the thigh as a severe inflammation. Then let the place be looked at. Then the vein begins to come out from the place of that inspection, as if it were a root of a plant, or an animal.
When therefore the extremity of it appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead, whose weight is one or two drachms, and tie it, and let the suspended lead hang from the thigh.
As often as anything comes out from it to the exterior, wrap that in lead and tie it. What if it is prolonged a great deal, then cut off a part of it, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it at the root until the whole thing has come out. For if you cut it, it contracts and returns into the flesh, and causes an abscess to occur, and putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Wherefore it is necessary that it be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it comes out from its last part, and nothing of it remains in the body: and from this vein comes out in some men what is five palms and ten in length. And now it has happened to me, that a man came out with it twenty palms.
If however it is cut during the time of your treatment, then insert the root into its hole, and perforate it with a long instrument, with a body, until everything that is in it of material comes out: and administer putrefaction of the place with medicines for some days. Then treat it with the cure for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot, or in the foot itself: and many openings occur to it, and a branch comes out from each opening.
Treatment is applied to it, as we said in the division, and with what has been mentioned beforehand.
On the excision of worms generated under the skin, and it is called Bouina disease.
This disease is called Bouina disease in a certain land among us: because it often happens to cattle, and there is a small worm that is born between the skin and the flesh.
And it wanders throughout the whole body, going up and down: and it shows itself to feeling during its movement from limb to limb, until it breaks out wherever it breaks out on the skin, and emerges.
And its generation is from the putridness of certain humors. As worms and snakes happen, that is, intestinal worms, and roundworms in the belly.
And it is feared on account of its malice. For when it wanders in the body, and rises to the head, and reaches the location: then perhaps it opens in it, and emerges, and destroys the eye: and this happens many times.
When therefore you wish to treat it, and extract it, then it is not done unless during its movement, and the appearance of itself to feeling.
It is necessary therefore, that you bind what is above it, and what is below it with a strong constricting bandage. Then cut above it, and extract it.
If it has become embedded in the flesh, and you do not find it: then apply a cautery with fire to the location, until it dies.
And the greater thing that is feared, is its corruption, which corrupts the eye, as we have said.
If however you see it having already reached the head near the eye, then bind beneath it the eyebrow with a good strong bandage. Then cut above it, and extract it.
And it is suitable that he should use the mild treatment of his body with medicines that dissolve putrid malignant humors.
And let him beware for himself of foods generating putrefaction.
On the incision regarding the disease of Nakir.
This disease is called in our land Nakir, and it is a pain that occurs in certain members, then it changes from member to member.
And I have indeed already seen it, according to which I relate to you. I was called to a weak woman in certain villages. And she stretched out her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm.
And after I remained for one hour, I saw one swelling entering with a thread, as a worm enters, ascending to her shoulder more swiftly than it can be, so that it is like living silver, when it runs from place to place.
The pain therefore receded from that place, and was fixed in the shoulder.
Then I sat for one hour, and it ran into the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm.
Then she told me that it was turning in her whole body, according to what I saw.
I was amazed therefore at the swiftness of its change from member to member. And I had not seen this disease before with my own eyes in this manner. However, I saw many who find the pain changing from member to member, and I did not see it with my eyes as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think that it is anything but because she was one of those who are from villages with a dry body, detecting the veins, hence therefore it appears to the sense that the veininess is changed.
And it is necessary that they not appear according to this method in those who are delicate and in bodies that are humid with hidden veins.
When you apply the treatment to it, and the patient feels that pain: then if it appears to your eye, as we have said, then press above it and below it quickly: and cut above it, until the constrained bloating goes out: and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your eyes: then treat it with a purge of the body, and with that which expels bloating, and purges them with foul-smelling pills, and pills made from serapion, and similar things.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are distinguished according to their kinds and according to the places where they fall from the body.
Their diversity is this: that among them are those which have large and small points, and are hollow and blunt. And among them are those which have three edges. And among them are those with barbs. And among them are those with little stars.
Those indeed which are according to the limbs where they fall, are according to two methods. For the limbs are either from the principal hollow limbs, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and similar things. When therefore it falls into any one of these limbs, and the signs of death appear to you, which I will tell you afterwards, then it is necessary that you should give up the extraction of that arrow from them, for death follows in him in whom it is, in most cases. And when these evil signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the limb, then extract it, and treat the wound.
For signs of the brain are when an arrow falls into it, and penetrates the bone, and exits through the siphac which is above the brain, there occur: because accidents happen from that, such as heaviness, and dizziness, and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation, and redness of the tongue, and spasm, and confusion of the intellect, and vomiting of bile. And perhaps blood exits from the nostrils, or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and voice is destroyed. And a moisture similar to white pus exits from the place of the wound. And there exits from it something similar to the decay of flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and extract the arrow if it does not exit.
The signs of an arrow when it falls into the heart are: that it is near the left breast: and one feels it as if it is fixed in a hard thing, not in a hollow thing. And perhaps the arrow has a movement similar to the movement of a pulse: and black blood runs out from the wound. And it is followed by coldness of the extremities, and a cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore, that death descends without a doubt.
And the sign of an arrow when it wounds the lung, is the exit of foamy blood from the wound: and the vessels that follow the neck become disordered, and the color of the sick person changes. And he breathes out a great, high spirit: and seeks an attraction of cold air.
For if the arrow falls into the membrane, which is in the chest: then it is near the small ribs: and there is made a great panting with vehement pain, with sighing: and all the limbs of the shoulders are moved.
And if an arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps something undigested will come out of the wound from the food.
And if an arrow falls into the belly and is fixed in it, something of dung will come out of the wound, or gas, or intestine which is already disrupted. There is therefore no hope in its cure, nor in the removal of the arrow.
What if an arrow falls into the bladder and urine comes out: and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is stronger above the weak place: then you should know that it is mortal.
The remaining limbs, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and shoulder, and upper arm, and spine of the back, and collarbone, and hip, and leg, and similar ones from the limbs are saved according to multiple arrangements: when the arrow does not find an artery or a nerve: and the arrow is not poisonous.
And I tell you what I have seen concerning the extraction of these arrows, so that you may understand it regarding your cure.
Which is, that to a certain man an arrow fell into the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose. I therefore extracted it from him on the other side, under the fat of his ear: and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which fell into the fat of his eye under his lower eyelid: and the arrow was already hidden, and I did not take out anything of it except a small end, with which it adhered to his bone: and it was a large arrow among the arrows of Turkish bows, with soft iron, in which there are no notches.
The Jew was therefore healed, and no bad accident happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And there was an Arabic arrow, and it is that which has a head. I cut above it between two guidings: and now it was submerged in his throat. I was careful with it, until I extracted it: and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from the man into whom it had fallen in his belly: and we thought that he would die from it.
After thirty days had passed for him, and nothing had changed in his condition, I cut above the arrow: and I applied a poultice to it, and extracted it: and he was healed, and no bad accident happened to him.
And I saw a man to whom an arrow had fallen into his back, and the wound was consolidated above it. After seven years had passed, the arrow came out at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman to whom an arrow had fallen into her belly, and the wound was consolidated above it, and the arrow remained inside her: and nothing changed in her condition, and the woman did not find any harm in any of her natural operations.
And I saw another man, to whom an arrow had fallen into his face: and the wound was consolidated, and it remained, not causing him much pain: and many things similar to these.
And I extracted an arrow from one of the King's leaders, which had fallen into the middle of his nose, and it had declined to the right side a little: and the whole arrow was hidden.
I was called therefore to his cure after the accident of the arrow for three days. I found the wound of the arrow constricted tightly.
I searched for it with a subtle probe, and I did not feel it: and he himself felt a puncture and pain under his right ear. The instrument is therefore a needle that comes from the extremity of the arrow.
I applied a plaster to the spot with a plaster in which there was an attractive and digestive virtue, trusting that the place would apostulate and the sign of the arrow would appear to me so I could cut above it, but nothing happened at the spot indicating whether or not the arrow had hit it.
I treated it then with a plaster for many days, but no effect occurred and the wound was consolidated meanwhile, and he remained weak from the extraction of it for a space of days, until he felt the arrow on a certain day inside his nose. And he told me that.
And he told me that. I applied then to that wound a sharp corroding medicine for many days, until it was opened: and when I probed I felt the subtle extremity of the arrow which adhered to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound through that sharp medicine until the extremity of the arrow appeared visibly to us, and it remained with me for a space of time around four months.
Then after the wound had been enlarged and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and move it, and it did not obey for exit: then I did not cease to subtly contrive and ingeniously work on it with modes of contrivance, until I began to work on it one day with well-made forceps, according to which the shape of them will be described at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And doctors indeed judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. I restored it then, and the wound was consolidated, and the weak one was healed with complete health, and nothing at all harmed him.
And I tell you the quality of the extraction of certain arrows, so that you may put a rule and significance on it based on what I did not say.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not comprehended with narration, nor does the book comprehend it, and the skilled artisan compares a little with much, and that which is present with that which is absent, and finds a new operation and a new instrument in the descent of extraneous things of this art when they occur.
I say therefore, that arrows do not come out of the members in which they are fixed, except in two ways. Or by extraction from the place into which it entered. Or by the opposite part of another.
And that which comes out from where it entered, is either an arrow proceeding into a fleshy place, and is drawn out, and comes out.
For if it does not obtrude to the exit from the hour in which it falls, then it is necessary that you should leave it for some days, until the flesh which is in its vicinity putrefies, and its attraction is alleviated, and its egression.
And similarly if it is fixed in a bone, and does not obtrude to you at the exit, then leave it also for some days, and move it with attraction and motion every day, until it comes out.
For if it does not obtrude to you at the exit after days, then it is necessary that you should perforate in the vicinity of the arrow in the same bone on every side with a subtle drill, until the arrows are enlarged. Then draw it out and extract it.
And if the arrow has already been hidden in a location of the body, and is concealed and hidden from sensation: then investigate it with probing. And if you feel it, then attract it with certain instruments which are suitable for its attraction.
And if you are not able to reach it, on account of the constriction of the wound, and on account of the length of the arrow in the depth: and there is no bone, nor nerve, nor vein there: then probe above it, until the wound is enlarged, and it is possible to extract the arrow.
What if there are to them bones which hold it, then free the flesh fixed in them from every side, with every ingenuity that you can, and ingenuities, if it cannot be above the separation of the flesh in the fracture of the bones, and their dislocation, until it is extracted.
And when you administer the extraction of the arrow, in whatever place it is, then use the dislocation of your hand, with forceps on all parts, until you make it come out: and proceed with utmost subtlety, so that the arrow is not broken, and its attraction and extraction are not made difficult for you.
What if you cannot reach it in one hour, then leave it for some days, until those things that are around it putrefy. Then return to it: for indeed the pus will have relieved the contrary condition for you, if on the other hand a flow of blood makes a complication for you, then administer what we said concerning its cure in its own chapter: and guard your skill from cutting the vein, or nerve, or tendon.
And administer the instrument in every way that is possible to you for the extraction of the arrowhead: and let it be with facility, and tranquility, or slowness, and firmness, as I have narrated to you.
And it is necessary, that you do when you pull out the arrow, that it be loose according to the shape, according to which it was in the case of the arrow itself. For it is of greater advantage. If however it is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you from the figures.
The arrow which comes out from the contrary part of another, or now something already proceeds from it to the exterior: or it makes a gibbosity of the extremity of the arrow by sensation from the upper part of the adjacent skin, and you see it standing out: cut therefore over it, and let the cutting be according to the quantity that the forceps can grasp in it.
Then pull it out: for it itself becomes easy to exit.
If indeed it is retained in the bone, then twist your hand according to its roundness, until the arrow impresses on the bone, and enlarges itself: then pull it out.
And if not: leave it for some days: then repeat it, until it comes out.
What if the wood of the arrow is in it, then drive it out with it.
If however the wood has fallen, and you want to administer a push: then introduce into it a concave instrument, so that its concavity enters into the tail of the arrow: then drive it out with it.
If however the arrow is concave, then drive it out with an instrument entering into it. For the arrow will be raised by that.
What if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary, that you hollow out the whole flesh, in which the venom is: if it is possible for you. Then treat it with what connects to that.
What if it is an arrow falling in the breast, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side: and is near to that which you touch with the probe, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and beware of the incision of a vein, or a nerve, and extract it.
Then sew the wound, if you see, and treat it, until it is healed.
And this is the form of forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let there be similar extremities like a bird's beak, which are made like iron: when they grasp above the arrow, they do not release it.
And they are made of them in many kinds, large and small and medium, according to the quantity of the size of the arrow, and its smallness, and the width of the wound, and its constriction.
The form of a concave pusher.
And the form of a convex pusher.
This is the extremity of the convex one, like a rod, so that its entrance is alleviated into the concave arrow, and it is pushed with it.
Of the cutting of veins.
The veins to be cut, whose course is known in the body, are thirty.
Of these, in the head are sixteen: two pulsating veins, which are behind the ears, called the two Herbae: and two arteries, which are visible in both temples: and two veins which are in both tear ducts of the eyes, which are called Aspicientes: and a vein prepared in the middle of the forehead: and a vein which is at the extremity of the nose. And two guide rods, which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins that are called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
The veins that are bled from in the arms, are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalica, and it is from the left side: and the common people call it the vein of the head. And the dark one. And it is the middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilica and a branch of the Cephalica: and the common people call it the vein of the body. And the Basilica, and it is placed on the right side: and it is also called Titillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the cord of the arm, and it is placed above the elbow: and it is that from which blood is let, and it is that which appears above the thumb with a visible and clear appearance.
And the Saluatella: and it is the vein which is between the ring finger and the little finger: and there are two branches of it.
And in the leg and in the foot there are three veins. One of them is the one that is under the bend of the thumb on the left side. And the second is the Saphena: and its place is at the heel on the right side. And the vein Ness: and its location is at the heel on the left side.
And in the other leg there are three similar veins.
Of the bloodletting of two veins behind the ears.
The bloodletting of the two veins which are behind the ears is a remedy for old catarrhs, and hemicrania, and sahafat, and bad old headaches.
The quality of bloodletting is such that it is secondary to which I relate. And it is, that the head of the weak one should be scraped: and his occiput should be rubbed at the place of the two veins with a rough cloth for good friction. Then the weak one should bind his neck with a ribbon, until the two veins appear. And the places of the ears are behind the ears in two depressed places of the head. Seek them therefore with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, there mark with a branding iron.
Then take the bloodletting knife, and it is that which is called the Incising one. Then insert it under the vein in the skin, until the bloodletter reaches the mouth. Then raise your hand with the vein and skin to the upper parts, and cut the vein with the skin with an incising cut. And let the length of the incision be the quantity of two joined fingers, or about that, and make come out from the blood as much as you want.
Then bind it with poultices, and leave it, until it is healed.
And now the incision of them has preceded in the beginning of the book, and their cauterization.
Of the two Arteries which are in the two temples, bloodletting is a remedy for old migraine: and wrong pride, and frequent eye disease: and an outpouring of overflowing humors to the eyes.
And the quality of bloodletting them is according to which I relate to you. Let the weak one bind his neck tightly, until the two veins appear with a clear and obvious sensation, and let their pulse be felt under your finger. Then apply the septicum with cauterization.
Next raise the skin upwards with your index finger to the upper parts and insert the lancet from underneath and raise the vein to the upper parts. And cut it, as you did with the two other veins, and let the blood flow out according to the quantity of your necessity.
Then loosen the tight bandage and put your finger on the vein for one hour. Afterwards put a plaster and poultices on it, and bind it tightly from above with a strong bandage: and leave it until it heals.
And now the description of them, and their cutting, and their extraction has already been dealt with at the beginning of the book.
The justification for bloodletting from the vein of the forehead after the bloodletting of the head is for long-standing facial ailments and for half-headache and redness and foul ulcers.
And the quality of this bloodletting is according to what I will tell you. The patient should bind his neck with a ribbon until the vein appears. Then take the instrument that is called a Fossorium.
Put the sharp point that is at the head of the Fossorium on the vein, and strike it with a hammer or something else in its manner: and let the blood flow out according to the quantity you want. Then loosen the bandage of the patient and bind it until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a broad lancet. However, it is not necessary that the bloodletter be of sharp extremity, but rather of broad extremity, unlike other bloodletting instruments. And let the bloodletting be second to the transfusion because the mouth is nearby and perhaps the bloodletter will cut it when it is subtle.
The treatment of the two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, is for an eye affected with itch and redness: and to purify the eye, and the pains of the face.
And the quality of the bloodletting of them is, that it constricts the weak neck with a thread. Then let the bloodletter perform on them: and you stand over its head.
And let the bloodletting be done across to a little length, with a small bloodletter, broad for a little while, for there is no flesh in the place. If the bloodletter is subtle at the extremity, perhaps it will be cut.
Then let flow out of the blood as much as is necessary for you. And put a plaster over them, and constrict them one night, and release them.
The bloodletting of the veins of the Nose is beneficial for acute fever and vehement swelling, and pains of the face, such as half-headache and redness which occurs in the face: especially when it is chronic.
And the quality of its bloodletting is, that it constricts the weak neck. Then hold its nose with your left hand, and take a subtle bloodletter, long: and fix it in the middle of the lip, between that which distinguishes the nose according to straightness. Because the vein does not appear to the sense there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you press your hand with the bloodletter for a little while: and let flow out of the blood according to your need. Then bind it for one night. For it is restored quickly.
The remedy of Guidetti's bloodletting is for constriction of the breath, and catching of leprosy, and melancholy diseases, and impetigo and evil sores, and corroding, that is, herpes and itch.
And the quality of the bloodletting of the ears is that it should constrict the weak one under both with a bandage on his neck: and let the artisan stand over the head of the weak person, and the weak person should be sitting on a seat.
Then let blood from the vein to the length of the bloodletting instrument, for a short time, being ample. Then take out from the blood a quantity tempered, or according to that which you see by necessity which is for it. Then do likewise for the other vein.
Afterwards undo the bandage, and constrict the two veins with a moderate tightness: so that the weak person is not suffocated. And let it be until morning. For the wound will heal.
Of the bloodletting of the veins, according to Algeberich. The remedy of Algeberich's bloodletting of the veins is: because it benefits the ulceration in the mouth, and corruption of the gum: and evil sores: and cracks of the lips, and evil sores, also which are in the nose, and its surroundings.
And the quality of the bloodletting of the ears is, that you should make the weak person sit before you: and constrict his neck with a band. Then uncover his lip, and look at the two veins: of which one is on the right of the lip, and the second on its left: and the sign of each is through blackness of the gums. And that vein is: because around the ears there are small black veins. Cut therefore on both sides with an oblique incision.
If however there is doubt in you, and you do not know which of them it is: then attend to the incision of the greater one, and it will become more manifest.
And do similarly for the two veins which are in the upper lip: and for the most part custom runs with it: and cut the two veins which are in the lower lip.
Of bloodletting from the vein under the tongue after the bloodletting of the head. The remedy of the two veins which are under the tongue for bloodletting is: that the bloodletting of the head is for the afflictions which are in the throat, and the sickness of the uvula, and the sicknesses of the mouth.
And the quality of bloodletting them is, that you should seat the weak one between your hands before the sun: and elevate his tongue, and look under the tongue from one side one vein, and from the other side another vein: and the color of both veins is inclined towards blackness.
Bloodlet therefore on both: and beware, lest your last hand be in bloodletting them. For under them are arteries, and perhaps it happens that there is a flow of blood from those arteries.
The three veins indeed which are bloodlet in the elbow, are in which the custom of bloodletting has run in all men.
And the bloodletting of them is done according to two methods. Either by pricking with a broad myrtle needle: or inclining to subtlety with an olive: or cutting with a knife-shaped needle, and it is called Alnesil. And this is its shape.
And from them are broad and subtle types according to the width of the veins and their tightness. The form of the myrtle phlebotomy is broad, as you see. And it converges to the opening of hollow veins, full, proceeding, apparent, thick and those which contain gross, turbid blood.
And this phlebotomy is of lesser width, and more subtle extremities.
And it converges to the phlebotomy of subtle veins, which contain subtle yellow blood.
The Basilica, however, which is one of these three veins for phlebotomy, is a remedy. For it extracts blood from ailments that are under the throat and neck, from that which follows the chest and stomach: and it converges to the phlebotomist with the phlebotomy instrument, so that he may cut, and is above fear of it, for beneath it is an artery. If therefore he misses, and touches the puncture of the phlebotomy, he will wound that artery, and there will be a flow of blood. Wherefore it is necessary, that the phlebotomist does not puncture with the phlebotomy instrument: but rather cuts with Alnesil.
What if the Basilica does not appear with a manifest appearance, then it is necessary that you displace it, and change to another, or that you seek some branch of it: or you make a phlebotomy in place of it on the arm vein. For it itself is manifest, and cut it with the phlebotomy instrument Alnesil, as we have said.
If however you use the phlebotomy of the same itself, then it is necessary before the tightness of the arm, that you touch the place, until you know the place of the pulse. Then apply a cautery on the spot with an incision, afterwards the arm, and cut the vein with a concave section using a phlebotome of Alnebil, as we have said. And let the cautery not fall through the space from the place of the artery.
And when you see, with you tighten the ligature, an inflammation in the place which you signed with a cautery, then that inflammation is the inflation of the artery: avoid it therefore.
For if you see blood at the phlebotomy jumping, like the urine of a child, and it is subtle blood, red: then know that it is from the blood of the artery. Then draw near, and put your finger on it in a long space, afterwards remove your finger. If the blood is then cut off (for it is often cut off), then tighten the arm, and release it: and let the weak one be cautious of its motion: and let it be above its fear.
And do not move it for some days, until it is healed.
If the blood does not stop, and overcomes you: and your medicine is not present at the moment, then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For the extremities of it contract, and the blood is stopped.
Or take the bark of a fig tree, and crush it: take one half, and bind it with a decent stricture over the place of the vein, with a ligature and poultices until another day.
Then if the blood is stopped, it is good. And if not: then treat it with that which the remembrance of the place has preceded, powder to stop the flow of blood: and the stopping of its blood is not difficult in many dispositions, because of the smallness of the place of the wound, and the possibility of a ligature from the arm.
The bloodletting of the dark vein is a remedy that draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; because it is composed of the royal branch and the cephalic branch, as we have said.
And it is suitable for the bloodletter to be above the fear from the bloodletting of it, because under it is a nerve. If therefore he adds to the puncture of the bloodletting and strikes a nerve, numbness occurs in it, and the healing of it becomes difficult, and perhaps it is not healed entirely.
And this nerve often appears to sensation. If however it is hidden in certain men, and is subtle, and does not show itself: then it is necessary that you place your bloodletting where you will bloodlet with a cut with the Alnesil, and divert the nerve with your strength.
For if the vein is between two nerves, then find the vein in its length.
On the cephalic vein. The bloodletting of the cephalic vein is a remedy because it draws blood from the head and is useful for the ailments of the eyes.
And it is necessary in this vein especially, if you wish, that you bloodlet it by puncturing with an olive branch phlebotome, or with a myrtle phlebotome: because it is the saver of all veins, because there is not beneath it an artery, nor a nerve.
Truly it is suitable for you at the bloodletting, that you divert with the phlebotome the head of the lancet so much, and seek a thinner place: and it does not harm it, if it is not found with the first puncture, that the bloodletting be repeated over it several times.
A true abscess sometimes forms in certain men when not bloodletting is performed on the first percussion, but that abscess does not harm him in any way.
Of the utility of Bloodletting, and the precautions to be observed in it. The quality of Bloodletting, however, and its accidents which arise from it, in whose correction you ought to consider.
First therefore it is of that which you ought to know, that bloodletting is not administered except in conservation of health and its preservation and caution against the approach of illnesses.
And that bloodletting is performed on one of the three veins which are in the elbow, namely the Cephalic and the Fusca and the Basilic. And that bloodletting is done at the beginning of Spring on an empty stomach, and in Summer, and Autumn, and Winter after digestion, when the signs of fullness appear.
And bloodletting should be done on the first day, or on the third, after three hours have passed from the day.
Bloodletting which is administered in illnesses does not have a determined hour, but when convenience and necessity provoke it, at night or day, and every hour, and every time.
And it is not fitting that children be bled until fourteen years are upon them. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled except from the fourth month up to the seventh. Nor should old men be bled who have already passed sixty years.
When therefore someone wants to be bled for any reason: it is necessary that he cleanse his intestines before bloodletting with a gentle clyster, if there is much stool retained in them: so that the veins do not attract from the intestines putrid and harmful superfluities to the principal members.
Nor should blood be let from a patient who is suffering from nausea, that is to say, disgust; nor one who is drunk or vomiting from intoxication, until that is removed from them. Nor the weak with dropsy.
And let blood be taken again after colic passion, and vomiting, and flux of the belly, and excessive coition, and labor, and exercise, and wakefulness, and fasting, and anything that dissipates virtue from the bodily or animal substance.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors beforehand, whether the blood is thick with foods and drinks and medicines, if it is possible for him.
Then let a bath be taken, if nothing prevents it, or let exercise be used, so that the blood may be thinned.
And let bloodletting be applied to his chest, around the third day, as we have said: and take care, that his chest may be evacuated on that day from all bad animal accidents: such as anguish, and anger, and fear: and from all bodily accidents, such as labor, and pain, and excessive coition, and similar things.
And let him have present the things with which usage has created a habit in men from aromaticity, and fragrance, and rubbing and similar things to the quantity of possibility.
