Then let the bloodletter sit on a cushion, which is higher than the cushion of the one to be bled: afterwards let him draw out his arm, and rub it with his hand twice or three times. Then let him tie the bandage with a strap, and turn it twice.
And let the tightening be moderate, because when the tightening is uneven, or excessive in tightness, then it prohibits the flow of blood: and in looseness, it also prohibits the flow of blood.
Then after the constriction, it is necessary that the hand of the bloodletter rubs his hands together, until the veins become swollen and are apparent to the sense.
Then the bloodletter shall wipe the vein with a little oil, belonging to himself or an old one.
Then he shall place his index finger of the left hand on the vein under the location where he wants to perform the bloodletting, so that the vein does not escape and the stroke is made. For there is a vein which when you come upon it during bloodletting slips away like a bowstring: and of them there is one which is full of wind: and when you place the bloodletting instrument on it, it contracts under the blade, and the bloodletter is deceived, and the vein does not show itself: and if it does show itself, it is constricted: therefore it is necessary that the bloodletter be steady, and be solicitous in all these things.
Then he shall put down the bloodletting instrument. If then the vein is exposed from one stroke, then it is good. And if not, then he shall try again another time in that location, or above it with speed, provided the place is not damaged.
What if it becomes inflamed, or becomes weak and feeble, then let it be for a day, or two days: and do not constrict the bandage. For perhaps heat will attract the inflammation. And let him not enter a bath.
Then he shall repeat the bloodletting, if it is loved.
What if the opening is small, or the blood flows subtly and thinly: and so that not a quantity of blood escapes which is excessive: then he shall try the bloodletting instrument in the same opening, according to what is right, and add a little to the operation.
And let him do it with speed, before the place becomes inflamed, for an inflamed place is a problem for many men during a small operation.
If then you see it already suppurating, then do not repeat on it altogether, for it does not excuse anything with you. And put something upon it from the dregs of oil: because it will alleviate the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly administer the dregs of oil in all your bloodletting, which you let blood to prohibit the flow of blood: and they call it Tyriac Alfarach, and Sagzenea, when it is put from one of them on the place.
For blood is refined and dissolved, when it is thick.
What if it happens in the place of bloodletting a large abscess, for it often happens, especially to him who is not accustomed to bloodletting except by chance, or is opening a small vein, then quickly and put on the place a sponge soaked in hot water and salt: and bind it for one hour: because it will dissolve.
And it is necessary that you do that after the blood has come out from the same vein, when its flow is complete, or from another vein.
What if something black or green remains in the place after days, it does not harm: and if you wish, put on it some aloe, or a solution of myrrh: or something from the juice of mint, that is, calamint or similar to it.
And often indeed abscesses and swellings occur with bloodletting of the Basilica.
Therefore put your hand on it, then if you find softness with compression on it, then that swelling is bad. Beware therefore, that you put on it something of what we have said. For perhaps blood of the artery will flow from it.
But make for it an plaster with that in which is astringency, so that the place may be indued. Then cure itself with the remainder of the treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary, that you extract from every man blood according to the quantity of his virtue. And what appears from the color overcoming the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it exit, until it becomes red: and similarly if you see it thick: then let it exit, until it becomes subtle: and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for him who is full, strong, and needs extraction of blood twice, that the phlebotomy of the vein be amplified, and the phlebotomist be skilled: and in him who is weak, let it be to the contrary of this.
And it is necessary, that he extract it in the veins, and that the opening be narrow: and better that which is administered in the phlebotomy of the vein is, that it be a cross-section, not a puncture.
And this mode of phlebotomy is safe from the flow of blood, and from the cutting of the nerve: and it is more to be praised, and safer than phlebotomy by width and length.
And to whom it is customary to faint at phlebotomy of the syncopis, it is necessary that he eat before phlebotomy moderate bread infused in water of granate or of musk, if it is warmed: and extract for him from the blood in three veins, or four.
And if it is a cold complexion, then let him receive before phlebotomy bread infused in syrup of myrrha, or in syrup of honey aromatized with spices, or in syrup aromatized with perfume.
What if it happens a faint at phlebotomy, and is the cause of its exit of much blood: then it is necessary, that he take in drink water of flesh, and subtle perfume syrup: and that he use aromaticity with galbanum, and that his breast be lined with it: and that he use the remaining things which we said in the division in the chapter of faint from evacuation.
Whoever wishes to purify his arm and release his blood secondly: it is necessary for him whose phlebotomy is due to abundant evacuation, and the virtue of it is weak, that he should release blood little by little according to the quantity of virtue in continuous days.
Who indeed wishes to purify his arm and release his blood secondly, and his body is strong: then let him do that after seven hours, or nine from his first phlebotomy.
Whoever wishes to attract blood from his body to the opposite part to which it inclines: it is necessary that he should be purified on the second day, or on the third.
In whom there is indeed much blood already heated and sharpened, and it causes fever to occur: it is necessary that blood should go out from it in a single vein, and a large quantity be extracted from it: and the opening be widened until syncope occurs: after which you have observed all conditions of phlebotomy: and that you place your hand on his pulse at the course of the blood, so that death does not occur in place of syncope. For it often happens, when the phlebotomist is ignorant, and negligence occurs.
And it is not necessary when the vein has been closed, and the second release of blood is to be made, with the orifice of the vein already closed, and the exit of blood becomes difficult, that it should be pressed upon with violence, and the curved needle extended quickly. For that is a grievous evil, leave it alone, until a second phlebotomy is made.
Or remove with acuteness of phlebotomy that which has coagulated from the blood at the orifice of the vein, and put on it something of salt dissolved in water. And put on it something of tyriac alfarch or sageena, and apply it with gentle compression, until the blood goes out.
If the vein has already become varicose, then leave it, and do not touch it, until the aposteme has settled.
What if necessity provokes to let blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then either perform phlebotomy on it above that place, or on another arm in another vein.
The veins of the arms are bled at the places of the Funis, and Basilica, when they are not found, and are hidden, because it is composed from them.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is, that an weak hand should be put into warm water, until it becomes red and the vein appears clearly.
Then let it be constricted above itself with a bandage of moderate tightening. Then let the phlebotomy be done across gradually, not across the width, nor along the length: and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be where you will phlebotomize above the joint of the hand for a short time.
What if the flow of blood is stopped, then put the hand again in warm water, and let the blood flow in the water, until you obtain your necessity.
If however it is in the days of summer, then you should be spared from putting the hand again in warm water. And for the most part, the flow of blood is put into warm water in winter time.
And the phlebotomy of this vein is salvation to all veins, because there is neither a pulse beneath it, nor a nerve.
The phlebotomy of the right Salvation vein is conferring with the liver's illnesses.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is, that you constrict the hand with a bandage, or your hand, after you have put it into warm water, until the vein becomes inflated, and shows itself to the sense. Then let the bloodletting be done by crossing over. And if you cut it entirely, it will not harm it in any way.
And be careful, lest you wound your hand with the lancet. For under it are the nerves of the fingers stripped of flesh.
Then put the hand into warm water, and let the blood flow out into it. For if you do not put it in warm water, the blood coagulates in the opening of the vein, and is prevented from flowing.
When you have extracted the amount of blood necessary, then put oil and salt on the vein, so that it does not close quickly.
And similarly it is necessary to do with every constricted branch.
The justification of its bloodletting from the left hand is that it contributes to diseases of the spleen: and do similarly in its bloodletting as you did in the second equally.
The justification of bloodletting from the saphena vein is for diseases which are in the lower part of the body, such as diseases of the matrix, and retention of menses, and weaknesses of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and thighs, and similar diseases.
And the quality of its bloodletting is that the weak foot should be immersed in warm water: and friction should be made on it until the veins are swollen: and bind above the joint of the foot with a bandage. And the place of the vein is near the heel, appearing towards the thumb: and many branches ramify from it on the sole of the foot.
Therefore perform bloodletting on its larger branch, or near the heel at its collection: because it is better and more healthful.
For if you perform bloodletting on the sole of the foot, then beware of the nerves which are under it on the surface of the foot.
And place your venesection where you want to make an incision across it, as if you wish to cut it, and let the cutter be a NESIL.
If on the other hand the flow of blood is impeded, then soak its foot in warm water, and let the blood run into it until it evacuates.
What if the bloodletter misses the vein at the first time, then let him repeat with venesection towards the upper parts for a little while, for the location is safe, and you will not take harm from it when you cut a nerve, as we have said.
And similarly with the saphenous vein of the other foot equally.
The location of the sciatic veins, as we have said, is at the heel on the wooded side.
And the indication for venesection of it is, for pain of the hip, when it is because of hot blood.
And the quality of its venesection is, that an weak person should enter the bath and be bound: and let the leg be constricted from the side of the hip up to above the heel four fingers, with a subtle long thread: for it does not appear unless because of that.
When therefore it appears, then venesect it according to whatever ingenuity is possible for you, or according to the crossing: and it is better, or that you cut it with an incision, or that you section it. For its location is salvageable, and it itself is hidden very much in many men.
For its location is salvageable, and it itself is hidden very much in many men. What if you do not find it, and it does not appear to the sense at all: then venesect some branch of it, and it is that which appears on the back of the foot, and turned towards the ear: and beware of the nerve, and make the blood flow in quantity as you wish.
Then loosen the constriction, and put a bandage over the place of venesection, and constrict the place. It will heal quickly.
Of letting blood, and the quality of its use.
Cauteries are made from horns, and from copper, and from glass.
And bloodletting is done in two ways. With scarification and the extraction of blood: and the other is bloodletting without scarification.
And this bloodletting which is without scarification, is according to two methods. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And the bloodlettings that are administered with scarification and the extraction of blood, are fourteen places in the body. Of which one is the bloodletting of Alnocrati, and it is at the back of the head: and Alkehel, and it is in the middle of the neck: and the bloodlettings of Alhagdahim, and they are two sides of the neck from both parts together: and the bloodlettings of the jaw, and it is under the jawbone in the lower mouth: and the bloodlettings of the two spaulders: and the bloodlettings of Alachosos above the end of the tail: and the bloodlettings of the two elbows, and they are in the middle of the two arms: and the bloodlettings of the two thighs: and the bloodlettings of the two hams.
And bloodletting indeed does not extract blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that, no virtue falls from them, as is the case with phlebotomy. Nor is it necessary to administer bloodletting, either with fire or without fire, in any of the diseases that occur from overfullness, until the whole body is evacuated.
Which if necessity calls for bloodletting because of disease or because of habit, let us administer it at every hour at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at whatever time it may be.
What is it, that which is from humans who when blood is multiplied in them to the point that it needs extraction through venesection, finds in its head a heaviness and swooning.
And there is one of them who finds fullness and redness in his face, head, and neck. And there is one of them who finds itching in his face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in his eyes.
And there is one of them who scratches the place of his venesection. And there is one of them whose sneezing is multiplied. And there is one of them who finds the taste of blood in his mouth: or it gathers on his tongue, and he spits out blood. And there is one of them whose sleep is multiplied. And there is one of them who sees in his sleep blood and redness, and killings, and wounds, and things that are similar to those.
When we see any of these things, and it is specifically in the third quarter of a month, we prescribe that venesection be done, after two or three hours have passed.
The indication for venesection of the neck is: since it brings about heaviness in the head, and what flows to the eyes. But it is necessary that it be after purging of the whole body.
And this venesection is in place of bloodletting of the head.
And it is not permitted that it be used by one who is of a cold complexion, or in whom there is a flux. For it brings to him great harm.
And because of that, it is not fitting that the elderly use it, and those in whose head there are cold ailments.
He who does it frequently over it, brings about for himself forgetfulness.
And on account of that it is necessary to warn the person performing the procedure, that he should place his hand with the veins for a short time in a lower position, fearing the generation of oblivion.
The application of the cup or Alkehel is in place of phlebotomy of the dark vein and the royal vein, and on account of that it brings together the spasm and constriction of breathing, and the fissure of the instrument of breathing, and coughing, and repletion.
And it is necessary that the cup of Alkehel be applied for a short time, because if it is placed below, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
The application of the cup, indeed, brings together pains that occur in the head, and diseases of the eyes, and half headaches, and perforations, and pain in the roots of the teeth: and it is in place of royal phlebotomy.
And it is necessary to warn the one performing the cupping, that he should not press his hand with the scarification, so that he does not cut an artery and cause a flow of blood.
The application of the cup under the chin brings together ulcers in the mouth and corruption of the gums and similar things from ailments which are in the mouth: and it is in place of the phlebotomy of the two lips.
The application of the cup to the shoulders brings together the disease of alchafacham, which occurs from congestion and heat.
The application of the cup to the inner parts of the two armpits brings together the substitution of phlebotomy of the three veins, the royal, the dark and the cephalic: because it itself attracts blood from all those small veins which are in the flesh: and those small veins attract from other thicker veins, until the attraction reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary to warn the one performing the cupping, that he should not proceed far with the scarification: because the place is bare of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
A poultice is applied to what is swollen above the anus, and it relieves hemorrhoids and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary to instruct the applier of the poultice that it should be large and made of air, because the location requires strong suction, and subtle poultices are easily torn, and a large scarification should be scarified.
Poultices of the legs diminish with manifest diminution: because they draw blood from the whole body, and relieve old pains in the kidneys, and uterus, and bladder: they provoke menstruation, and relieve a flux, and amenorrhea: and they take the place of phlebotomy of the two saphena veins, and aloxocheni.
However, they greatly weaken the body, and cause syncope to occur in many men.
And the benefit of poultices of the vein behind the knee is similar to the benefit of poultices of the two legs.
The quality of the position of poultices is that you should put a poultice in a vacuum, and draw with tempered suction: and you should not prolong the positioning of poultices, but rather put them quickly, and remove them quickly, so that humors come to the place equally: and do not cease to repeat and continue this, until you see the place already red and inflamed, and the appearance of blood appears: then scarify, and repeat the suction little by little. Then consider the disposition of bodies.
For one who is of tender flesh and rare pores among men, it is necessary to scarify the place once, no more: so that the location is not ulcerated.
And instruct the applier of poultices to amplify the scarification and make it a little deeper: and to temper the suction with facility, speed, and its own motion.
What if there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary to scarify the place twice. In this case, what is first investigated is the subtlety of blood and its thinness: and in the second, what is investigated is the scrutiny of extracted coarse blood.
If indeed the blood is very foul, then let there be made a third scarification: so that it may follow the last.
And in the end, when we wish to extract blood little by little, or for a short while, one scarification is sufficient for us. What if we wish the extraction of much blood, we make many scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is coarse: then it is necessary that there be made a deep scarification: and the limit of equality in the scarification is the depth of the skin.
What is that thing which ought to be administered from oils at the placing of the bandages, and what the bandage covers.
In that case in which the skin is coarse, hard, dark, and its pores are constricted, it is necessary that the places of the bandage be anointed with oils which are opening, loosening, resolving: if it is in the summer season, such as is the oil of cloves, or the oil of violet, or the oil of sweet almonds, or the oil of the seed of gourd.
And if it is in the winter season, then the oil of narcissus, or the oil of lily, or the oil of chamomile, or sabbatical: and similar things.
If indeed the excess is coarse, cold: then let the oil be bitter, or the oil of balsam, or the oil of abeny, or the oil of dill: and their similars.
If indeed the bandage is of large pores, of tender flesh: then it is necessary that it be prohibited from oil, and it is necessary that these places of the bandage be washed after the bandaging with rose water, or with cold water, or with solatri water, or with gourd water, or with purslane water: and their similars.
He whose blood is of much humidity, should wash the ventoses with vinegar, or with water of myrtle, and sumach: and similar things.
Whose superfluities are gross, should wash his ventoses with old wine, or with water of samsuc, or decoction of anet, or camomile: and similar things.
And it is fitting that the ventosation should be received in a bath, or in a dressing room of a bath, and it is fitting that it should be administered after the exit from the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And it is not fitting that anyone should sleep after ventosation.
What is it that one ought to be governed by, the Ventosed or the one who will be bled before ventosation.
And after that it is fitting that you consider first: then if the Ventosed or the one who will be bled is choleric, and there are conquering on his blood acuteness and inflammation: then it is fitting that he should receive cooling things, such as granulated mucilage: and also an enema with vinegar and lettuce, and secaniabin, and iuleb: and similar things.
And let his food be soft porridge, and meat of boiled eggs, and seasoned foods with verjuice: and similar things.
And whose complexion is cold, it is fitting that he take in a drink syrup of honey, or of almiua, or alsecaniabin made from seeds: and let him take fragrant wine, which is between old and new: and let him be prescribed to use a small amount of food. And let his food be soft porridge, and cane sugar: and peas, that is, small birds: and the soft porridge of doves.
And it is fitting that the drink on the day of ventosation and phlebotomy should be more than the food: and it is fitting that there should be given in a drink to certain men at the third hour alfaroch, or medicine from musk, that is, muscad, or alesselithe before ventosation: and before phlebotomy, or after: so that the principal members may be comforted, and the blood may be thinned. And it should not be taken in a hot drink.
On inflammation without incision. Inflammations which are without incision, are those that are applied on the liver, and spleen, and gout, and on the navel, and the umbilicus, and the kidneys, and the groin: because these members do not tolerate incision on themselves.
And it is not sought for through them, unless there is an attraction of blood from one member to another member: as we apply a plaster on the breasts in case of a flow of blood from the nose. And we use them to dissolve a cold inflammation from a member into another member: as we apply a plaster on the abdomen and navel. For it rarefies the member, and warms it, and removes the pain for that reason, because it resolves that inflammation.
And it is also applied on the kidneys, when there occurs in them a stoppage or a stone. For by the virtue of its attraction it perhaps opens up the blockage, or extracts the stone from its place.
And it does similarly when it is applied on the liver and spleen for a tertian fever in both of them.
And these plasters should be given to those who are with incision, only so much. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered with warm water full in cases of pleurisy: and that is, so that the plaster is filled, and is large, with hot water alone, or with water in which herbs that are suitable have been decocted.
The form of a plaster administered with fire.
Let the width of its opening be two fingers, according to which we form it: and its quantity in depth be half a palm: and let there be on its side in the place which is around the middle of it, a small hole, according to the quantity that the needle enters: let it be made from copper or brass, of thick edges, smooth, equal, polished: so that it does not harm the member at its position on it: and let there be in its middle a transverse rod of copper or iron, where the candle with fire is placed.
And now this large suture is made, greater than we have described, and a little less, according to the ailments and age of the one who uses it.
For there are different sutures for children and for the thin, than for men and those with large bodies.
The quality of applying this suture with fire on the member, is that you set alight a small piece of linen with fire from a lamp or a small candle made of tallow: and place it above the middle of the transverse rod which is in the middle of the suture: so that the fire burns around the lower part of the suture, so that the body of the weak person does not get burned.
Then it is placed on the member: and the finger is above the hole that I mentioned, until when we hold the suture: while we press, we remove the finger, and vapor will come out over that hole, and the suture will be loosened immediately.
Then apply a small piece of linen to the fire according to its size, and remove it, if you press on it.
In the suture that is administered for the ailment of dropsy with water, there is no transverse rod, nor hole: and it is not administered, unless it is filled with water, and placed on the member only.
And this is its form.
And this suture, the larger it is, so that it can hold much water, is better.
Of the suspension of bloodletting.
Bloodletting is not administered to many, unless it is in those members where the application of sutures is not possible, due to their smallness, such as the lip, and gums, and similar things. Or in a member stripped of flesh, such as the finger, and nose, and similar things.
And the quality of using them is: that you should use bloodletting from the veins that are in clear and clean waters, free from corruption.
Then they should be soaked day and night in fresh water until they are softened and nothing remains in their stomach. Then the body should be treated at first with bloodletting and with applications to the belly. Then the weak member should be cleansed until it becomes red. Afterwards they will be placed upon it.
When they are filled and fall, and it is possible to suck the place with an application, then it is almost the end of the treatment: and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, and rubbed, and squeezed out.
What if the flow of blood is prolonged after a fall of the blood vessels, and it is that which flows back: then soak a linen cloth in cold water, and put it on top, until the flowing back is cut off.
What if the blood increases, then powder on it aseh powder, or alum, and similar things from astringents: until the blood is cut off.
Or they will be placed on the place half-cooked broad beans: and left until the beans stick to the place, for the blood is cut off.
And it is necessary if a repetition of bloodletting is needed, when it is possible, to change them.
If however the blood vessels are forbidden from suspensions, then the place should be cleansed with fresh blood. Or apply a sharp point to the places, until something comes out of the blood. Then they should be applied, for as soon as they feel something from the blood, they stick at once.
When therefore you use them so that they fall, powder on them something from powdered aloe, or salt, or ash: and they will fall at once.
This chapter is also often necessary in the art of Medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of accidents in bones.
Know, O sons, that the foolish medical men and the common people mock this chapter, and who have never inspected any ancient book, nor read a letter from it.
And for this reason it happens that this method is deprived of the sciences in our land. And I did not find anything good in it at all, and I would not have attained what I attained if not for the length of reading ancient books, and study beyond understanding them, until I extracted the knowledge from them.
Then I adhered to experimentation and study for the duration of my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this Chapter all that my knowledge contains: and on which my experimentation has passed, after I had shortened it for you, and freed it from the occupation of prolongation, and shortened it at the end of shortening, and showed it at the end of declination: and formed in it many forms of instruments that are administered in it, when it is with the addition of declination, as I did in the two preceding Chapters.
Chapter II.
It is fitting that we say in the prologue of this Chapter, the sum of the discourse and chapters, for the understanding of which the artisan is compelled at first to stand on their truth: namely so that you, and those who are prompt, may know or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture or dislocation or torsion or fall happens to someone, then it is necessary first to bleed him and/or to treat him, both at the same time if nothing prevents it, such as weakness of strength, or if he is one of those persons who has a fever, or an old decrepit man, or if it is a time of violent heat or of violent cold.
Then let him be content with his food of cooling herbs, and flesh of birds, and of fish: and prohibit wine, and fat meats, and fill him with foods and every food that fills the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from the hot aposteme, and you do not fear the effusion of material to the member: then let the weak man return to his first regime with which his habit was accustomed.
When, therefore, the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the weak man be fed with nutritious foods of much nourishment, thick, heavy, in which there is viscidity: such as food made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and cabbage, and feet and hocks of geese, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and similar things. For with this regime a consolidation of the fracture happens more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in early old age and decrepit, it is not possible that they knit and consolidate according to their nature always, because of the dryness of their membranes: and they consolidate and knit which of them are at the end of softness, such as the bones of little boys.
Truly, however, nature joins to the broken bone from every part something like glue, in which there is thickness, with which it adheres and binds itself, until they knit together and bind together, until they come to the end of strength and firmness, as they were at first, until nothing prevents them from their operations: and for this reason it is necessary that you put nourishing food for the weak man, in which there are thickness, and viscidity, and grossness, as we said.
And you should know that there are different types of fractures, according to the diversity of organs, because a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the head bone, and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone, and similarly all the other organs are different from one another, and I came with remembrance of each species of fracture exposed in its distinct Chapter, separate from another. And the species of bone fractures also differentiate among themselves.
For it becomes a fracture that is curved, besides which stars occur in it. And when a fracture occurs according to the length of the bone, and its fractures are jagged, that is, stars, and additions proceed, as they are in a reed, when it is adapted to a knife for writing. And not proceeding, and it is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head, and it is a small fissure fracture.
And each species has its own ingenuity, or proper instrument for its restoration, according to which comes the remembrance of it, distinct in its places.
And from that which the fracture of the bone is recognized, is its twistedness and its own prominence: and its appearance to the senses, and its sound when you press it with your hand.
When therefore the twistedness is not apparent in its place, nor sound upon your touch when you touch the bone with movement, nor does the weak one find great pain: then there is no fracture there, but it is possible that there be a twist or small fissure: therefore it is not necessary to move it with hand and compression at all, but rather put on top of it from medicines, which remembrance came after the moment that it is suitable for the place: then let the strict bandage be tightened.
And you should know that the place when it is curved and splits into two, besides which stars occur in it, nevertheless every part is moved away from its comparison: then it is necessary for you to begin from your hour to rectify it, equalizing it before hot apostema occurs to it.
If a hot abscess happens to it, then leave it for some days until the abscess quiets. Then treat it equally according to whatever method is possible from gentleness and ingenuity.
And you should know that the restoration of it and its equalization is easier than of a bone, in which occur fractures.
Then bind it, according to what the memory of it will indicate to you.
If there are fractures in the bone, then extension of this broken member is necessary from two parts, if it is the hand or foot: or with your hand, if it is a small member: or with two cords, or with a cord and a hand.
And let your position be where you place the member on an equal place, according to its natural figure, until when the parts of the broken member are extended: then try, that you make those additions return with all ingenuity, and according to whatever method you can in their places: and beware according to your strength, that pain or injury does not happen to the weak one through your operation: and try according to your strength, that you apply one of the two bones with its companion, according to better forms.
And it is necessary at that time, that you touch and feel it with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it, and equalize it according to the quantity of your ability.
And beware of violent extension and strong compression, as many stupid ones do. For they often cause a hot abscess to happen through their operation, or a contraction in the member: as we have already seen that from their operations many times.
Then connect after equalization, and decent operation, and straitness of that member, and quiet, and tranquility: and warn the weak one above it. And let it heal so that it does not move during the time of its watchfulness and its sleep with regard to its revolution and its agitation and with regard to its ejection and all its movements with its final strength. And let it heal so that the position of the limb is not a position in which pain follows. Which is, that when it feels pain or injury in the disposition of the limb's position, then it is necessary to change it to a different disposition from that in which it does not feel pain.
And let it heal with that, so that its position is equal and straight, so that crookedness does not occur in the limb when it is restored.
And the quality of the fracture of the broken limb is according to which I will describe.
Know that fractured limbs differ in their smallness and size and shape.
Therefore, those of them which are small, such as the arm and finger and elbow and similar things, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft cloth, fine. Those of them which are large, such as the hip and thigh, it is necessary that there be a wrapping on them of broad, hard cloth.
For a broad bandage connects the large limb and binds it on every side with equal constriction, so that it does not press upon it unevenly.
Then begin after your adjustment to put a plaster suitable for it on the soft cloth over the place of the fracture itself. Then begin to wrap a bandage over the place of the fracture itself with three or four wraps, according to what the limb deserves, and bind your hand firmly with the bandage for a short time.
Then go up with it to the upper part from the place of the fracture and bind less tightly than was your constriction, with which you bound the fractured place. Then stretch yourself with an involution from the place of the fracture for a little while, and gradually soften the stiffness, until you take from the healthy place a large thing.
Then take another cloth, and involution it again over the place of the fracture with involutions. Afterwards lead with an involution to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be on the tightening of the involution and its softening, according to what we said in the first involution above.
Then put between the involutions a piece of flax or cloth, with which the crookedness of the fracture is equalized, if there is crookedness in it: and if not, you do not put anything in it.
Then involution another cloth over it. Afterwards equal the stars made from the plaster directly over these involutions, if there is not an inflammation in the place, nor a hot abscess.
If however there is an inflammation or an abscess in it: then put on it what will calm that abscess and take away the inflammation: and leave it for some days. Then bind over it the plasters at that time.
And let the plasters be from the middle parts of latten cane, scraped, formed wisely: or let them be from wood from which are made cribs, which are made from pine or gerahid, that is, branches of palm, or calingi: and let the width of each plaster be according to this form, and this same figure.
Truly it is necessary, that the plaster which is put on the same fracture, be a little thicker and wider than the other plasters: and the length of the plaster be according to what is suitable for the member from its size and smallness.
Then bind over the plasters with another cloth, according to the first tightening, the same. Then bind from above with threads in the middle, according to what we said about the dressing: and it is, that your dressing should be above the place of the fracture more: and the more it is elongated from the fracture, the dressing is less.
And it is fitting, that the threads should be moderate in thickness and fineness, and should be of soft flax.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the operation of foolish surgeons who put their threads of flax twisted into strings, it is a great mistake: for the dressing falls with them beyond the quality.
And very fine threads are not suitable either: for you will not achieve through the dressing with them that which you wish.
And it is not fitting, that it should be between stitch and stitch less than a finger.
What if a weak person is injured by the ends of the stitches after the dressing on healthy places: then put under them from soft lint or wool, anointed with salve, until nothing is injured from that.
When, however, there is a fracture with a wound, and a disruption occurs in the skin, I will come to it with discussion of it in the second chapter according to its uniqueness.
And know that not every broken member needs to be bound with stitches from the first day. What is, that when the member is large, it is not fitting to put stitches upon it, unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your security from the event of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters, with which the ancients restored, which are put on a fracture and dislocation.
The method of a common plaster abbreviated, restoring a fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially for women and children: for it is from that upon which neither heat nor cold prevails.
And it is, that you take from the powder of the millstone, and it is the marrow of the flour which hangs in the walls of the mill near the motion of the mill: and you knead it as it is, except that you mix it with the white of the egg: and you put its confection not thick, nor subtle. Then use it.
Another method of plaster for restoring a fracture, and a dislocation, and a twisting. Take mastic and laudanum and acacia and elecampane and mugwort: and juice, of each a similar amount, myrrh and aloe of each a similar amount, and of electam a third of that, let all be pounded, and sifted, and mixed with water of electam: or with the white of an egg, if the plaster is applied to a weak complication. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, it restores broken bones quickly, and confirms many men.
Another method of plaster, which also confirms fractures and twists. Take mugwort and mastic and white plant, of each a similar amount, myrrh and aloe of each a similar amount, acacia a sixth part, Armenian bolus a twentieth part, let all be pounded with much grinding, and sifted, and kneaded with water, or the white of an egg: and administer.
Another method of plaster for joints, and bones separated from their places: and it calms the accidental pain to them, and the cracking of bone, and the fracture. Let lanolin be applied, and submerged in vinegar, and oil cooked: and put upon the place.
And in this plaster there is not virtue of restoration, but it is good for calming a hot abscess: and it properly expels pains.
Another method of plaster, which restores a broken bone. Take the leaves of the fig and the leaves of the poppy, and pound all, and make a plaster from both.
The method of abbreviated plaster, administered for the restoration of the fracture of a bone: and when you wish to resolve a remaining postulate. Take the root of the altea, and chamomile, and the flowers of violets, and the powder of the herb, all in one portion: pound all, and mix with wine, if the member is not heated.
If the member is hot, then mix it with water of moist coriander, or with water, and apply it thereto.
Another method of a stronger plaster for resolution, than the first one, when there occurs a hard postulate during the restoration of a member. Take the root of the altea, and seed of flax, and balsam, and melilot, and samsuch, and flowers of violets, and chamomile, all in one portion, pound all, and mix with water of willow, or with sweet water, or with wine. All that according to the heat of the member, and the tranquility of its heat.
Hippocrates indeed did not say in his book that anything should be put on a fractured member for its restoration, except a cerate made from wax and oil: none other: and he described it as being a mean between thickness and subtlety.
But Galen used to put on a fractured member for its restoration things, in which there is an exciting of heat, such as myrrh, and aloe, and frankincense, and similar to them from those which we have described.
A method of plaster that is useful for a wound and pain. Take mugwort, and cichory, and cut human hair, or dove's wings, and altea, and salt in equal parts: pound, and sift, and mix: and make from it a plaster.
The quantity however according to which it is necessary that the ligature remains and then is dissolved, is, that you consider then, if not severe pain occurs, nor itching, nor the fractured bone moves from its place: then you do not resolve it for many days. If there happens to it in the place intense itching, or troublesome pain, or swelling: then hasten and dissolve it within the hour, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a marine sponge, moist: and submerge it in warm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching is stilled, and the pain is assuaged.
If there happens to it in the place intense itching, or troublesome pain, or swelling: then hasten and dissolve it within the hour, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a marine sponge, moist: and submerge it in warm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching is stilled, and the pain is assuaged. Then let the limb rest for one hour. Then put on it a wool cloth soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind over it for one night, until you are secure from the hot abscess, and the inflammation of the limb is stilled, and its pain is removed.
Then repeat it to a subtle binding and small plaster: and do not bind it with your first constriction, but subtly, until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess and redness and pain and swelling and all accidents are entirely removed, and you apply plaster and binding, then repeat it as you did at first equally.
If none of the things we have spoken of happen to the limb, then do not dissolve it unless after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is left for twenty days, all of that which appears to you from the solution of the limb, as we have said, until when the fracture adheres and the coagulation of flesh approaches over it: add then also in the binding more than your whole first binding: and add also in the thickening of the weak body, according to what its remembrance has preceded.
If you see the place of the fracture is already becoming dry and withered more than it should be then you should know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Apply a warm poultice to it at every place where you reduce the fracture and let it be thus for three days, and alleviate the tight bandage for a little while.
Through this operation nourishment runs to the limb and it heals quickly.
What foolish restorers do when a limb is fractured and not restored at first as it ought to be, but instead is restored according to its deformity, is an error in their operation and a great danger: and if it were correct, the ancients would have said so in their books and would have operated with it.
And I have not seen any trace of them at all, and it is better that it is not done.
On the fracture of a wound on the head.
The species of fractures are various and its shapes are many: and the causes of it are distinct.
For there is a fracture that occurs from a blow with a sword: and the blow removes the bone as far as it reaches to the siphac, which is under the bone, just like an axe does in wood: and because of this it is named this species of fracture Axial.
Or the sword strikes a part of the bone and strikes its surface, and the cut does not penetrate to the end of it: and this species of fracture is named Kalan, that is, Absolute Separation.
And there is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture that is a crushing or bruising: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which now approaches the periosteum, which is under the bone: or it is on the surface of the bone: and there is a wound of these two fractures also, either small or large.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone in the fineness of a hair: and it is a small fissure, and because of that this type of fracture is called Capillary.
And there is a fracture which occurs from a fall, or a blow with a stone, and similar things, which cause the lamina of the bone to penetrate to the interior, and it becomes concave in that place: just as happens to the caldarii of copper, when a blow occurs to them, and a part of them penetrates to the interior.
And it happens most often in the heads of moist bones, such as are the heads of children.
And there are for all these types of fractures treatments separated, and I will come with a reminder of the whole thing in its own place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by detection upon them, and an inquiry into them with probes, and the removal of corrupted flesh which is upon them.
The species of Capillary fracture is recognized by this, that it is detected on the bone, and wiped away, and anointed upon it with a plaster: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, consider first the accidents of the weak person: then if you see from the accidents something that signifies a manifest sign of fear, such as vomiting of bile, and stretching, and loss of intellect, and interruption of voice, and fainting, and acute fever, and protrusion of the eyes, and redness of them, and similar things to these from accidents in many conditions, without doubt it is not cured: and if you see accidents not terrifying you, and you hope for health: then begin the treatment of it.
Which is, if the wounded person comes to you immediately after being wounded: and it is in winter days, then it is necessary that you should strive for the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every condition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you remove the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone from the bandage becomes rotten, and those accidents which we have described happen.
What if the fracture of the bone has already reached the bandage touching the brain, and is with damage, and pulling: then it is necessary that you cut the damaged and pulled part, according to what I will tell you.
And it is that you shave the head of the wounded, and uncover the bone as much as is possible for you, and according to the shape of the wound, and what is least above the weak part.
What if there occurs to you during the uncovering of the bone a flow of blood, or a hot abscess: then apply to it what is necessary. And it is that you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil: and make the wound rest, until the abscess settles, and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin with the cure of the bone, and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method indeed is, that you cut the bone with a strict razor-shaped incisor: and this is its form.
Then administer another incisor wider than the first one, but only for a short time: and this is its form.
Then administer also another incisor even wider also than the second one.
And the second method is, that you have a number of different incisors: some of which are wider than others, and some of them shorter than others: and let them be sharpened at the ends, and let them be made from hardened iron or steel: and use facility in striking on the incisor, so that it does not strike the head, and harm it.
If the bone is strong and hard, then it is necessary that you perforate around it before you apply the incision instrument, with non-penetrating awls which are called non-penetrating awls; and they are not called thus, except that they themselves do not penetrate beyond the limit of the bony tissue to what is behind it: because the awl has a round extremity above what is under its pointed head, similar to a margin, and a small circle prevents submersion and penetration of the thickness of the bone.
And it is suitable for you to take a large number of these awls, each one of which suits the thickness of the bone, until there is an awl available to you for every skull: whose pointed extremity's quantity in length and width is, according to the quantity of the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three species of awls, or trephines.
The quality of perforating around a fractured bone is: that you place the awl on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has already been penetrated. Then exchange the awl to another place, and put what is between every hole a quantity of thickness of the radius, or around it.
Then cut with incision instruments what is between every hole in the bone: and make it thinner than you can above that from the fineness of lightness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, either with your hand, or with some other one of the instruments which you have prepared for it: such as pincers and subtle borers.
And it is necessary that you proceed with all caution, lest the awl or incision instrument touch anything of the brain.
When you have perforated around the bone and it has been separated from the brain, if it was continuous with it, and you extract it, it is necessary that you scrape and smooth out the extremity of what remains in the bone with another instrument similar to an incision instrument. The needle should be thin and finer than those of other surgeons.
What remains from the small bones and sawdust, then take it with ease, along with what you have among your instruments. Then treat the wound afterwards with bandages and ointments which we mentioned.
And another method of the operation is easy, far from fear, which Galen said and praised it with great praise, and this is his statement. It is necessary for you first to begin to remove the piece of bone from the place where the fracture is most violent and most apparent, until when you have removed that piece and placed under it the extremity of this grooved incisor, of which this is the shape.
And the grooved part does not cut gently, and its pointed part on both sides of it proceeds along the length, as you see, so that the grooved part is attached to the saw, with its pointed face cutting into the bone.
Then strike the incisor on one side with a small hammer, until you cut the whole bone with ease, as it turns out, and you will be safe from the accident that you fear, namely.
And if the doctor were not more stupid than men and more ignorant of it, nor asleep, then if something remained attached to the bone from the bandage in certain places, he would cut it away from it with the extremity of the grooved incisor itself, and free it from it with ease. For it is freed from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the bandage, and what is cut away from the bone is its surface, and remaining in it are roughness and subtle sawdust, then it is necessary that you scrape away that roughness and remove those sawdust with fine rasps which you have prepared in a number of different quantities, so that it is possible for you to apply them in every place more conveniently and more correctly, according to what the operation leads you to, and the bone itself, and its shape. And it is necessary that you manage with a rasp, which in the first place has a wider tooth than those of other rasps. Then use afterwards something more subtle than it.
And it is necessary that you manage with a rasp, which in the first place has a wider tooth than those of other rasps. Then use afterwards something more subtle than it. And do not cease to make that a second time in succession until you reach making something thinner and more subtle than all of them.
In each and every one of the remaining small fissures or tiny little fractures, it is necessary that you administer care according to what leads to its correction.
And it is a thing which is not hidden from everyone, that in this art there is something to be studied, and it stands upon what we have written, and we show in great fractures.
If indeed a piece of cloth is detected and retained when you extract the bone: then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth, according to the quantity of the wound, and soak it in rose oil, and put it on the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth doubled or tripled, and soak it in wine and rose oil, and put it on the first cloth: and do that as easily as you can over it, so that it does not press on the spleen.
Then administer over it a wide bandage: and do not bind it, except in the amount that will hold the wound so much: and leave it with this care for one day, or two days, until you are secure from a hot abscess. Then undo it, and administer after that medicines with which there is a dry excess, such as the roots of lily, and flour of herbs, and powder of frankincense, and aristolochia, and similar to them: and it is that you make from these medicines a powder, which will be powdered over the wounds as they are dry.
And universally administer on it every medicine of a property which is that it cleanses and does not bite. And beware according to your virtue, that the wound is in your cure clear: nor is there in it filth of ointment, nor foulness.
And beware according to your virtue, that the wound is in your cure clear: nor is there in it filth of ointment, nor foulness. And do not allow the pus to gather in it at all: for when pus gathers on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts it, and putrefies it, and a great evil happens to the weak after that; and now it happens in the cerebral membranes, when the mouth is cleansed from it, especially when the care of it is neglected, blackness on its surface.
Consider then, that if there happen to the weak the accidents which we have said, you should know, that it is pernicious without a doubt.
And if the blackness does not happen, except from the medicine which is put on it, and it is in the virtue of that medicine, that it makes that blackness: then you should take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and anoint the cloth with them. Then put it on the cerebral membrane: afterwards cure it with the species of cure which are suitable, until it is healed.
On the restoration of the nose when it is fractured.
Know that the nose is not fractured except when its two upper parts break at the same time, or one of them: because it itself has two bones: for the lower part of it is cartilaginous, does not fracture, and nothing happens to it except bruising, and twisting, and deviation.
If one of its two parts is fractured, then you should insert a small finger into the nostril, and straighten that fracture with your index finger and thumb, until the nose returns to its natural figure: and this should be done with skill. And beware, that pain does not happen to the weak because of your operation.
What if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and does not reach to that finger then it is necessary that it be adjusted by a small thickness of the radius.
If the fracture is in two parts, then make it similar to that and restore it immediately on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day with rest of a warm apostume.
Then insert into the extremity of the nose a linen pledget from a linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part: or insert two pledgets if the fracture is in two parts: and let there be in the pledget a thickness of such quantity that it fills the nostril.
And some of the ancient restorers have said that the pledgets should be soaked in butter and changed every day. That however does not seem to me, but rather it is necessary that the pledget be soaked in the white of an egg mixed with powder of meal. Then leave the pledget until the bone becomes firm and the cartilage becomes hardened.
And sometimes a feather of a goose is inserted into the nose in place of a pledget, after which a soft cloth is wrapped over it: and it is done so that it holds the fracture of the nose more vigorously, and so that it does not prevent the weak from inhaling.
And this is not a necessary thing. If you want, do that: and if you want, do pledgets.
What if it happens to the nose again while you are operating with a warm apostume, plaster the nose with a cerate, or with a paste mixed with rose oil, or with a little of dyaculum.
If however the warm apostume does not happen, then it is necessary that you plaster it with flour of sesame and powder of frankincense mixed with the white of an egg. Then put on top of it a linen cloth, and do not tie the nose with anything at all.
If the bones of the nose are slightly fractured or broken, then it is necessary to split over them, and extract them with instruments that suit them. Then sew up the wound, and cure it with what promotes flesh, and consolidates it with suitable plasters or ointments.
What if there happens to be a wound between the nostrils, then it is necessary to cure it with threads: and administer a tube of lead, until it heals.
On the restoration of the lower jaw, when it is broken apart.
When the lower jaw is broken apart and its fracture is not with a wound, then consider if the fracture of it is only a slight defect and does not break into two, and is curved inwardly: then the treatment of it is easy.
It is necessary, therefore, if the fracture is in the right part, to insert the index finger of the left hand into the lower mouth: similarly if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand: and push with it the curvature of the fracture from within with ease to the outside with your other hand from the inner side of the bone, and straighten it with that.
What if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary to administer extension from both parts, according to straightness: until its equalization is possible.
If however there has already occurred a shaking or disruption of the teeth, then tie them with a thread of gold, silver, or silk. Then put on top of the fractured jaw a cerate. Then put on top of it a double-folded cloth, and put on top of the cloth a large splint made to fit: or a piece of leather sole equal in length to the jaw.
Then bind it from above, according to the preparation you make for its bandage, and let its application be suitable until it is not destroyed: and order the weak person to have tranquility and quiet: and put his food in soothing sips.
But if you think that something of its shape has already changed in some way: then hasten, and release it on the third day. Then correct what has been changed in it, and plaster it with dust of rose powder, or with the flour of barley, after you have removed the hair: and put a soft wool over the plaster.
So long as that plaster remains attached to it, and the bone's arrangement is not changed, leave it, do not remove it, until it is healed: or bind the fracture. For this fracture is often bound for three weeks.
If meanwhile a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in its treatment, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider. For now a fragment is already separated from the bone, or from the edge: then be subtle in this, so that you remove those fragments of the edge with what suits you in the removal of them by means of instruments.
If indeed the opening of the wound is narrow, then widen it with a lancet according to the quantity of your necessity.
Then when you have removed those fragments, and none of them remain, or then the opening of the wound, if it is wide: and if not, put on it some plasters that suit it, and let it heal, until it is healed.
On the restoration of the Jaw, when it is broken.
The Jaw is indeed broken most often from the front part near the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it either breaks and separates into two, besides which accidents happen to that fissure: and it is easier for the restoration of it.
Or those things that happen to the bone of the fissure: and it is more difficult for the restoration.
Or it is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation in it when there is a fracture without a wound, is that you should have two attendants present, so that one of them may hold the help that follows the fractured Clavicle, and the other stretch the neck towards the part.
Then equal the fracture with your fingers, until its form is made, according to what is necessary: and it should not be in eminence, nor concave.
What if you indicate extension more, then it is necessary that you put under the weak little cloth a sphere of cloth or wool: and its size should be according to the quantity of your necessity: and extend and elevate the Clavicle, and bind the sphere with your hand, until it equals the fracture, according to what is necessary.
What if you cannot draw the extremity of the Clavicle to the outside: because it penetrates to the depth: then it is necessary that the weak one should lean on his neck: and you put under his shoulder a middle-sized pillow, and an attendant presses his shoulder downwards, until the bone of the Clavicle which is in the depth rises upwards: and then rectify the fracture, and equal it with your fingers.
If you feel a false movement from the Clavicle, and it moves: then it is necessary that you bind over it, and extract that false movement with ease.
If it is retained in the bone, then make an incision of it with one of the Incisors which you have prepared for that purpose, after you have placed under the Clavicle an instrument that preserves the siphach: and this instrument is made of wood or iron. And this is its form.
It becomes like a spoon for one who has no spoon.
And let its width be according to that which you need it from the size of the bone, and its smallness. Its length also according to what is possible for you for the operation.
And let it have two ends, as you see: and one end be wide, and the other narrow.
If the disruption of the wound which you cut when you extracted the sequestration of the bone is wide, and you are far from the hot aposteme: then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you are near the hot aposteme: then fill the wound with rags, and poultices according to the quantity of the division of the wound.
If a hot aposteme happens, then soak a cloth in rose oil and vinegar and wine, and put it on.
The stricture of the bone when it is without a wound, and without a cut, is, that you put on the bone a plaster made from flour of millstone with the white of an egg. And put a soft dressing over it. Then put a ball under its nipple, if you need it. Then take a bow, that is, the wheel which the Saracens hold, which should be very long: and let its width be one palm, or about that. Then put a double poultice over the dressing and plaster. Then take a rod from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap the cloth with the rod in the place of the bow that falls on the fracture and place. Then put the splint over the fracture as it is turned, and wrap it around his neck, and under his sound shoulder, and under his weak shoulder: and bind it over every part, and however you see that the bandage holds the fracture, the bandage is sufficient: and it is that which is not hidden from you, and all, so that the star is not removed from above the broken bone. And for this reason, it is necessary that you examine the weak part every day. How often therefore the bandage is loosened, and you see the star already being removed: then correct it, and tighten the bandage.
Then put a pad on the weak part over his back, and put under his shoulder near the weak part a small pillow at night, so that with it his helper is elevated from his side. The fracture of the arm is therefore elevated by elevation of the shoulder.
Or tie his arm to his neck: and do not untie the bandage, unless an accident occurs on account of itching or from a gathering, until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster, if you see that it is suitable for it. And add to the bandage, and diminish it, until it is restored and united.
And the fracture of the arm is bound and strengthened mostly within twenty days: and sometimes it happens in certain men in less than that.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the shoulder blade.
The shoulder blade is rarely broken, and in its broad place: and they are not broken except for their edges.
When therefore it is broken from that place, and it breaks in the middle of it, and it is not recognized except by touch: then according to what is the figure of the fracture, prepare its treatment. And I reduce it to a natural figure in every way possible for you.
Then put on the place a dust of grinding with the white of an egg, and a linen cloth, and put above it a small pillow from a double cloth. Then put on it a broad star made from a subtle board according to the quantity of the entire spatula or a little more.
If indeed under the star there is a concavity in some place of the spatula, then equal that concavity with a linen cloth, until the star descends according to equality.
Then bind above with a long alum paste, a decent constriction, and firmness of the star, until it is not moved from its place.
And consider the ligament in every day. As often as the ligament is softened, bind it, and equal the star if it is moved from its place: and let the weak one lie on his healthy side.
And the spatula is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When this number is completed, then solve the ligament: and you will be in security. For it is one of the bones whose joining is not feared, nor their separation.
If a fragment proceeds from the bone and pricks under the skin: then cut above it, and remove it. And do what we said in the fracture of the jaw from the application of a warm poultice, if anything happens from it.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the breast.
The chest is rarely broken in the middle of itself, and it hardly ever happens to it. Its extremities are most often broken and joined from its accidents.
When its middle is broken, which declines to the lower parts, and a violent pain happens to it, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing: and perhaps it vomits blood, and is declared in the fractured bone, and is not hidden from sensation. The restoration of it is, that the weak one should rest on his back: and put between the spatulas of it a pillow.
Then press his shoulders, and gather the ribs from the two parts or sides with the hands, and reduce them in their equality, according to every manner that is possible for them and is suitable, until the shape of the bone returns, according to what is proper.
Then put on it a plaster and a dressing, and put over it a splint made from a thin board of willow or hazel, or similar things, lightly, after you have wrapped it in a cloth. Then bind it subtly over the fractured bone, so that it is not removed: and see to it with a bandage according to the roundness of the back several times, and tighten it with a suitable constriction.
Then consider the bandage every hour, and as often as it becomes loose, tighten it: and if necessity demands its removal on account of itching, or corrosion which occurs in the place, or pain, or abscess: then hasten, and remove it, and eradicate the plaster, and correct what has happened with the method of its cure.
Then put back the plaster, if you see it is suitable for that face: and attach to it a dressing, until it is healed.
Of the restoration of the ribs, when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur, except in thick places which follow the back, and from the extremities of them on the front side. And damage does not happen to them, except because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from feeling on examination with the fingers.
And its restoration is done in such a way, that you press the fracture with the fingers according to the possible method, until it takes on the shape, according to what is proper. Then apply a plaster, and bind the fractured bone with a splint, if it needs it.
And if the fracture of the ribs is inclined to the interior, then intense pain occurs to the weakened person, and puncture, just like the puncture that occurs to one who has pleurisy: because the bone pierces the membrane, and also difficulty in breathing occurs to them, and coughing, and vomiting of much blood: and this is difficult to cure.
And now the ancients have devised many devices for it: and among them there are those who say that you should apply foods to the weakened person which generate inflammation and flatulence, so that the belly may be inflamed, and distended, and the fracture pushed outward.
We indeed disapprove, that a cause for making a hot abscess does not happen quickly, if it does not occur. But if it has already occurred, then he adds to it.
And some of them said: let a poultice be placed on the swollen place, then sucked with force, and it is similar to reasoning. Truly, it is feared that the poultice will attract the excesses of the swelling to the location, because of the disposition of weakness.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the location be covered with wool which has already been soaked in hot oil: and small pillows are placed between the ribs, until it is full: so that the bandage is equal when you wrap it around according to its roundness.
Then the weakened person should be treated with a cure for pleurisy, from food and medicine.
Since the intense thing constricts the weakened person, which it does not tolerate, and the bone pierces the membrane with a wounding puncture, and we fear for the weakened person: then it is necessary that we cut above the location, and remove the fractured rib. Then let us place on it an instrument that preserves the health, whose memory has gone before: and let us cut the bone with ease, and let us cut it.
Then we shall bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large with a suture: and we shall treat it with plasters, until it heals.
What if a hot aposteme happens in the meantime, then apply it, and pour into rose oil poultices, and put over the place: and let the weak one be treated with that which is suitable for the aposteme.
Meanwhile also let him breathe on the side above that which he sleeps, until it heals.
On the restoration of fractures of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
When a fracture occurs to the bones of the neck, and rarely does it happen to them with an injury: and similarly to the vertebrae of the back also. When therefore that happens to anyone, and you wish to know, whether it will heal or not, then consider. For if you see his hands already softened and numbed and deadened: and he cannot move them nor extend them nor clench them: and when you clench them with your fingers and prick them with a needle he does not feel it and does not feel pain in them: then know that it will not heal, and in most cases it is mortal.
For if he moves them and feels in them a constriction with the fingers and a pricking: then know that the marrow of the bone is safe, and that the weak person will be healed with treatment.
For if it happens to the vertebrae of the back similarly to that, and you wish to know whether it will heal also or not: then consider his feet: then if you see that they are softened, and it happens in them what happened in the hands: afterwards when he lies on his back, flatus and vomiting come out without his will: and when he is turned on his belly, vomiting comes out without his will: and when he is turned on his back and wishes to urinate and cannot: then know that it is mortal. I do not therefore undertake his cure.
If on the other hand nothing happens to him from it, the thing is lighter.
And the treatment of what happens from it is that you apply a dressing of hot aposteme: thus, that you put on the affected spongy part rose oil alone: or with roasted egg whites.
Apply on it that several times a day, until when the hot aposteme is applied: then apply over the place one of the comforting, drying, and constricting plasters, and bind over it with a bandage: and instruct the patient to use quiet and rest. And let him not sleep on the side on which he feels pain, until he is healed.
What if it happens at the place of fracture of a bone, or something that is already separated from it: then it is necessary to cut over the skin, and to remove it. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a suture. Then treat it with some kind of ointment, from the oils, until it is healed.
If on the other hand the end of the bone is broken, and it is the end of the tail: then it is necessary that you insert the thumb of your left hand into the anus, and straighten the fractured bone with your other hand, according to what is possible, and a second adjustment.
Then put on it, and bind it: and treat the wound with the treatment that preceded, until it is healed.
Of the restoration of the nail.
Chapter 11.
The bones of the donkey are rarely fractured. What if they are fractured, then the fracture of them is not, except a comminution at their extremities: and they split along the length, and bend inwardly: and there happens to the patient pain in the place, and puncturing: and the leg which is fractured becomes numb.
And its restoration is that you extend your hand over it, until you are above the fracture in whatever manner its figure is.
If the fracture is only in its extremities, then equalize that fracture, according to what is prepared for you from equalization, until it is assimilated to its natural figure.
If the fracture is in the length, or it declines to the inside: then make the weak one lie on its belly, until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When you have equalized it, put a plaster on it: then put above it a splint of wood or of leather: and bind a bandage over it which you do not fear to change the fracture, nor remove the splint.
And equalize the concavity of them with what fills it, until the bandage takes it according to equality: and instruct the weak one, to sleep on its back, or on its healthy side.
If a hot abscess happens to it: then cease from its extension and its restoration, until the hot abscess has subsided: and put on it what subsides it, according to what has preceded, then return to its restoration: and bind it as is necessary.
If indeed a crack occurs in the bone, or it becomes comminuted, extract.
On the restoration of the fracture of the assistant.
The assistant is that which is from the elbow to the head of the spatula.
If it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to the shape that follows.
And two bandages are bound on the two extremities.
Then let it be suspended from a raised place: and let the weak person sit on a seat. Then he should throw his broken arm over a stick, until it reaches his armpit, in the middle of which let the curved piece of wood be attached.
Then let something heavy be suspended from above it, so that the assistant extends it to the lower parts. Then the physician adjusts the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns, according to what is necessary.
And another method is, that the weak person rests on his neck: and his hand is suspended from his neck with a bandage. Then instruct two assistants, that one of them should hold what is above the fracture with their hands: and that the other should hold the lower part of it, and each of them should pull to their own side.
And if you want it to be a stronger extension, then tie under the fracture with a bandage, and above it with another bandage: and let each of the two assistants pull to their two sides.
If the fracture is near the extremity of the shoulder, then it is necessary that one bandage be placed under the armpit, and another under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that a bandage be placed above that place, and above the elbow itself.
Then adjust the fracture with ease without effort, until when the fracture is adjusted according to what is necessary, and a good application is applied: then tie it, if not a warm abscess occurs.
