If a warm abscess does occur, then loosen the bandage until the seventh day. And place on it a piece of wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess subsides: then tie it: and the method of tying it is, that you put a plaster on the fracture.
Then put a dressing made from new cloth over the plaster. Then he should place his arm on the help itself and put his open hand on his shoulder.
And put a cloth and tie it over the help for the arm, so that the arm stays in place: if nothing prevents you from it, nor is anything altered by you from the fractured bone.
If you fear that something will be altered by you from the fractured bone, then use a splint: and it is, that you put on the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And put between every splint the width of a finger: and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers on each side.
Then tie over the splints a bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage should be tighter over the place of the fracture. And the more the fracture stretches, the less tight the bandage should be.
If you see the splints and bandage as we have said at the hour when you restore the limb immediately, then do it: and if you fear a warm abscess, then remove the bandage and splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the bandage for all three days, so that there does not occur in the place itching, or inflammation, or is prevented by foods reaching the limb, because of the condition from the excess of the tightness. Therefore straighten the whole thing, according to what we have said.
If you are confident that nothing will happen from it, then do not loosen the bandage unless after three days or more.
And let the weak person sleep on their back and their hands on their stomach: and put under the arm a full cushion of wool equally.
And consider at every hour of night or day, that the shape of a fractured limb is not dissolved, nor the bandage loosened. Set it right therefore with all your skill.
And give food to the invalid according to the order which we previously mentioned, so that it should be a subtle diet at first, until the mouth begins to close: then it is necessary that you increase his food.
For it is the custom of the hip and the thigh, that it should be constricted for forty days: then therefore it is necessary that it be loosened, and a bath be administered, and medicine be applied with ointments which restore it.
If the fracture is foul and contused, then you should not loosen the bandage from it, but keep it until fifty days, and until two months.
On the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two little foemen.
Of which one is small: and it is the one that follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is the one that is placed under the small one from the lower part.
Perhaps therefore the larger forearm bone alone breaks, or the smaller one: and perhaps they break together.
When therefore the upper small forearm bone breaks, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower forearm bone breaks, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse when both bones break together.
If therefore the bone that breaks is the upper small forearm bone, then it is suitable for the doctor at its restoration to apply a slight extension with ease, until it becomes equal.
If indeed the large forearm bone is the one that is broken: then it is necessary that a strong bandage be applied.
And it is suitable that he should put a figure of a hand near its restoration and its own extension extended on the pillow. And the thumb of the hand should be higher than all the fingers above: and the joint should be lower than all the fingers or the rest: and let the weak one sit squared upon itself, and let the pillow be before it, or in a straight line with it, in height, so that the weak one does not incur labor.
Then the assistant should extend the arm from its lower part, or his hand, or with a bandage. And another assistant should extend it similarly from above.
Then the physician adjusts the bone until he reduces it to the best shape that he can.
What if there are splinters in the fracture of the bone, then take care to reduce every fragment in its place with your strength.
If indeed a separated splinter appears in it, and it pierces the skin, and there is no hope for its restoration: then sew over it, and remove it according to the method that we have spoken of concerning those things which were mentioned before.
If the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter about that, so take the treatment of it from there.
What if at the beginning of your restoration a hot abscess occurs, then spread a linen cloth made with rose oil and white wax, and let it be a medium between thickness and subtlety: and bind the cloth over it with a subtle bandage, until when the abscess has settled: then remove the wax, and put an prepared plaster made from powdered flour with egg whites. Then put splints, and let the splint that is put on the fracture itself be a little wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most arrangements: whether the fracture is in one forearm or in two forearms at the same time.
Then put your bandage over the place of the fracture, stronger and tighter, and somewhat more with the bandage towards the upper part of the fracture, or to the lower part of it: or put a softer bandage instead of it for a short while, according to what was stated in the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped over the fracture be gentle cloths, moist: and not rough, harsh. And let the thread with which it is tied be a linen one, medium between subtle and coarse, as we have described.
And consider the limb and the bandage after some days. What if something happens which needs correction, such as an itch that happens to the limb: then it is necessary, that you bathe the limb with warm water, until that itch calms down, and you release the limb not constricted, until it calms down, then repeat the bandage.
What if the bandage has already been softened, and the wound has already closed, and similar things to these: then correct that entirely with your skill, and examine again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, on account of the excess of the bandage, then it is necessary that you soften it for a while, and release it for some days, until nourishment runs to it, then tie it.
If however nothing happens to the weak person among those things which we have said, then it is not necessary that it be undone, unless after twenty days, or around that.
Then let the hand of the weak person be suspended at his neck. And let his neck be straight, and let it be protected by its strength from agitated movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. The whole of it is secondary to the dispositions of the complexions, and the dispositions of the virtues.
On the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that a fracture of the bones of the hand and the joints of the fingers rarely occurs. And it does not happen to them unless from repeated trauma.
When therefore a fracture of the sole or a trauma occurs, then it is necessary that the weak one sits on a seat: and before him is a seat according to equality. Then place his hand on it extended.
Then the servant extends the fractured bones, and the physician adjusts them, until they join with a good union.
Then it is necessary that he put on a plaster and a dressing, if not a hot abscess occurs. Then place a splint over it according to the quantity of the place: and you have already wrapped it in a linen cloth.
If the fracture is near the lower part of the heel of the foot, then make a sphere-like shape out of cloth: and order the weak person to press it with his foot that has the fracture: then it is tied with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather may be applied to the body of the foot: and tie it, according to what is necessary.
If the fracture is on the outside, then it is necessary that you put a splint over it, and another splint under it on the foot: and let the hand be open and straight. Then put a bandage, as the hand turns: and extend between the fingers with a binding.
Which if a fracture occurs to any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, adjust according to what is necessary. Then tie with the foot: and if you love him, put on a straight small splint, so that the fracture may be elevated, and not moved.
If the fracture is of the remaining fingers, such as the middle, and index, or ring, and little finger, then it should be straightened, and bandaged with the healthy finger that follows it: or all should be bandaged according to continuity: and it is better. Or put a small star on top of it, as we said for the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the appliance of a warm poultice: and apply with it what is necessary, when anything happens from it, according to which its treatment is repeated.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the hip.
The neck of the hip is frequently fractured, and it is revealed by sensation: since it is converted to the inner and back part.
And its restoration is done in this way, that a bandage is tied above the fracture, and another bandage below the fracture. And a plaster is applied over its surface.
Then every bandage is extended to its own part according to equality: and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If the fracture is near the root of the hip, then let the bandage be to the part of the chest, so that the extension falls upwards: and another bandage below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then let the bandage be near the knee, so that the extension is to the lower parts.
Then the physician adjusts the bone with both his hands, until he reduces it according to the likeness of its natural shape: and the bone is joined with a good joining.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, if an abscess does not occur on the limb. If an abscess occurs in it: then leave it for some days, until the warm abscess subsides, then return to your cure.
The narrowing of it should be thus. It is necessary that you bind a hard bandage over the fracture, that is, a binding: and fold it twice or thrice: and let some of it remain as an excess. Then bind the leg, until the heel reaches near the root of the buttocks.
And insert a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee downwards, and let the ends of the thread appear from the upper part of the two parts. Then wrap over the leg and hip what remains in excess of the bandage.
Then put over the hip, in the place of its fracture, a splint, and put one of them as a splint over the bone of the leg.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and hip with soft rags, so that it equals the narrowing.
Then begin with a strong bandage from the middle over the place of the fracture, with three or four windings. And the more you extend it with a bandage from the place of the fracture, let your narrowing be less and softer, and looser.
Then tie the two ends of the thread that you inserted between the hip and the leg. Bind therefore with three splints which are above, to both. Then bring the two ends of the thread downwards, until you reach the heel of the foot. And tie with them also the ends of the splints on the other side, so that the bandage is not moved from its place.
Then leave the bandage on for as long as it stays, so that there is no corrosion of the member, nor an abscess, nor inflammation, and similar things. If anything of that should happen. Apply therefore its remedy, and correct the whole thing that happened from it, according to what we have said many times.
If there is a fragment of bone in the wound which pierces from the bone: then it is necessary that you reduce it equally, if it is possible for you. And if not, find above that and remove that, and treat with that which the aforementioned treatment has preceded, until it heals.
And when this fracture of the hip is tightened, besides that pieces of bone are added to it, as we said regarding the help and arm.
Truly however in the restoration of one fractured thus, does not occur in the weak a lameness. And if it is restored alone, besides that another leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that the one whose it is, is always lame.
And know that the hip is tightened in fifty days, or a little is added to it, or a little is reduced from it: all that is according to the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
On the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap of the knee.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap rarely occurs, but an injury to it happens many times.
If indeed a fracture occurs to it, then it is not unless either a split or a hole in its parts. And it is with a wound, and without a wound. And let it be that the whole is treated with sensation.
And the restoration of it is such that what has been disrupted from its parts with the fingers, until it aggregates and is joined according to what is possible from equality and ease and fabrication.
Then put a plaster made from flour of sesamus, or of milled wheat, with the white of egg: and put above it a round piece of bone, if you desire it: and tighten over it a bandage suited to that.
Then consider all the dispositions that we have narrated concerning other fractures: such as a hot application, and similar to that: so that you oppose every accident with what is suitable for it, until it heals.
On the restoration of a broken skull.
You should know that the skull is made up of two bones, one of which is thick and is called by the name skull, and the other is subtle and is called foecile.
And it happens to both types of skull fractures what happens to the two bones of the arm, and because of that, its restoration is made, just like the restoration of the arm, equally, and the operation is one.
What if the two bones are broken together, the skull will be converted to all parts.
And if the subtle bone is broken, the skull converts to the front part.
And if the thick bone is broken, you find it below, and it is one of those that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and alignment, and binding of the stars according to that equally.
Truly it is necessary, if the fracture of the skull is a foul fracture, having many sores: that there should be a lesser extension, and a gentler alleviation of yours.
And in the skull there is something from the operation above the arm: and it is, when you equal the stars, and complete your entire operation, then take two splints of pine wood, which are used in the making of wooden sandals, that are placed under the tables: and they are made from palm spatulas: and choose from them those which have a small thickness, and not from the tiny ones: and let their length be according to the length of the skull from the knee down to below.
Then wrap over each one a cloth with two wraps according to its length. And put one part of the skull on one side, and the other on the other side: and let them be from the knee down to the bottom of the foot.
Then tie two splints in three places with two extremities and the middle. For when the tightening is prohibited, the leg declines to the right and left, and it stands in a good position.
And now let a little bed be administered from wood according to the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed in it, so that it conserves itself from motion.
And it is very necessary, that you should do that in the fracture when there is a wound with it of its own.
Then consider the leg every day, and study it with utmost care for signs of an abscess, or inflammation, or other remaining things. If therefore anything happens from those, then apply to it what is necessary, until it is healed, if God wills.
Of the fracture of the bones of the foot and toes.
The heel indeed does not have a fracture within.
A fracture happens to the bones of the foot and to the toes also a little, and it does not happen to them except by bruising in many dispositions.
What if a fracture happens to the bones of the foot, and you see those bones raised above each other, then let the weak foot be placed on the ground prepared, as when walking.
Then stand and place your foot on what is elevated from those bones: afterwards press, or tread upon it: and make it equal, until it returns to its places.
Then put a plaster, and pack above: and place under the sole of the foot a small board, which has two flat ends.
Then bind it with the foot a suitable tightness, after you have wrapped it with cloths, and other things which it needs.
When therefore three days, or four, have passed for it, then loosen the binding. For you will find the bones equal, whether they are fractured, or dislocated. The operation therefore is in them, as you have seen.
If however some fingers are broken, then restore them, and make them equal according to what I have told you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then put a splint on your finger according to the length of the finger, and let it be a little wider than it: then place under the foot this board which I have described to you, and bind it with a suitable binding.
If indeed what is broken from the bones is two, or three, or more, then put on top of each finger a splint of its own size, wrapped in linen cloth: and bind the board to the underside of the foot: and let it stand on one end of the board outside the belly of the foot, so that it holds with good retention.
And it is fitting for you, that you do not forget, but consider all things which I have told you regarding the remainder of the fractures from accidents which we have spoken of: and oppose each accident with what is necessary.
Of the fracture of a woman's vulva, and the os pubis, and the virga of a man.
When the vulva of a woman is fractured, then make her sit squarely. Then lean her backwards a little to that side, and let it be held back.
Then the midwife should fill the vulva of hers with a cake, until it fills it: and let it become in the vulva from that like a sphere.
Then let the woman be shaken, and her back be lifted up little by little. For that cake will come out, until it forms before the opening of the vulva like a sphere, and the fractured bone returns.
Then put a pillow on her back. When she then wants to urinate, remove the cake with ease, then let her urinate: and reduce it itself according to the manner in which you first introduced it: then let her return to her first pillow.
Let it be done for seven days, or thereabout, for the mouth itself is restored.
And if you need, take a small reed tube and tie over the opening of it a thread of papyrus, and insert the whole tube into the vulva. Then blow into the tube with force, until the tube is inflated inside the vulva, for the fracture returns.
Then let it be filled with a gauze, and let it stand for some days, according as we have said, until it is healed.
And when the mouth of the pubic bone of a man or woman is broken, then administer for its restoration and its adjustment that which we have described for the bone of the ankle.
And let not the rectum be hidden in these nearby fractures, which little touch, especially to him who has something of a stud or a peg.
And understand my book this much, that the fracture itself shows you in many treatments the right way for its restoration and its adjustment: understand therefore.
When the rod of a man is broken, take the neck of a goose, and insert the rod into it. Then put over it a dressing made of cloth, and let it be wrapped, and let it be left for three days, or thereabout, and it will heal, if God wills.
On the restoration of bone fractures, when there is a wound.
It is fitting for him to whom a fracture with a wound happens, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone, or the like of it, that he should be bled at once: if the conditions of bleeding, as we have said before, testify to you.
For if the wound flows with blood, then it is necessary, that you should proceed to its cutting, so that you powder over it the powdered zeolite: if you have not present another thing.
Then begin to restore the fracture on the same day: and do not postpone it, unless an abscess forms.
If a hot abscess forms, then abandon the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause bad accidents to happen to it.
If the fractured bone is protruding above the skin, then it is necessary that you should gently reduce it and adjust it with your hands with ease and slight extension.
If the extension of it and its adjustment with your hands is not prepared, then reduce it with this instrument. And the instrument is made of iron, whose length is seven or eight finger widths, and its width is according to the size of the wound. And because of that it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them according to the quantity he needs for the cure in every type of fracture.
And let it be round, and let there be a small thickness in it, so that it does not double at the time of compression during the operation: and let it have a sharp end, which has a edge on its ends. And let its upper part be thicker from the middle of it to the lower parts.
And this is its shape.
It is necessary in this operation that the pointed curved end of it be placed over the end of the protruding bone: and push it with it once, until the bone returns and is adjusted by some adjustment.
Therefore adjust the ends of the fracture to each other.
If the fractured end is delicate and does not receive the instrument with a good grip, then it is necessary to cut the end of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
If you cannot reduce the bone with what we have narrated altogether, then cut it with what is suitable for it from the incisions that we have mentioned: or saw it with one of the saws in any way possible for you.
Then rub away what remains in the bone from roughness and small fragments.
When therefore the bone is reduced, and the weak person finds after its reduction a vehement painful wound, then know that the bone did not return to its natural place.
Do it therefore. For you confer on the weak person a great benefit, when your restoration which you do for the bone is completed.
Then dip a cloth in black astringent wine. And especially if it is in summer. And do not put on the wound a plaster, nor anything in which is oil, so that it does not cause to happen in it putrefaction and corruption.
Then administer the stars at the hour of completion of your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound uncovered: so that you cut with a fork a hole in the bandages according to the quantity of the wound.
And beware with all caution, lest you constrict the wound with a fracture. For many foolish physicians have done this: and have caused to happen to their weak patients either death, or corruption, or ichor, that is, sanies. And let your dressing be soft, gentle, different from the dressings of fractures and other things.
For if the wound is bad, or is a great wound, and you find on it certain evil accidents which we have narrated, and you find pain in the place suspended: then it is not suitable that you put on it a plaster. And make for them bandages from hard cloths in the place of plasters, and constrict it with them.
When it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus: then remove from above the dressing that you had placed on it with wine. Then apply bandages and ointments with which we are accustomed to treat wounds, such as the ointment tetrapharmacon and similar ones.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the dressing. And examine the wound every day morning and evening, until it becomes consolidated and healed.
And it is necessary that the member be prepared in such a way, that the pus runs out from it downwards with ease.
If many days pass for the wound and it does not consolidate, nor is the pus separated from it: then know that there are small splinters of bone there. Wherefore it is necessary to probe the wound with a probe. Whatever of those small splinters are separated, remove them, and extract them. And those of them that are not separated, and prick the member, and cause pain, try to cut around them, and remove them in every way possible for you.
If there happens to the wound zuchen or ekileten, that is, estiomenus, or another species of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary that you oppose each one of them with what is suitable for it from the cure, whose description preceded in its own chapter.
And in that which it is necessary for my speech to stand, and to which you should direct your attention when a bone fractures, and protrudes above the member, such as the hip bone, and the shoulder, and similar ones of the large members, it is that you should not attempt to pull it back, for many times death occurs from this, instead leave it alone until it becomes putrid.
For it will surely fall out by itself after twenty days, or thirty.
Then take care of the wound, if it seems to you, and if not, leave it alone.
On the cure of swelling in the case of a fractured limb.
This swelling often occurs in the healing of a fracture of the limb. Especially one that is near the joints, and is disfigured from the shape of the member. And perhaps it prevents the limb from performing its natural function.
Consider then, if the swelling is recent, administer to it medicines which have a contracting property, such as aloe, and frankincense, and myrrh, and gum, and acacia, and similar things.
Thus, that you take some of these or all of them, and mix them with a drying wine, or with the whites of eggs, or with vinegar: and apply them to the swelling in a poultice, and bind them tightly over it.
And leave the binding on, nor loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another, until the swelling is removed.
Or bind over it a sheet of lead from Decenter. For lead has the property of removing all that is inflamed from the limbs.
But if the swelling has already turned to stone, and is constricted, and necessity urgently demands its removal: then cut on its upper part, and incise the prominent excess: or scrape it with certain scrapers, until it is removed: and take care of it, until it is healed.
On the cure when it is restored, and the limb remains after that subtle, not according to its own nature.
When a fracture of the bone is restored, and the limb remains after that subtle and weak, then this does not happen except for many reasons. One of them is the multitude of the solution of a bandage and its ligation not according to what is opportune: either because of the superfluity of the strictness of the bandages, until the nourishment is prohibited from running to the member: or because of the multitude of superfluous embrocation at not its hour: or because of the paucity of blood in the body of the infirm, and its debility.
And the cure of it is to feed the infirm, and to make tender its body, until the blood is multiplied in it. And administer a bath. And bring joy and gladness upon him.
Then put pitch on the member, so that it may attract much nourishment to itself. And repeatedly moisten its bandage with warm water, until the nourishment runs and it returns to its natural figure.
Of the cure of fractured bones when they are restored to their twisted state.
When a member already restored after its healing becomes twisted and a bone that is fractured or knotted is prominent, and the form of the member is disfigured by it, nevertheless the member is not prohibited from its natural operation: then it is not necessary to heed the advice of him who thinks that the member should be broken again.
And there have already been many foolish doctors and restorers, who have done this in our land.
And this operation is indeed highly culpable, leading to death, or great fear, so that it would be mortal.
But if the lameness and knot are recent: then it is necessary that a bandage be applied with warm water, in which there have been decocted softening herbs, such as leaves of marshmallow, and its root, and melilot, and their like. And let there be made for him a plaster with a softening plaster, such as diachylon prepared from the decoction.
Let a plaster of the root of marshmallow be applied, and let it be rubbed with hen's fat or with sesame oil: and let it be applied as a poultice.
Let the fat figs be pounded, and let them be ground with dove's dung, and let these and similar things which are named from medicines that lessen the consolidation.
And when a knot is loosened with continuous, gentle, and mild friction, which is done with moist hands. And let the movement of the limb be administered to every part at all hours.
If however the deformity has already preceded, and has become strong, and is turned into a callus, and necessity has been provoked for its treatment with iron: then it is necessary that the part above it be cut, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and that which exceeds in knot or bone be cut out with fine incisions: and let facilitation be administered to it with solicitude and study.
On speech in the case of Dislocation.
Dislocation is the exiting of a joint from its place. For which it is prohibited from its motion, and the figure of the limb is deformed. And it causes pains to happen on the weak part, and vehement injuries.
When therefore a dislocation happens to someone, then it is necessary to hasten to its restoration: and let it not be delayed at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes calloused, and perhaps with that the reduction of the dislocation is hindered.
On account of this therefore it is not necessary that it be moved, nor extended at the time of its callous formation. For it often causes spasms to happen, and painful pains.
But when that happens, then it is necessary that you proceed to the bleeding of the weak part. Then let it be applied, until the aposteme quiets.
Then let the limb be anointed with warm water and oil. Then let it be reduced with ease.
And the whole limb should be treated with that which is recalled to its proper place.
I have already discussed the chapters on dislocation and also according to what preceded in fracture, from the upper part of the body down to its lower.
Of the cure of dislocation of the lower jaw.
Both jaws are rarely dislocated except slightly.
And the dislocation of both is done according to one of two methods. Or they are moved from their places with slight movement, and made soft for a while. Or they are dislocated with complete dislocation, until they become soft to the part of the chest, until the saliva of the weak one runs, and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is involved with speech.
When however the dislocation of it is slight, then it returns in many cases by itself with swiftness.
If indeed the dislocation is complete, entire: then it is necessary that the reduction of it be administered quickly, and not delayed at all: and it is, that the assistant holds the head of the weak one: and the physician inserts the thumb of one of his hands into the root of the dislocated jaw between his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he inserts both his thumbs together, if the dislocation is from both sides. And the rest of the fingers of his hands are outside, with which he pulls.
And command the weak one, that he cleans his jaws, and frees them for chewing on every side. And the physician pulls the jaw, and thrusts the jaw, until it returns to its place.
What if the reduction of it is difficult, especially if both jaws are affected at the same time: then administer vapor with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy: nor should you delay the reduction of both entirely, as we have said.
When the jaws return and are equal, and the weak mouth is closed, and not softened: then put over them cotton pads, with cerate made from wax: and rose oil.
Then it should be bound with ease with soft ligaments.
And the weak person should sleep on their back: and their head should be steady between two pillows, so that they do not move it to the right or left: nor should they be forced, or compelled to chew anything: instead soft food should be put in their mouth, until when the pains have subsided, and the jaw is knotted: then they should eat what they want, and it should be administered with ease.
He should not exert himself over the operation of his mouth during eating and drinking, and speaking, until the jaw is knotted and healed.
What if the reduction of the two jaws is difficult, when they become dislocated at some time, and do not return to their own place: then often fevers and constant thirst occur from it: and perhaps the belly of the weak person is loosened, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
When you see that, then know that it is a loss, and often he dies to whom it happens in ten days.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the elbow, and the extremity of the shoulder.
The elbow indeed is not dislocated from the inner side, because of its continuity with the chest. And it is dislocated whenever to the outer sides: and that is shown by sensation.
And its restoration is, for the weak person to lie on their back, and to extend their arms. Then grasp the constricted place with your palm, for the constriction itself will relax.
Then put a plaster on it, and poultices, and bind it tightly.
The limbs which follow the elbow and are connected with it are not dislocated except rarely. What is dislocated on that day should be reduced and adjusted according to what we have said, and what has been prepared for you.
Then put a plaster on it, and poultices, and a bandage. And instruct the weak person to adhere to stillness and quiet until he is healed.
And with this same treatment the arm is reduced when it is separated from its place.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the shoulder.
Know that the shoulder is not dislocated except in three ways.
Of which one is, that it is dislocated towards the lower part of the armpit. The second is, that it is dislocated towards the breast. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the shoulder, and that happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated to the back, on account of the blade. And it is not dislocated to the front, on account of the nerve.
And most often it is dislocated and protrudes downwards towards the armpit, especially in those whose flesh is sparse. For it protrudes quickly in them and goes in quickly.
In those, however, whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite, namely that it protrudes with difficulty and goes in with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some men a blow or a fall, and the shoulder becomes swollen with a hot swelling, and it is thought because of that, that it is already dislocated.
It is necessary, therefore, that you should test that thing, until you are certain of it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And a dislocation is recognized when it turns to the lower parts of the shoulder joint, and you distinguish between a dislocated shoulder and a healthy one. For you find between them a clear difference, and you find in the head of the shoulder a concavity, and below the joint upon touching the head of the shoulder as if it were an egg: and the weak one cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if the shoulder is dislocated downwards or upwards, then you find a palpable feeling, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this kind of dislocation is easy, when it is recent, or the weak person is a child.
And its reduction is, that the assistant lifts his hand to the upper parts. Then put your thumbs under the joint and lift the joint with force upwards to its place: and the assistant lifts his hand and extends it upwards. Then he puts it downwards, for it returns quickly to its place.
If however it does not return by what we have said, and there are many days since the dislocation occurred: then it is necessary that the weak person should bathe in warm water, and an embrocation which softens and soothes should be administered, such as if the roots of marshmallow and fenugreek and melilot are decocted in water, and administered.
Then the weak person should rest on his back: and a sphere and wool which is equal between softness and tightness should be put under the joint.
Then the physician should place his heel on the sphere, and lift the head of the shoulder with strength, and pull the weak person's hand downwards.
And if you do, draw him back according to this method: and it is, that you make the strong man be present who is longer than the weak one, and stand at the side, and insert his shoulder under the armpit of the weak one: and lift up his armpit to the upper parts, until the weak one is suspended in the air. And another servant should pull the hand of the weak one to the lower parts of his belly. If the weak one is lightweight: then it is necessary, that something else be suspended with him, so that it weighs him down: for dislocation returns immediately.
And it is drawn back also according to another method: and it is, that a long wooden pole be fixed in the ground, whose head is of a round figure: such as a mason's mallet: which is neither thick nor subtle. Then let it be placed under the weak one after they have put soft cloths on top of the head of the wood: and let the weak one stand according to the length of the wood.
Then let his hand be stretched out to the lower part from the other side, and let his body be stretched out from the other side with force: for the joint returns to its place with speed.
What if the reduction of it is difficult through all that we have said, then use this cure: and it is, that you take a wooden pole whose length is the quantity of two arms, and its width the quantity of four fingers, and its thickness the quantity of two fingers: which has a round head, so that its entrance is alleviated into the depth of the armpit, according to this form.
Then soft cloths should be tied over the round head, so that the wood does not harm the weak one.
Then let it be placed under the armpit of the weak one, so that the hand or arm is stretched out over the wood up to the lower parts: and let the wood be tied over the aid, and arm, and extremity of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on the step of the ladder in width, and the hand is extended to the lower parts, and the rest of the body is let down suspended from the other side. For the joint will enter immediately.
When therefore the entrance is completed according to whatever mode is possible: then it is necessary, that you place under the eyelid a sphere of equal quantity made from wool. Then put the prepared plaster made from the powder of millet with benzoin and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it turns out above.
Then tie under the eyelid a proper bandage: and turn the ligature over the plaster above: and attach his hand to his neck, and let it be, nor move his hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that food of little nourishment be given, until the member is strengthened: since it is quicker for its healing.
Then a larger one is put on after seven days, or five, and it is exercised with movement. If therefore it becomes firm and is not softened, then it is already healed.
If indeed the joint is dislocated many times, on account of the humidity which happened to it, or on account of another cause: then it is necessary that a cautery with three openings be administered to it, according to what was previously mentioned in the chapter about cautery.
What if all this is done, and the ligature is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not confirmed, and the plaster and bandage are put on it many times, and it does not become firm, and falls, and cannot be lifted up to the upper parts: then know that the nerves which are at the head of the shoulder have already been cut, or stretched, or softened: and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
And dislocation which occurs towards the chest and breast, or a reduction to the back, is done with an impulse and extension with the hands: until it returns, and the rest of the bandage and care are administered to it, until it is healed.
What if after healing a heaviness occurs in the limb and slowness in its movement then let the weak one use a bath many times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its first nature.
On the cure of dislocation of the elbow.
You should know that the joint of the elbow is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty also: and it dislocates to all parts, and especially to the front or to the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: since it falls under sight and under touch, according to whichever shape it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, it is shown to you clearly. For the joint is shortened, and it cannot bend the arm, nor touch with it its own shoulder.
And it is necessary that you hurry, and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot aposteme occurs to it.
For if a hot aposteme occurs to it, its reduction becomes difficult: and perhaps it is not healed at all, especially when it is to the back. For it is worse than all kinds of dislocation, and a more vehement pain occurs to them: and many times death descends with it.
And its restoration when it is from those which are possible to reduce, is that the hand of it is extended with two hands: and its arm is stretched out, and the hands of the doctor are above the elbow and below it, and he himself pushes the joint with his thumbs or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or indeed the dislocation is to the front: then it returns thus, that he bends his hand with his own hand, until he strikes with the root of his palm the dislocated elbow.
What if not preceded dislocation to reduction, then administer violent extension, and it is that two assistants stretch the arm, and two assistants hold it also, so that it is not moved during extension.
Then the arm is turned to every part after which a double layer of cloth or a broad bandage is applied over the hand.
And when the physician touches with his hand the joint, he should anoint it with oil: so that it is one of those things which help with the slipperiness of the joint with ease. Then he pushes the joint with a vigorous push until it slips back.
And after its reduction, it is necessary that a plaster be placed on it, in which there is astringency and excitation with the whites of eggs: and it should be bound with a decent bandage. And the arm should be suspended from the neck of the invalid, and left for some days.
Then it should be loosened. If then the joint is stabilized in its place, then undo the bandage from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint not being well bound, then apply another plaster, and bandage, and leave it for some days, until it is bound. Then undo it.
If however heaviness occurs to it after the reduction of the joint, and the arrangement is in motion: then administer a moistening in a bath, and subtle rubbing, and compression, until it is soothed, or put on the joint a plaster made of fat of the spleen. Then bind it, and leave it on for a day and a night. Then remove it, and introduce it into a bath. When it sweats, then rub the joint with moderate rubbing. Then repeat over it a mixture of vinegar one, and second, and third time with the bath, until it is soothed.
And if you use it, put on top of it a poultice of dung, warm and heated with butter.
Do that several times. It is soothed by itself, and returns to its own condition.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the elbow.
The elbow of the hand is often dislocated, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. Unlike the rest of the joints. However, it is necessary that there be speed in the reduction of its dislocation at the hour in which it becomes dislocated, before the place becomes inflamed: or a hot abscess occurs in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation in it is, that you put the elbow of the hand on a table, and an assistant extends the hand of it: and the physician puts his palm on the prominence of the joint, and presses it, until it returns.
However, it is necessary that it be considered, if the palm has already receded to the front of the hand: then let the back of the hand be put on a table at the extension and reduction: and if the dislocation proceeds to the back of the hand, then let the position of the hand be turned inward on a table, that is, the inner part of the hand be put on a table, so that the hand of the physician falls on the prominence of the joint.
If it returns immediately, it is good: and if not, then bind it with a poultice sitting on the abscess: and leave it, nor repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you above the reduction of it, after the days have passed, however the joint remains according to its twistedness: and nothing harms the patient, except that the hand becomes soft, and cannot grasp anything: then know that the nerves are cut or injured.
There is not therefore ingenuity in it, except that it is tightened with a cautery near its edge. And perhaps it does not press upon anything.
When therefore Almahassem does not return, then put on top of it the plaster which we have described. Then let it be tightened and left for five days: afterwards let it be loosed and left, and the hand is exercised.
If however the motion of it is hindered, and some heaviness occurs in it: then let it be soothed with warm water, and rubbed several times, until it is soothed.
On the cure of dislocations of the fingers.
When a finger is dislocated in any direction, or a finger to the back of the hand, or its joint: then extend the finger, and push back the dislocation with your thumbs, until it returns.
Then hold the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then hang it, and extend it, until it becomes straight on its own on that day.
When therefore it has been so, bind it according to the same method: and do not cease to loosen it during the day: and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Do this for several days, until it becomes tight.
And do similarly with it, if it is dislocated towards the joint of the hand: and bind it to the same part, and perform your first operation on it, until it is healed.
And do likewise with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
On the cure of dislocations of the vertebrae of the back.
When there occurs to someone a complete dislocation of the vertebrae of the back, or of the neck, or many vertebrae are removed from their places: then there is no cure for it, because death quickly follows the infirm.
And the sign of it is that the weak bowel comes out without control, and it cannot hold it, and often some of its members become softened because of it, or two of its legs, or two of its arms, or one of them: or one vertebra is removed from its place.
For often a removal is made, because often a removal is made to the four parts.
That which is removed towards the back is called Gibbositas.
The treatment of it is that you should consider then, if the Gibbosity occurs from childhood: then there is no treatment for it, nor is there any healing from it.
In that which occurs from a fall, or a blow, or similar things to those, the ancients have already spoken of the methods of treatment in prolix discourse. To whom it does not return with profit.
I have already abbreviated from it, whose fault excuses little because of its abundance in my proximity to the intention: and my exposition which I expounded it, and formed an instrument for it in a different way than that which they showed it, and exposed it.
I say therefore, that in the humpbackedness which occurs from the front part, there is no ingenious remedy, nor healing from it.
And likewise in that which happens to both sides also.
And it is not treated from them, except that which happens on the back properly, with what I relate: and it is, so that the weak one is extended on his face on a level platform near the wall: and under it a humid cloth is spread, so that his chest is not harmed.
Then a straight stick is fixed in a hole in the ground, at the end of the platform, near his head: and another stick near his feet at the other end of the platform: and an assistant holds the stick, and it is not fixed in the hole. One servant should hold the other.
Then tie above the head of the weak person, and under his armpits a soft ligature, firm, and extend the end of the ligature to the wood which is near his head, and tie it on that.
Then bind another ligature above his ankles, and above his knees, and near his calves. Then gather all the ligatures together, and tie them on another wood which is near his feet.
Then each servant should pull the ligature to the part of the wood, and the two woods should not be moved from their places where they are fixed: nevertheless they should not be tightened, as we have said.
And the physician should put his palms on the vertebrae with force, until it returns, or he should put a board on it. Then he should attach his feet to the board, until it returns.
But if it does not return through this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three cubits. And bore in the wall which we mentioned that is near the weak person, a place into which the end of the board will enter. Then put the middle of the board on the vertebrae. And the physician should put his feet on the other end. And he should pull strongly, until the vertebra is constricted and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with a lathe that is turned by your hand: and it is, as if you fix in the ground near the head of the weak person at the end of the bed two woods, whose length is one cubit: and near his head at the end of the bed two woods.
And let the length which is between each wood be one palm: and now a hole has been made in every place where the lathe runs: and each wood should be fixed firmly in the ground, so that it does not move at all.
And a round stick is inserted: and it is turned in which the ligature revolves in the two holes of the two sticks.
And at the end of it is a hole, in which the stick is fixed, whose length is a palm with what revolves: and in two other sticks there is similarly one of them.
Then tighten the ligatures that you have tightened on the chest with the turnstile that is at its head: and the ligatures that you have tightened on its shins with the turnstile that is at its feet.
Then let a servant stand at each turnstile, who twists his hand with the cloth, with which the turnstile revolves: and the physician adjusts the Spondile, according to what we have said.
This is the form of the turnstile, which follows.
Then after the Spondile has returned, and the place is adjusted, then it is necessary that you put on an exciting plaster with white of egg. Then a poultice. Afterwards put on top of the plaster a bar of wood, whose width is three fingers, or about that. And its length such a quantity as to cover the place of the gibbosity, and over the sound part of the Spondile.
Then let it be tied with the ligature that we have said. And let the weak person use subtle nourishment, until he is healed.
Which if something of the swelling remains in the place at the end of the healing: then it is necessary that medicines which soften and soothe be administered, with the administration of the bar that we have described for a long time. And now let it be applied on that plate of lead.
And when a swelling occurs on the posterior Spondile of the back: and it is thought that it is due to a dislocation, and it is that bone which now protrudes. It is not suitable that a presumption be made for him with this medicine: perhaps it will cause death.
On the cure of a dislocated ankle.
You should know that the joints of the ankle and shoulder do not suffer anything except dislocation, nor does what happens to other joints due to slight movement and concavity happen to them.
And the joint of the ankle is dislocated in four ways. That is, because it dislocates to the front and to the back.
And most often it dislocates inward, and little dislocation occurs to the front and to the back.
And the treatment of its dislocation inward is: that when you join the weak leg to the healthy one, it is longer: and its knee is more prominent than the healthy one, and the weak one cannot fold its foot at the groin: and the place which follows the groin is affected with a warm poultice, because the head of the hip has already reached there.
And the sign of one to whom a dislocation to the outside occurs is that its accidents are contrary to these accidents.
And the sign of one to whom a dislocation to the front occurs is that it stretches out the healthy leg in extension: however it does not bend it without pain which is in the knee: and if it tries to walk, it cannot step forward: and its urine is retained, and its groin is affected, and upon walking it steps on its heel.
And the sign of one to whom a dislocation to the back occurs is that it does not stretch the knee, nor can it bend before the groin is bent: and its leg is also shorter than the other: and the groin is softened, and the head of the hip is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction of this dislocation of species is that you should consider then if the dislocation is old, which has already become established with the one who has it. Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains according to its own disposition: then there is not in it a cure at all. Not therefore ought a complete presumption be made for it.
In that case in which the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four species of dislocations: then proper for this, that you bind the joint, and extend it to the interior and to the exterior, and move it right and left: perhaps indeed it returns, and does not need another cure.
If however it does not return in this manner, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong minister, and let him extend his leg from the lower part: either with his hands, or with a ligature, which will be tied above his leg above the knee: and another minister extends his hand from above, so that he inserts his hands under his armpits. Then let him tie with a gentle ligature over the root of the hip, and hold the end of the other ligature.
And let its extension be either from the front part of the groin to the region of the armpit, or from the back to the region of the back.
And let the extension be of all of them in place of one, until the weak one is lifted with his body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this indeed is a common species of extension among the four species.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary for every species that which I will say about its own cure.
In the reduction of it, indeed, when the dislocation is to the interior, it is necessary that the weak one lies on his healthy side. Then let a ligature be placed over the root of the hip, in that which is between the head of the hip and the place that is under the groin. Then extend the ligature upwards from the part of the groin to the higher part of the body, to the region of the armpit.
Then the assistant should take his strong arms and cut the thick place of the weak hip, and stretch it to the outer stretching of the vein. For it returns to its own place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment, by which this member is reduced.
If it does not obey you for entry with this type of treatment at all, then it is necessary that the two weak feet be bound together with a strong and gentle binding over the two heels and over the two knees: and let the length of each be from its fellow the quantity of four fingers: and let the weak leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then the weak one should be hung over the head of the wooden beam, which is in the house: and its length from the ground should be the quantity of two arms.
Then command a strong youth to support the head of the hip: and let another youth be hung with the weak one: and let another youth push, supporting the hip with strength, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
In the reduction of it itself, however, when there is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the weak one should lie on a platform, according to what we have described for one having a hump. And let a bandage be tied over its weak leg itself, and over its chest.
Then two wooden beams should be placed, one at its feet and the other at its head. Then an additional wooden beam should be placed in the middle of the platform, fixed well: on which let wet cloths be wrapped, so that it does not harm the weak one: and let the beam be between its hips, so that it is not drawn to the lower parts at the extension.
Then every assistant should stretch to his own part, and the physician should accomplish the reduction with his hand.
If it is cut to restore it, it will be good.
And if not, put on it a plate, and press on it, as we have said for hunchbackedness, equally. It is fitting that the weak one should lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is fitting that the weak leg should be extended quickly: and this is a second narration on the couch: and the physician puts the palm of his right hand on the weak groin. Then he tightens it with his other hand, and with that tightening he puts it extended downwards towards the knee joint.
And if the dislocation is to the back, then it is not fitting that the weak one should be extended downwards, and it should be elevated above the ground: but rather it is fitting that it should be placed on a hard surface, as it is also fitting if his hip dislocates outwardly, according to what we said about its lying on two hips: and it is on its face, and the ligaments are extended: according to what we said just now.
And it is fitting that compression should be administered with a plate also on the place from which the joint protrudes, when the reduction is completed according to what is fitting.
And the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you: and it is that you should extend the two weak legs: and when you see them equal, and the weak one draws in his leg, and expands it without effort: then you should know that the limb has already returned according to what is fitting: then join the two hips, and put on a plaster, and bind with a bandage, that is, a wheel: so that the hip may not move to any part: and let the weak one adhere to quiet for three days, or four.
Then undo the bandage and plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in condition, then you should know that the dislocation has already been fixed: therefore release the weak one to walk.
And if you see something of softening: then reduce it, and apply a plaster to it, and bind it according to your first binding: and leave it for three days.
Then undo it and make it stay behind in walking on both for some days, until it is well comforted.
Of the Dislocation of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outward, inward, and downward, namely to the back.
And it is not dislocated forward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you will find the weak one applying his leg to his hip. If however it does not adhere to the hip: then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all modes of its dislocation is that you make the weak one sit upright, so that now he extends his leg if he can over it, and you adhere your back to his face: and put his foot between your feet.
Then make his knee adhere with your palms, and straighten them out between your fingers over his knee. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with strength. And another assistant extends his foot, until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the hip gently, not laboriously.
Then apply a plaster to it, and make the leg adhere to the hip. Then bind them together with a bandage for three days, or four. Then undo it.
And the weak one does not use walking for some days, until he is healed.
If however the reduction of it fails you, then administer a strong extension with bandages, whose description preceded in the cure of the ankle, until it returns.
On the treatment of dislocation of the heel bone.
The heel bone is sometimes moved with a slight movement and sometimes it is dislocated according to its complement. And its dislocation occurs, either towards the inside or towards the outside.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel bone swollen, protruding to the side towards which it is dislocated.
The treatment of its displacement is an easy reduction, and it is that it should be extended with ease by the hands, and straightened until it returns to place.
In the treatment of it when it is dislocated according to its complement, it is necessary that you make the weak person sit upright and a strong assistant should hold him firmly from behind on the middle of his back.
Then you should hold with your right hand his foot from the upper part of it, and with your left hand from the lower part of the foot at the place of the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards yourself with your right hand. Then with the left hand with two fingers.
Then stretch it with the left hand, and push the foot's heel upwards with the right hand towards the leg without strain.
Do that twice, as we have described.
Then push the heel of the foot towards the leg for the third time, and you pull the ankle.
If then it returns to its place at the first or second attempt according to this method, and you see the foot is straight, it is good: and if not, then repeat the operation on it, since it will return.
If however the reduction of it is prohibited to you with what we have described: then make the weak person lie on his back on the ground, and fix a strong stick in the earth firmly, so that it falls between his thighs: and you have already wrapped something around it, so that it does not harm the weak person.
Then the servant should take his thigh: afterwards the servant should stretch the other foot, either with his hands or with a bandage that binds above the ankle. Then the servant should stretch everything in the opposite direction of his comparison: and a small stick should stand between the two hips of the weak person holding it, so that his body is not pulled downwards at the lower part during stretching.
Then the physician adjusts the dislocation with his hands, and another servant holds the healthy leg below. For the dislocation returns with swiftness.
When then the dislocation returns, and the health of its return is declared to you: then put a plaster and a dressing, and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the foot with a bandage to the leg.
And it is necessary that you beware or avoid the nerves that are above the posterior heel, so that the bandage is not oppressive over them and harms them.
Then leave it for two days, or three. If the bandage becomes loose, bind it: then undo it on the third or fourth day: and forbid the weak person from walking for forty days. If indeed he tries to walk before that time, there will not be security unless the dislocation is dissolved over it, and becomes putrid, and it does not receive a cure after that.
What if a hot abscess occurs to him: then it is necessary that you administer to its treatment what we previously mentioned in another place concerning the cure, and an application of warm poultices, until it is removed.
Of the dislocation of the toes of the foot, and its cure.
It is necessary that what is dislocated among them should be adjusted with a little extension without labor: and this indeed is not difficult, rather it is easy.
If the dislocation is in certain nodes of the foot's back: then it is necessary that the weak make his foot sit on an equal place to the ground, or on a board: and he stands as one walking.
Then you stand, and put your foot on that which protrudes from those joints. Then tread on it with your foot with strength, until it returns, and you see it already equal, and it does not appear in the place of prominence.
Then put under the sole of his foot a board that can hold the whole foot, which has two ends. Then bind it with a binding for three days sufficiently. Then unbind it, and make it rest from walking for many days, until it is bound and secured from the reduction.
Of the species of dislocation with wound or fracture.
When something happens to it, and you consider its cure and restoration, many times death occurs: and because of that, it is not necessary that one presumes on the cure of such a thing, unless one who is prudent in the art from long usage, merciful, pious, slow, not daring, nor presumptuous: and so that he uses medicines which calm hot apostems only: and entrusts the weak to divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for health from destruction with gentleness of sickness, and in which appears something to be hoped. Therefore, consider the reduction of it immediately at the beginning of the thing, before a hot apostem happens.
If the member returns according to what you wanted, then administer a regime that calms an apostem: and cure the wound with what is suitable for it from dried plasters.
Which if the dislocation is with a fracture, and they happen in a separated bone, then consider their removal, and do on it what we said in simple sicknesses from that which preceded in their places.
And beware according to your ability, and guard yourself from the entrance on the way of fear: according to that which my preceding preaching has instructed you.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting to your glory, and more healthy for your body among them in your time.
